    ACCM-SME: A New Theoretical Framework for
    Adopting Cloud Computing Model for Small and
         Medium Enterprises in Saudi Arabia

Signature:




                                            I
                                     Abstract
Background: Cloud computing technology is widely adopted by small and medium
enterprises (SMEs) in developed countries. However, many SMEs in developing
countries, particularly Saudi Arabia, still rely on conventional technology like on-
premises servers. With the Saudi government's Vision 2030 initiative launched in 2016,
significant technological, cultural and economic transformations are anticipated by 2030.
Therefore, it is essential to investigate the factors impacting Saudi SMEs' adoption of
cloud computing services to leverage the benefits of Vision 2030 programmes.

Aims and Objectives: This thesis aims to investigate the intention of Saudi SMEs to
adopt cloud computing services from the perspectives of employees, IT managers and top
managers. The objectives of this study are to review the current literature to explore the
barriers and challenges that influence Saudi SMEs' adoption of cloud computing services,
identify the most influential factors that impact the adoption of cloud computing services
and validate an appropriate conceptual framework for understanding Saudi SMEs'
adoption of cloud computing services. Therefore, a new theoretical framework, Adoption
of the Cloud Computing Model by Saudi SMEs (ACCM-SME), was developed to
evaluate the potential influence of 17 factors from four different constructs: technological,
environmental, organisational and social contexts.

Methods: This thesis employs a mixed-method approach that combines quantitative
(questionnaires) and qualitative (semi-structured interviews) methods. The
questionnaires gathered data from 412 Saudi SME employees in Riyadh city and
conducted a statistical analysis. Before further analysis, the validity and reliability of the
instrument used were assessed using SPSS software. Then, a hypothesis analysis was
conducted using Structural Equation Modelling (SEM) through AMOS software. After
that, the next stage was conducting the semi-structured interviews and collecting data
from 15 IT managers and top managers and analysing them thematically. The results from
both stages were integrated to understand the factors affecting cloud computing adoption
and to provide recommendations for enhancing the adoption process.
Results: The findings revealed that while cloud computing adoption in Saudi Arabia is
still in its early stages, there is a growing trend toward broader adoption. The study
identified key factors influencing cloud adoption by Saudi SMEs using a new framework
(ACCM-SME) built on the TOE Framework and DOI theory. The ACCM-SME
incorporates technological, organisational, environmental and social (user behaviour)
factors. Quantitative and qualitative data analysis confirmed these factors' importance, as
12 out of 17 hypotheses have been accepted.
Conclusion: These insights highlight the factors influencing this process and can help
cloud providers enhance their services and strategies. Policymakers and business leaders
can also use these findings to formulate strategies that facilitate cloud adoption among
Saudi SMEs. In summary, this thesis significantly contributes to existing knowledge of
cloud computing adoption among Saudi SMEs.

                                                                    XVII
Chapter
1 Introduction
This research investigates the challenges that have been raised in terms of the necessity
of establishing a secure, reliable and safe association between organisations and cloud
computing services. Controversy regarding data security and safety has been voiced over
the past two decades since cloud computing services started being discussed in computing
literature reviews. Considering the remarkable growth of the use of cloud computing by
contemporary organisations, issues of confidentiality, privacy, data control, service cost,
lack of cloud knowledge and integrity among others have not been adequately
investigated, especially in Middle Eastern countries and Saudi Arabia in particular.
Certain challenges have been identified as of concern to small and medium enterprises
(SMEs) in Saudi Arabia given how they act as barriers to the adoption of cloud computing
services (Alsafi & Fan, 2020a). The continuing gap in this area of research can be
considerably minimised by focusing on the most significant issues that pose a challenge
to the adoption of Cloud computing services.

Investigating and determining the various challenges that exist in relation to the adoption
of cloud computing services, as well as which factors encourage it, will be the first step
in identifying which challenges pose a threat to organisations seeking to store their data.
This investigation will be carried out through a comprehensive framework which will be
designed for the Saudi SME context. This framework will be validated by an extensive
data gathering process across SMEs in Saudi Arabia where the current research will take
place.

Cloud computing helps organisations access data resources remotely through internet
services. This accessibility is provided and implemented through a series of processes,
equipment and techniques that can transform the managerial, financial and technical
practices and procedures into information that is normally stored in external servers. This
can be done through either public or private cloud services. The existing literature implies
that the possible challenges of cloud computing have been concentrated in developed


                                             1
countries, such as by the members of the Organization for Economic Cooperation and
Development (OECD) (Abubakar, 2016a). However, other countries still need to conduct
further investigations into how such services can pose significant threats to confidentiality
and data integrity. In Saudi Arabia, although recent years have witnessed a considerable
development in internet services including cloud computing services, dealing with
advanced security issues requires further investigation and understanding (Alassafi et al.,
2016a; Alghamdi et al., 2019a). The recent establishment of the Saudi Data and Artificial
Intelligence Authority (SDAIA) is considered to be a sign of interest and orientation
towards digital technologies in general, and cloud computing services in particular. The
existence of a government organisation that supports the migration to cloud computing
services, whether in the public or private sector, is significant.

Cloud computing has become more widespread and provides various technical services,
and resources. The shift from storing data in internal sources to external sources is central,
along with other factors such as the low cost, self-service nature and flexible network
access. All of these are what prompts the public and private sectors to switch to cloud
computing services (Pibul, 2018). From the point of view of this research, cost and self-
service are not the only factors affecting the SMEs’ adoption of cloud computing services
in Saudi Arabia. There are more important factors that will be covered in detail later on
in this chapter.

The current research aims to identify, investigate and discuss the most significant
challenges that face cloud computing within SMEs in Saudi Arabia. In order to achieve
this broad aim, this study’s introductory chapter will present the research problem,
questions, hypotheses, objectives, rationale and contribution to the existing knowledge.
The subsequent chapter will focus on the literature review of the relevant publications
and empirical studies. Another theoretical chapter will be provided with a focus on
establishing a distinctive theoretical framework of the critical challenges that face cloud
computing services, especially in Saudi Arabia. The methodology chapter will show the
research approaches, methods, instruments, philosophies and data collection stages. A
data collection and analysis chapter will be provided to show how the relevant data was
gathered in the empirical study, before presenting the research findings and discussing
them in a critical manner. The conclusion and future recommendations will then be
presented.



                                              2
    1.1 Research Background

There have been many misinterpretations of the term ‘cloud computing’ because in some
SMEs, it is only in its initial stages. There is no consensus on the definition of cloud
computing and how it can be used for various purposes. However, there are some
common characteristics that have been pointed out by researchers regarding the nature of
this term. A significant definition introduced by the US National Institute of Standards
and Technology (NIST) is that cloud computing is a method that enables convenient, on-
demand network access to a shared platform of configurable computing resources (for
example, networks, servers, storage, applications and services) that can be provided
independently or through collaboration with a service provider (AlSudiari & Vasista,
2012a). It has been also described as an essential stage in the evolution of on-demand
information technology services and products(Vouk, 2008). Cloud computing is
considered to be a significant leap for technical services. This is because the technical
resources are shared with a number of groups who wish to use cloud computing services
through the Internet.

Cloud computing provides a contemporary economic and technological pathway to shift
from the conventional ways of providing IT services to a more contemporary paradigm.
In Saudi Arabia, the fast-growing economy and the economic prosperity of the country
have both been investigated from different perspectives (Altaee et al., 2016; Belloumi &
Alshehry, 2018). The technological dimension is an essential element of economic
enhancement and it plays an indispensable role in transforming the organisations’
traditional work into technological pathways. This requires a contemporary and strong
technology infrastructure. The IT background and usage of cloud computing in Saudi
SMEs has been addressed by recent studies (Alrubaishi & Robson, 2019; Faridi & Malik,
2019; Mezghani & Almansour, 2019). However, confidentiality, privacy, data control,
the service cost, a lack of cloud knowledge and the integrity of the data have not been
adequately considered in the recent studies that have been conducted in the area of data
security (Alassafi et al., 2016a; AlHumaidan et al., 2018). In order to fill the knowledge
gap mentioned by the previous authors in their recommendations for further investigation,
an attempt will be made to identify all of the significant factors that influence cloud
computing adoption in Saudi SMEs. It is important that this list of influencing factors is
not considered definitive until thoroughly investigated.


                                            3
In this study, the research will focus on four main domains to represent the essence of the
Cloud computing challenges that will be investigated in this study; the technological
context, organisational context, environmental context and social context (see Figure 1.1).




   Figure 1-1: The main challenges of cloud computing investigated in the study

The four essential domains in this study reflect how the independent variables in the
technological, organisational, environmental and social contexts can be sensitive issues
for contemporary SME practices. They have an impact on the adoption of cloud
computing services by Saudi SMEs.

Firstly, there are challenges facing the adoption of cloud computing in Saudi SMEs in the
technological context. This includes many factors, the most important of which are as
follows:

- Confidentiality

The significance of investigating the confidentiality of cloud computing practices stems
from the potential risks that can occur in the processes involved (AlSudiari & Vasista,
2012a). The relevant concerns have been investigated regarding the possibility of external
parties’ involvement in providing cloud services and having control over the


                                            4
organisations’ sensitive information. This poses a significant risk to both privacy and
information confidentiality (Lorünser et al., 2015a; Rao, 2016a; Sahmim & Gharsellaoui,
2017a). In fact, most users of traditional systems, whether companies or individuals, have
very serious concerns about switching to cloud computing services regarding the
confidentiality of their information when it is shared with a third party that manages the
services.

- Integrity

Data integrity has been studied by various scholars in terms of the possible risks that
might occur in cloud servicing such as preserving the data, finding effective solutions to
these risks and establishing a sustainable secure work environment (Aldossary & Allen,
2016a; Gaetani et al., 2017a; Kumari & Paul, 2016a; Yu et al., 2016a). The integrity of
both the data and information is a crucial matter, and it should meet the global standards
that ensure that the users' data is not tampered with. A loss of integrity in cloud computing
services means that the services will no longer be considered viable.

- Availability

The concept of availability means that cloud computing services should be available from
anywhere and at any time through the Internet. The service should be ready upon request
directly without any delay.

- Security

Security is an important factor in any system or organisation wishing to protect
information from unauthorised access. A lack of security creates an insecure environment
for companies and raises concerns about the transition to cloud computing services.

-Cost

The concept of cost is fundamental and must be discussed because the cost should be
proportional to the services provided. SMEs are always interested in the actual cost of the
transformation process to cloud computing services. Thus, the actual cost depends on
several variables such as the resources that will be used, the period of use, the basic
specifications of the resources used and the geographical location of the resources used.



                                             5
A second set of challenges facing the adoption of cloud computing in Saudi SMEs are in
the organisational context. This includes many factors, the most important of which are
as follows:

- Regulation

This factor is at the forefront of the challenges facing Saudi SMEs when seeking to move
to cloud computing services, due to the current lack of support from the General Authority
if their data is hacked or destroyed. This means that there must be clear and publicised
standards that support the transformation process.

-Size

The size of the company is also an influencing factor and refers to the size of the
investments in that company and its market value, number of employees, the size of
annual returns and the number of branches. SMEs usually have a fear of switching to
cloud computing services. This is unlike large companies that have more ability and
flexibility in the transformation process to deal with any problems.

- IT Readiness

Technical readiness is a very important factor. It is measured based on the organisation's
infrastructure and the extent to which it will accept the transition to cloud computing
services. Companies that have a strong technical infrastructure are able to adopt cloud
computing services within the shortest time and at the lowest cost.

-Top Management Support

According to Alkhater et al. (2018), the top management always play an important role in
companies regardless of the size of the company. When the vision of the transformation
to cloud computing services is supported by the top management, it will have a big impact
in the transformation process. Consequently, any organisation needs the support of its top
management in order for them to make appropriate decisions at the right time and without
delay.




                                            6
Thirdly, there are the challenges facing the adoption of cloud computing in Saudi SMEs
in the environmental context. This includes many factors, the most important of which
are as follows:

- IT Infrastructure

Cloud computing provides its services completely through the Internet. The lack of a good
infrastructure thus reflects negatively on the cloud computing services. According to Sabi
et al. (2016), the providers of cloud computing services face problems when it comes to
providing their services to companies that are located in places where the technical
infrastructure is poor and does not carry the necessary provision for cloud computing
services. Any technical system that depends on the Internet for its work needs a very
strong technical infrastructure to ensure the continuity of its service provision any time.
After the coronavirus pandemic hit the world, it was realised that countries with a good
technological infrastructure were, for the most part, able to continue their work without
interruption, whether in education or elsewhere. Having a good technical infrastructure
means the presence of renewable and continuous systems that keep pace with the
information technology revolution.

- Enterprise Competitiveness

The concept of competitiveness means that the company is ready to compete in the field
without delay. This will ensure its sustainability and reserve a place and a name for it
among its competitors. Often, some competition is positive in that it pushes all
competitors to adopt everything that meets the aspirations of the field in which they work
without hesitation. Therefore, competing companies must adopt cloud computing
services early on in order to be able to gain the maximum benefit and to obtain better
operational efficiency.

- Physical Location

The physical location is an influential factor regarding where the data is stored by the
service providers. This is because most service providers of cloud computing store their
customers' data in other countries. Consequently, this matter has become a major barrier
to companies when it comes to making the decision to switch to cloud computing services



                                            7
for several reasons, the most important of which is that there are no strict international
laws that regulate the security and integrity of this data.

- Legal Framework

The legal framework is considered to be a very important factor due to the problems that
can occur in this field. This is because sometimes the service provider is in one country,
the beneficiary is in another and the data centre is in a third. This means that the rules
governing this service may differ significantly. Flexible and effective rules that can meet
the global standards are still required. El-Gazzar et al. (2016) discussed this very clearly
and concluded that it is very important to look for a legal framework that is suitable for
both providers and clients, and can meet the local requirements as well. From this study’s
point of view, the adoption of cloud computing services must follow the regulations of
the country that hosts the services in order to be safe. There must be trust in the
supervision of the legal organisation.

Finally, there are the challenges facing the adoption of cloud computing by Saudi SMEs
in the social context, which includes many factors, the most important of which are as
follows:

-Trust:

In cloud computing, trust is considered to be a crucial issue. SMEs are concerned about
how to avoid the lack of transparency and control over data in cloud computing services
in order to keep their reputation at a high level and to maintain people’s trust in their
organisations (Khan & Malluhi, 2010a). Therefore, the lack of trust does not simply refer
to the service provider, but it also refers to the mistrust in the technology itself and its
ability to offer a good service without any loss of data.

-Awareness

Community awareness plays an important role in accepting new technologies for several
different reasons, the most important of which is that there cannot be adoption without
awareness. Therefore, awareness is always considered to be one of the main barriers to
adopting new technologies directly. Thus, SMEs must first educate their employees




                                              8
before adopting cloud computing services in order for them to have sufficient knowledge
and know-how about the services.

- Culture

Culture is an important influence on SMEs as it is on any other organisation, whether
public or private. Culture will reflect and work within it, especially if the company
provides a service related to the community members (Alqahtani, 2016a). Therefore, it is
necessary to study the causes and barriers resulting from this culture to mitigate any
negative influences of said culture on the adoption of cloud computing services.


    1.2 Research Questions

   1. What is the current situation of the adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi
       Arabia SMEs?

   This will be determined by a review and exploration of the barriers and challenges to
   cloud computing adoption in the current systems that are derived from a review of the
   literature.

   2. What are the most influential factors that impact the adoption of cloud computing
       services by Saudi SMEs from the perspective of the top managers, IT managers
       and employees?
   3. What is an appropriate conceptual framework for understanding the adoption of
       cloud computing services by SMEs, specifically in Saudi Arabia?
   4. To what extent is there an impact due to the adoption of cloud computing services
       by Saudi SMEs from the perspective of the IT managers and top managers?


    1.3 Research Objectives

In order to answer the research questions, the following objectives have been established:

   -   To review the current literature in order to explore the barriers and challenges that
       influence the adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi SMEs.




                                            9
   -   To identify the most influential factors that impact the adoption of cloud
       computing services by Saudi SMEs from the perspective of the top managers, IT
       managers and employees.

   -   To develop and validate an appropriate conceptual framework for understanding
       the adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi SMEs.

   -   To investigate the impact of the adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi
       SMEs from the perspective of the IT managers and top managers.


    1.4 Methodology

This research is designed as a primary research study that employs both quantitative
(survey) and qualitative (semi-structured interviews) approaches to collect the data. The
empirical part of this research will be carried out through a number of reliable approaches,
methods, instruments and techniques as well as a philosophical position that reflects the
nature and distinctiveness of this research.

The quantitative approach is widely used in research areas that seek to investigate the
impact of some variables on others. In addition, scientifically, quantitative methods are a
reliable way of measuring the levels of influence between variables and investigating how
critical challenges can be affected by specific phenomenon. Rationally, using the
quantitative approach is an appropriate choice as many advantages have been introduced
and discussed which support the reliability of this method. However, using a qualitative
approach also has advantages as it allows the participants to fully express their views on
particular issues. Nevertheless, the significant advantages of the quantitative approach
have been indicated by Rahman (2020), such as the ability to utilise a larger sample and
being able to acquire and process data relatively quickly. In addition, Nardi (2018) notes
the further advantages of the quantitative method such as the lower cost when compared
to the qualitative method, standardised questions that are ideal for investigating opinions,
the views and personal attitudes towards specific phenomenon, the ease of preserving
anonymity and confidentiality, probability sampling appropriateness, more accurate
generalisability and it being easier to conduct comparative research in similar contexts.




                                               10
    1.5 Data Collection and the Research Instruments

This research employs both quantitative (survey) and qualitative (semi-structured
interviews) approaches to collecting the data.


1.5.1 The Quantitative Approach

       The questionnaire will be designed in accordance with the research objectives and
       hypotheses in order to answer the questions and test the hypotheses that have been
       formulated. Researchers who use questionnaires normally take into consideration
       a number of stages that should be carried out in order to obtain reliable and
       accurate results and outcomes (Brace, 2018; Patten, 2016). In this regard, research
       planning, hypotheses formulation, questions and statement formulations,
       simplicity and the length of the questionnaire should be considered in quantitative
       research that employs this kind of instrument.


1.5.2 The Qualitative Approach

       The qualitative approach is an accredited investigation method according to many
       different academic disciplines. Qualitative researchers aim to deepen their
       understanding of practical behaviours, and the factors or causes that govern and
       drive these behaviours. The qualitative method investigates why and how
       decisions are made, not just what, where and when they are made. Therefore, there
       is a need to focus on small rather than large samples. Therefore, face-to-face
       interviews will be conducted with the top managers of SMEs in Saudi Arabia, as
       well as with the information technology managers in those enterprises, and the
       beneficiaries of the services of those enterprises. This will be in order to achieve
       the objectives of this research and to find direct answers to the research questions.
       Regarding the managers, the period of their employment in their companies will
       need to be for at least five years in order to ensure that the answers provided by
       the participants are more likely to have sufficient expertise and know the needs of
       the SME extremely well.




                                            11
    1.6 Research Population and Sampling Frame

The research population will be comprised of the top managers, employees, policy makers
and IT managers in Saudi SMEs. The use of cloud computing services in this sector has
grown considerably over the last two decades (Boss et al., 2007; Lohr, 2007; Weiss,
2007). The research takes into account the growth recently occurring in a number of
Saudi SMEs that have been established and cannot go forward without the point of view
of the top managers, employees, policy makers and IT managers who will be targeted in
order to get a more balanced perspective.


    1.7 Risk Assessment

This research may face certain risks that could affect its progress. These risks must be
taken into account. These risks are as follows:

   •   The difficulty of collecting information about Saudi SMEs from the
       Communications and Information Technology Commission, as well as getting
       statistics about the companies that do not implement cloud computing in their
       system.

   •   The research requires interviewing managers in Saudi SMEs. However, they may
       refuse to be interviewed owing to their busy work schedules.

   •   Some of the Saudi SMEs may refuse to distribute questionnaires to their
       employees until they have been approved by the company’s legal authorities.

To avoid the problems mentioned above, the Communications and Information
Technology Commission will be contacted to request assistance and to clarify that this
information will be used in scientific research and not for any other purpose. As for
interviewing the company managers, they will be contacted early to allow plenty of time
for them to choose a convenient date. SMEs in Saudi Arabia will also be contacted early
to allow them to get any legal permission necessary for us to conduct the research and
distribute the questionnaires to their employees.




                                            12
    1.8 Research Contributions
This research contributes novel insights into Saudi SMEs' adoption of Cloud computing
services. This section addresses the above research questions in Section 1.3 by presenting
the following main contributions:

1.8.1 Literature Analysis
    This research conducted the literature review (see Chapter 2) and found that adopting
   cloud computing is crucial for SMEs' success in general, particularly in Saudi Arabia.
   However, Saudi SMEs are still in the early stages of cloud adoption. More
   comprehensive research is needed on the factors delaying this process. Previous
   studies focused mainly on obstacles such as security and privacy concerns and
   financial implications, but they did not consider all influencing factors. These factors,
   including expenses, data confidentiality, regulations, trust and culture, significantly
   affect the migration process to cloud services. A comprehensive understanding
   requires examining all these aspects. Notably, most research in Saudi Arabia has
   concentrated on the public sector, and no prior study has comprehensively explored
   the factors influencing cloud adoption among Saudi SMEs. Given the early stage of
   cloud computing in Saudi Arabia, this study aims to find the most critical affecting
   factors and then develop a comprehensive framework to validate them and help Saudi
   SMEs make informed decisions about adopting cloud services. This contribution
   answers Research Question 1 and highlights the current adoption of cloud computing
   in Saudi SMEs.

1.8.2 Influential Factors
   The literature review, a pivotal part of this research, allowed the researcher to identify
   the DOI theory and TOE framework as the most relevant for studying the influential
   factors in cloud adoption, as mentioned in Chapter 3. However, while significant,
   these frameworks need to be expanded to include additional factors. Therefore, this
   study goes beyond the DOI and TOE frameworks, establishing the following
   components as independent variables: Technological Context: This section focuses
   on technical aspects influencing cloud adoption, including Data Confidentiality,
   Integrity, Security, Relative Advantages, Service Cost and Service Quality.
   Organisational Context: Examines managerial and infrastructural aspects, including


                                            13
  Organisation Regulations, Organisation Size, Technology Readiness and Top
  Management Support. Environmental Context: This section considers external factors
  such as Technology Infrastructure, Cloud Provider, Legal Framework and Enterprise
  Competitiveness that might hinder cloud adoption. Social Context: Analyses user
  attitudes and behaviours, focusing on Culture, Awareness and Trust. Identifying these
  factors is crucial for Saudi SMEs to remain competitive in the digital landscape.
  Understanding these factors helps top managers and IT managers formulate strategies
  to overcome obstacles and promote cloud service adoption. This contribution answers
  research question 2 and highlights the factors that affect Saudi SMEs' adoption of
  cloud computing.

1.8.3 Research Framework
  This study developed the Adoption of the Cloud Computing Model by Saudi SMEs
  (ACCM-SME) based on previously identified factors and a thorough review of prior
  research on SME cloud adoption, as shown in Chapter 3. ACCM-SME integrates the
  TOE framework with critical elements of DOI theory and additional challenging
  factors to create a comprehensive model guiding the investigation into cloud adoption.
  The framework aims to boost SMEs' intention to adopt cloud services, enhance the
  perception of cloud computing, and examine its impact. The ACCM-SME model,
  evaluated through descriptive and statistical studies, provides practical insights for
  policymakers and professionals. It sheds light on the factors that drive Saudi SMEs to
  adopt cloud services, forming the basis for the research hypotheses. These hypotheses
  help to understand the impact and relationships of various factors on cloud service
  adoption, thereby guiding policy and practice in the field. Chapter 5 analyses the
  questionnaire results from SMEs to validate the hypotheses and explore the
  relationships between independent and dependent factors in cloud adoption. The
  results for each research model factor are shown in Chapter 5 and discussed in Chapter
  7. This contribution answers Research Question 3 and offers the Adoption of the
  Cloud Computing Model by Saudi SMEs (ACCM-SME).

1.8.4 Impact of Cloud Adoption
  In Chapter 6, a thematic analysis of semi-structured interviews with IT and top
  managers revealed critical insights into their perspectives on cloud adoption. These
  leadership views significantly impact decision-making, offering a deep understanding


                                         14
   of the barriers and opportunities for Saudi SMEs to adopt the Cloud. The analysis
   brought to light recurring themes and trends, which can help researchers and
   organisations understand the challenges leaders face in adopting cloud services. This
   understanding can be a beacon of hope for current businesses and startups, guiding
   them through the difficulties of cloud adoption. Policymakers can use these insights
   to develop supportive technology systems and regulations, further enhancing the
   potential benefits. The thematic analysis identified seven main themes reflecting
   participants' perceptions, discussed in chapters 6 and 7 and illustrated in Figure 6.1.
   This information is valuable for established companies and new ventures planning to
   adopt cloud computing and remain competitive, as well as to researchers in the field.
   This contribution answers Research Question 4 and highlights the impact of Saudi
   SMEs' adoption of cloud computing from the perspectives of top managers and IT
   managers.


    1.9 Research Structure

This thesis consists of eight chapters, as shown in Table 1.1, and the research stages that
have been followed are shown in Figure 1.2.

                                Table 1-1: Thesis chapters
   Chapters      Headings
   Chapter 1     Introduction

   Chapter 2     Literature Review
   Chapter 3     Theoretical Framework for the Adoption of Cloud Computing Services
   Chapter 4     Research Methodology

   Chapter 5     Quantitative Data Analysis
   Chapter 6     Qualitative Data Analysis
   Chapter 7     Discussion
   Chapter 8     Conclusion and Recommendations




                                              15
Figure 1-2: The Research Stages




              16
Chapter
2 Literature review
    2.1 Introduction
As shown in Figure 2.1, this chapter reviews the literature relating to the adoption of cloud
computing services by SMEs in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia. The chapter will
investigate the background of Cloud computing, define cloud computing, describe its
characteristics, its deployment and service models, and the general architecture of cloud
computing.




      Figure 2-1 Stages 1&2: Investigating and identifying influential factors
The chapter also provides an overview of SMEs in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, the size
of these enterprises and how they operate. Statistics for these companies detailing the
number of employees, branches and their field of work are shown in order to facilitate an
understanding of the environment in which these enterprises operate. An overview of the
Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, its geographical location and its population is briefly outlined.



                                             17
Also, the work of the Small and Medium Enterprises General Authority (Monsha’at)
which was established by the Saudi government to support and encourage SMEs is
summarised to show its influence. Moreover, Also, this chapter will address the role of
the government of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia in supporting SMEs through Vision 2030
that was recently launched.

The chapter focuses on the quality of the Internet and its infrastructure in Saudi Arabia,
as this is an influential factor in the adoption of cloud computing services in the Kingdom
of Saudi Arabia. Literature relating to the challenges facing Saudi SMEs in adopting
Cloud computing is reviewed and discussed as well as any that addresses the potential
benefits of adopting cloud computing services in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia. It
concludes by analysing and reviewing previous studies related to research in developed
and developing countries, and Saudi Arabia, and the knowledge gaps in the literature are
identified.


    2.2 Cloud Computing Background
With the development and explosion of the Internet over the past years, the latest
qualitative leap has made the business of cloud computing grow and prosper. In view of
this, cloud computing technology (CCT) has arisen as an important tool that enhances
cooperation between organizations (Attaran, 2017). However, cloud computing services
developed rapidly over the past five years and now grow up at a compound annual growth
rate (CAGR) of 18 percent on the new SMEs that are looking to create innovative income
chances for cloud sellers (Dahlqvist et al., 2019). Therefore, there are still some barriers
that face SMEs in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia in adopting cloud computing services,
and the most important of these barriers are the infrastructure of these facilities and
internet services as well.

The cost of using traditional methods of storing data, the energy needed by computers
and the hardware has risen sharply and continues to rise as the demand for these systems
increases. Thus, the provision of a specific amount of storage space or quality of internet
service does not solve the problem, as it is necessary to find new quality solutions, such
as cloud computing, that keep pace with developments in technology to reshape those
traditional computing problems (Islam & Reza, 2019). As companies increasingly face
these problems, they need information about new platforms in order to improve their



                                            18
businesses, and they require accurate data about cloud computing. Many enterprises could
benefit from cloud computing in order to take advantage of vacant resources, increase
economic efficiency based on utilization rate, and reduce their hardware energy
consumption (Zhang, Zhang, et al., 2010). Therefore, the process of switching to cloud
computing services would potentially have a very large positive impact on technical,
commercial and human levels, although some studies indicate the low level of cloud
service acceptance by consumers because of privacy risks (Y.-W. Chang & Hsu, 2019).
Consequently, there are barriers facing this transformation and solutions must be found,
and previous experiences of the process of transformation to cloud computing services
must be made good use of.

As cloud computing services become available when needed and at low cost, actual users
will be able to benefit from those services without the need to buy any hardware or update
software since the cloud services include all of them (Almajalid, 2017). Cloud computing
offers a number of other advantages, the most important of which is flexibility in the
amount of cloud computing resources used to suit the on-demand delivery of computing
services (Abubakar, 2016b). Accordingly, cloud computing services can be accessed by
users based on service level agreements that govern, maintain and develop the computing
services provided on request.

The main characteristics of cloud computing are summarized by Armbrust et al. (2010)
in three important points, which are: that computing resources are available upon request
without delay; there is flexibility in the ability to modify resource allocation according to
the customer’s need for them; and that the customer is able to pay fees to the service
provider. Cloud computing offers many services that have been classified into three main
types, namely infrastructure services, platform services and software services (Rashid &
Chaturvedi, 2019) (see Figure 2.2); and therefore, service providers differ from one
company to another based on their ownership of a tracking service for one of the
previously mentioned service types. Clients also look for a suitable service that is
appropriate for their work environment.




                                             19
                Figure 2-2: Layers of Cloud (Rashid & Chaturvedi, 2019)
 Note: IaaS (Infrastructure as a service), PaaS (platform as a service), and SaaS (software as a service).


The fast development of cloud computing and the possibility of owning software and
computing capability remotely, unlike the traditional model of IT, means that the term
‘cloud computing’ is widely discussed by businesses as well as IT companies (Zhang,
Chen, et al., 2010). However, there is no single fixed definition which describes cloud
computing precisely, as it is possible to find a number of definitions in the field (Zhang,
2008). When the idea of ‘cloud computing’ first emerged, it may have seemed strange to
many people, as it was an entity that was purporting to provide traditional services for
information technology remotely and without much need for human intervention in its
services.

A more detailed description of cloud computing is that it is a combination of distributed
computing, grid computing and parallel computing that has been developed to operate as
a single system under a single term that provides a set of services that solve the traditional
problems existing in old systems (Huang & Wang, 2010), and the NIST definition of
cloud computing is more precise, as the researcher mentions on page 23. For example,
every day many people use their local computer to send emails, files and other data from
their personal devices. These elements form an important part of daily business, and


                                                    20
losing them means losing a lot of work, in which a lot of time and effort may have been
invested while the cloud services providers offer backup service like Amazon’s S3 (You
et al., 2012). Therefore, the concept of cloud computing appeared as a solution to this
problem as ‘the Cloud’ was a means to store and process data through a group of devices
deployed to provide services on time, without interruption and continuously ensuring that
stored data would not be lost (Alam & Benaida, 2018). This is because one of the
advantages of cloud computing is that it continuously updates the items stored in it when
any modification occurs by multiple users and keeps the Cloud active, such as Dropbox
(Yuan & Yu, 2014). Thus, the adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs enable
them to transfer traditional infrastructure services into the network and are available
anytime and anywhere on demand.

Cloud computing helps organisations access data resources remotely through internet
services. This accessibility is provided and implemented through a series of processes,
equipment and techniques that can transform the managerial, financial and technical
practices and procedures into information that is normally stored in external servers. This
can be done through either public or private cloud services. The existing literature implies
that the possible challenges of cloud computing have been concentrated in developed
countries, such as by the members of the Organization for Economic Cooperation and
Development (OECD) (Abubakar, 2016). However, other countries still need to conduct
further investigations into how such services can pose significant threats to confidentiality
and data integrity. In Saudi Arabia, although recent years have witnessed a considerable
development in internet services including cloud computing services, dealing with
advanced security issues requires further investigation and understanding (Alghamdi et
al., 2019b;Alassafi et al., 2016b). The recent establishment of the Saudi Data and
Artificial Intelligence Authority (SDAIA) is considered to be a sign of interest and
orientation towards digital technologies in general, and cloud computing services in
particular. The existence of a government organisation that supports the migration to
cloud computing services, whether in the public or private sector, is significant.

Cloud computing has become more widespread and provides various technical services,
and resources. The shift from storing data in internal sources to external sources is central,
along with other factors such as the low cost, self-service nature and flexible network
access. All of these are what prompts the public and private sectors to switch to cloud



                                             21
computing services (Pibul, 2018). From the point of view of this research, cost and self-
service are not the only factors affecting the SMEs’ adoption of cloud computing services
in Saudi Arabia; and there are more important factors that will be covered in detail later
on.


      2.3 Cloud Computing Concept
Cloud computing is a combination of a number of technologies that are used as a complete
service, as the majority of information technology services employed by users can come
in a variety of forms, such as virtualization and Web 2.0, and all these services can
potentially be contained within a single technical environment, and it is this environment
that constitutes ‘the Cloud’ (Jeffery & Neidecker-Lutz, 2010). Cloud computing has
reshaped information technology, as it has become a pool that contains many resources
and services that contribute to building cloud computing systems that meet the objectives
of the enterprises at low cost.

According to Gong et al. (2010), in 2007, after Google and IBM’s announcement about
their cooperation in cloud computing, the term ‘cloud computing’ became popularized.
Also, the transition to cloud computing services is becoming more common because it
decreases cost and provides flexibility (Wang et al., 2010). For example, users can use
cloud services from any place and any time and only pay for the services they use.

In fact, the concept of ‘cloud computing’ brings together many researchers and others
interested in this field, so we may find a variety of names and definitions, depending on
the goal of the study and interests of the researcher. In addition, the different technologies
used in cloud computing are on a continuous path towards development and have no
specific stopping point (Al Khater, 2017a). As a result, the term ‘cloud computing’ has
become a global term that is constantly being discussed and updated with the ongoing
development and the increasing demand for adopting cloud computing services in many
developing countries such as the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia.

According to Oyemike (2017), Amazon has been officially approved as the pioneer of
cloud computing because it was the first company to provide cloud computing services,
such as virtual data storage space and server access, to its customers. Amazon actually
entered the field of cloud computing in 2006 when they modernized their Amazon Web
Service (AWS) in order to be able to deal with a large amount of data and to improve the


                                             22
efficiency of their internal systems (Hyseni & Ibrahimi, 2017). After they had built these
systems and conducted an analysis of their use, they realized that many of these systems’
resources were not being used, so they decided to offer these resources to clients, whether
individuals or companies, for hire on demand (Maurya et al., 2021). Amazon then decided
to provide cloud computing services to external customers, and launched the Amazon
Web Service (AWS). After Amazon's investment in the field of cloud computing services
had been shown to have been successfully developed, other major companies such as
Google and Microsoft decided to provide similar services to their customers (Al-Sayyed
et al., 2019). Just as developing countries seek to emulate developed countries that have
succeeded in adopting cloud computing services, so companies benefit from the
experiences of their counterparts in the same field and develop their enterprises to be
unique with better cloud services.

The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) in the US has created a
definition for the cloud computing, and many researchers have agreed on this definition
and used it widely, because it includes most of the characteristics related to this concept.
The NIST defined cloud computing as “a model for enabling ubiquitous, convenient, on-
demand network access to a shared pool of configurable computing resources (e.g.,
networks, servers, storage, applications, and services) that can be rapidly provisioned
and released with minimal management effort or service provider interaction” (Mell &
Grance, 2011). This definition is considered acceptable because it includes all major
aspects of the concept of cloud computing, and includes how to enable users to access the
network and its resources at any time and from anywhere from a shared pool of computing
resources that are provided.

Most researchers always try hard to find an appropriate definition that includes all the
various aspects of the term. For example, Buyya et al. (2009) defined cloud computing as
follows: “Cloud is a type of parallel and distributed system consisting of a collection of
interconnected and virtualized computers that are dynamically provisioned and
presented as one or more unified computing resource(s) based on service-level
agreements established through negotiation between the service provider and
consumers”.

More than 20 definitions exploring the characteristics and features of the term ‘cloud
computing’ have been studied and reviewed by Vaquero et al. (2008). Thus, researchers


                                             23
have generally defined the term ‘cloud computing’ as consisting of several large virtual
resources formed as a group that works together and can be quickly provided to handle
the required load, and where it is easy to access and use these resources which are
available upon request. In addition, cloud computing is characterized by the application
of the payment-as-use model within the range of service level agreements (SLA's)
between the service provider and the consumer (Subramanian & Jeyaraj, 2018).
Consequently, all the definitions mentioned above come from different scientific sources
and explain the concept of cloud computing from several different aspects to understand
that term more deeply and ensure that the idea of cloud computing is very clear.
Therefore, the NIST definition of cloud computing is considered comprehensive for
describing all the aspects of cloud computing and has been used extensively in many
scientific studies, so this research will use the NIST definition for cloud computing.

2.3.1 Cloud Computing Characteristics
For the sake of a prosperous technological future in cloud computing services, whether
application or web service, promising cloud computing features have emerged to
guarantee distinct services that keep pace with the development that is taking place in this
field (Rashid & Chaturvedi, 2019). The National Institute of Standards and Technology
(NIST) has already identified five main characteristics of cloud computing. This research
will cover those five important characteristics along with others that have recently been
discussed in the literature as follows:

2.3.1.1 On-Demand Self-Service

Cloud computing services can be provided by the service providers to the end user with
the capacity required and without any human intervention (Diaby & Rad, 2017). Thus,
these services, such as storage, network access, processing, etc. are provided
automatically.

2.3.1.2 Broad Network Access (mobility)

According to Olive (2011), in the 1990s companies were avoiding connecting terminals
to powerful minicomputers and trying to use simpler devices like desktop-PCs; and this
is the opposite of what is happening now. Users can now access cloud computing services
from various devices, whether PCs, laptops, or mobile devices any time and from
anywhere through internet connection.


                                            24
2.3.1.3 Resource Pooling

All cloud computing service providers are keen to offer their services as a pool of
resources to serve end-users and meet their needs through multi-tenant systems (Diaby &
Rad, 2017). Therefore, using virtualization technology, clients can share resources with
others.

2.3.1.4 Rapid Elasticity

Based on the client’s request, the resources can be provided rapidly and elastically
according to the consumer's need for those resources available upon request (Rastogi &
Sushil, 2015). Thus, the clients can scale the resources up or down automatically and
purchase any time in any quantity as they need them.

2.3.1.5 Location Independency

It is not necessary for the users to know the physical location of the data centre, and in
which country or city it is located. The most important thing is the continuity, quality and
presence of the service for the end user, who probably does not know the actual location
of that service. For example, Microsoft provides their services to clients in the Middle
East although their data centre is located in Dublin (Azeemi et al., 2013). However, clients
sometimes are given the choice of knowing the exact location of the data centre.

2.3.1.6 Measured Service

A group of cloud computing service providers can use systems that enable them to control
and monitor the computing resources provided to the end-users to ensure that the
resources are optimally used as assigned (Rastogi & Sushil, 2015). Therefore, the use of
cloud computing resources will someday be like people's daily use of electricity, water
and gas resources.

2.3.1.7 Pay-Per-Use

This feature enables customers to stop wasting resources, as the clients pay for use only
(Suryateja, 2018) without the need for a local server or reserving large spaces without
using them. Therefore, everyone, whether individuals or SMEs. will be able to take
advantage of this feature, which eliminates the setting up of technical infrastructure and
reduces the cost.




                                            25
2.3.2 Cloud Computing Deployment Models
Due to differences in the nature of the work done by SMEs, it is natural for there to be
differences in demand for cloud computing technologies based on different business
tasks. Therefore, various deployment models for cloud computing have recently
appeared, but according to NIST there are four main cloud computing deployment
models: Public Cloud, Private Cloud, Hybrid Cloud and Community Cloud.

2.3.2.1 Public Cloud

Service providers in this type provide the service remotely and for the public through the
Internet; and often, services such as storage, e-mails, software, etc. are free (see Figure
2.3). Many people benefit from this service in several ways in their daily lives, and some
SMEs employ these free services provided to the public for their employees to use in line
with policies set by the service provider (Imhanwa, 2017). Google applications are the
best example of this and are now widely used in daily work; for example, Gmail for
messaging, Microsoft Office 365 for word processing, Drive for storage, Meet for
meetings, etc. (Lakshminarayanan et al., 2013). Accordingly, the United Arab Emirates
is one of the countries that took early steps in adopting cloud computing services in the
MENA region, especially in SMEs, where, in 2017, it spent Dh439 million on public
cloud services (Alsharari et al., 2020). Moreover, the demand for adopting these services
is expected to grow and a further Dh1.51 billion spent on them (Alsharari et al., 2020).
As for the cost, public cloud services come at very low cost as they are most often
provided free of charge, and may be suitable for use in SMEs.




                      Figure 2-3:Public Cloud (Imhanwa, 2017)

                                            26
2.3.2.2 Private Cloud

This type is the opposite of the public cloud, as it is often used by large organizations.
This is because a private cloud is used by one organization only, and is only intended for
the workers and customers of that organization and resources are not shared with anyone
outside of the organization (see Figure 2.4). Therefore, the private cloud is controlled and
owned by the organization, the service provider or both as agreed (Mell & Grance, 2011).
In fact, the cloud for this model is arguably similar to the traditional IT infrastructure; it
is generally considered to come at a high cost and does not exhibit the characteristics of
cloud computing that pertain to the public cloud. The private cloud can however be
provided with a pay-per-use arrangement, and might be owned or rented by the
organization for internal use (Grossman, 2009). Lebanon is one of the developing
countries in the MENA region where SMEs were considered to be in a position to adopt
cloud computing services but failed to do so. Skafi et al (2020) investigated the reasons
for this delay in cloud adoption and found that SMEs did not deploy even a private cloud
service for a number of reasons, including poor infrastructure, lack of government
support, lack of top management support and lack of technical experience. A private cloud
may be suitable for organizations that have a large number of customers as it affords a
large number of users. Amazon Virtual Private Cloud, Azure Private Cloud from
Microsoft and NetApp Private Storage for Cloud from NetApp are all examples of private
cloud companies.




                      Figure 2-4: Private Cloud (Imhanwa, 2017)


                                             27
2.3.2.3 Hybrid Cloud

This model is a combination of two types and as the term ‘hybrid’ explains, as this word
gives an indication that there are two elements working together towards one goal (see
Figure 2.5). In a hybrid cloud, data, applications and services related to important business
are under the control of the organization while the less important processes are managed
and operated by the third party in the public cloud (Imhanwa, 2017). Thus, this model is
effective for organizations that want to acquire a private cloud to fulfil their daily business
requirements, whatever these are, and at the same time reduce their costs by using public
clouds for processes that do not require a private cloud (Kim et al., 2009). Hybrid cloud
marketing has begun to appear every day with more offers, that present themselves to
customers with features that enable them to have greater control over their resources. This
field is in fact new in the world of cloud computing and it must be studied thoroughly to
know its pros and cons if organizations, especially SMEs, are to adopt it (Sultan,
2011).Through this mechanism, the organization will be taking advantage of both public
and private clouds under the name of ‘hybrid cloud’ by using the private cloud to share
data privately and internally to the organization only, in order to maintain security, and
using the public cloud for less sensitive processes to reduce the cost.




                      Figure 2-5: Hybrid Cloud (Imhanwa, 2017)


                                              28
2.3.2.4 Community Cloud

This model is new to many organizations, because it has only recently appeared, and does
not have much popularity in the cloud market yet. According to Goyal, (2014) the
principle for operation in this model is based on the sharing of infrastructure between two
or more organizations, where the management and operation of that infrastructure is
conducted by a group of organizations benefiting from the services or by a third party
(see Figure 2.6). Furthermore, this model is built for a specific community that shares the
same interests, so that these technical resources are shared with more than one
organization and not limited to a specific organization over the Internet (Schubert Jeffery,
2012). Therefore, it can be said that community cloud is a technology that has been
reformulated by organizations to meet the needs of organizations that share the same goals
as it has the same requirements and policies for each organization (Mohlameane &
Ruxwana, 2014). Moreover, the cost is shared among all users of a community cloud,
whether they are individuals or organizations, which ensures that there is a reduction in
the cost based on the sharing of payment. The perfect example of what has been explained
above is the US-based dedicated IBM SoftLayer cloud for federal agencies.




                   Figure 2-6: Community Cloud (Imhanwa, 2017)


                                            29
2.3.3 Cloud Computing Architecture and Services Models
Cloud computing architecture is composed of a group of four layers, each of which has a
specific action, and are known as the application, platform, infrastructure and hardware
layers (Foster et al., 2008), (see Figure 2.7). Therefore, end-users can take advantage of
applications in the top ‘application’ layer; whereas the platform layer provides the
necessary software (i.e., the programming language and the operating system) in the cloud
to build applications at this stage before they are finally presented to the end-users. The
infrastructure layer in which computing resources are shared with the end users is based
on virtual computing technologies; and in this layer, both management and operation are
by the computing service provider (Bokhari et al., 2016). The last layer is the hardware
which is at the bottom of the arrangement of layers, as it is considered the main part which
contains all the physical tools used to build the cloud computing system, such as servers,
power generators, monitoring systems, cooling systems etc. All these layers are
integrative in that they work together to produce a cloud computing system that achieves
the desired goals, and all these layers are interdependent (Rashid & Chaturvedi, 2019).




    Figure 2-7: Cloud Computing Architecture (Q. Zhang, Cheng, et al., 2010)



The ‘back end’ and ‘front end’ are considered two main aspects of a cloud computing
architecture. The front end becomes available to the user via internet connections,


                                            30
allowing for user interaction with the device; while the different cloud service models
make up the back end (Odun-Ayo et al., 2018). Therefore, cloud computing service
models can be thought of as falling into three types of service model, which are: Software
as a Service (SaaS), Platform as a Service (PaaS) and Infrastructure as a Service (IaaS)
(see Figure 2.2 and 2.8) (Tadapaneni, 2017). More details about each of these service
models will be discussed in the next section, which will explain the differences between
each of them and how they work.

2.3.3.1 Software as a Service (SaaS)

Software as a service often depends on the other two types that will be discussed below.
The service providers in this type need an infrastructure that contains its own applications
in which they can manage their services and provide them to their customers through a
virtual machine. SaaS also needs a platform that hosts the services it provides to its
customers. So, potential SaaS customers are looking for a service that meets their business
needs, regardless of how that service is set up and provided (Shojaiemehr et al., 2018). In
this model, a wide range of services are provided to customers by providing full access
to all available products, either free of charge or with specific fees, as agreed on, which
is usually pay-per-use (see Section 2.3.1.7 on cloud characteristics). Software as a service
enables the user to obtain the service without entering into the complexities of managing
and updating the provided services and their maintenance or obtaining their own hard
hardware (Srivastava & Khan, 2018). It therefore appears to the customers that they have
purchased the final service they benefit from; but in reality, the customer pays for this
indirectly as the final service depends on an infrastructure and a platform that is fully
managed and operated by the service provider who enables the final SaaS. Software as a
service is provided to organizations, especially SMEs, who can then enable their
employees to benefit from it, and individuals who pay a monthly fee. Examples include
Google Apps and Microsoft Office 365.

2.3.3.2 Platform as a Service (PaaS)

According to Simmon (2018), cloud service customers (CSCs) are given the ability to use
platform resources to create an application using available platform tools such as
programming languages, libraries and servers supported by the service provider while the
cloud service provider (CSP) manages and operates the underlying cloud infrastructure
including networks, storage, servers and operating systems. This service is similar to SaaS


                                            31
as services can be rented from the provider by the customers, but more extensively; as in
this model, customers can purchase the service as a platform on which to build and host
applications in their preferred way; and to control application configurations and
application-hosting environment settings. While SaaS enables the customer to modify
existing applications to meet their needs in line with the features available in the
application, the PaaS gives the customer complete freedom to alter the original software
they now own (Oyemike, 2017).

The Alibaba as a service platform is a great contributor to the transformation process
towards various technical services, as it has enabled SMEs to obtain technical
functionalities and business services that went beyond traditional methods of transaction
processing in order to ease the acquiring any new software or tool (Li et al., 2018). From
the point of view of the research, the PaaS is a very good option for SMEs, as they can
hire service providers to host the company's applications without wasting effort and
money on a traditional infrastructure. If there is any need for an infrastructure to support
advanced private applications, then Infrastructure as a Service (IaaS) is an option, and is
discussed in the next section.

2.3.3.3 Infrastructure as a Service (IaaS)

IaaS refers to an integrated technical infrastructure that appeared with the emergence of
the term ‘cloud computing’, and includes all the services that can be provided in a
traditional infrastructure such as servers, networks, storage, processing, etc. This
infrastructure is located in another place, often far from the organization benefiting from
the service, whether inside the country where the organization is located or outside it; and
it is used remotely via the Internet (Wang & Wang, 2020). The physical infrastructure is
managed by the CSP, and there is a virtual infrastructure system that is delivered to the
CSCs who operate the infrastructure virtually and permanently control it with the support
of the cloud service provider. According to Mell and Grance (2011) the storage, operating
system and deployed applications are controlled by end users; and CSCs are able to obtain
full management powers to achieve their requirements from the system without referring
to the CSP, except in cases that require its intervention. Therefore, there are many
companies that provide IaaS and are working continuously to develop their services and
attract businesses to their various services, common examples of which are Rackspace,




                                            32
Amazon Web Services (AWS), Microsoft Azure and Google Compute Engine (GCE)
(Rashid & Chaturvedi, 2019).




          Figure 2-8: Cloud Computing Service Models (Imhanwa, 2017)

    2.4 Study Context
Small and medium enterprises (SMEs) are the best means of developing and motivating
the local economy for any country that desires a fair, competitive environment that creates
investment opportunities and opens the way for everyone to participate. SMEs improve
the areas in which they arise by providing employment and training opportunities for local
people as well as giving creative people with innovative ideas a chance to find solutions
and try out new methods (Van Gils, 2005). However, hiring people and supplying
customers with products is not the only goal of small and medium enterprises, as they
may also have a role as the right hand of large companies; and supply these companies,
thus fulfilling their market needs without entering into contracts with individuals (van de
Vrande et al., 2009) In effect, a lot of large companies interact with SMEs and also act as
their main suppliers as well as getting their supplies from SMEs at the same time (Müller
et al., 2018). However, when SMEs trade with large companies, there are requirements
imposed by these companies on SMEs newly emerging in the market, which often entails
the latter having to adopt new services, especially technological ones. Originally, most of
the existing large companies started within the same level of SMEs, but due to the success




                                            33
of their goals and plans they have become large companies; and current SMEs can one
day reach the same level as those large companies.

The European Commission defines SMEs as those establishments that have no more than
250 employees, and whose annual income does not exceed EUR 50 million (Mittal et al.,
2018). In 2010, the European Commission explained that European SMEs have grown
rapidly and twice as fast as large companies that do the same task, while international
SMEs have grown three times faster than the large companies that work locally with them
in the same field, and provide the same services to their customers (Love & Roper, 2015).
SMEs are often owned by individuals or large companies who establish them as ‘invisible
hands’ that can perform small tasks for them. Therefore, these enterprises face several
obstacles and risks, for example, inconsistency of information between companies and
investors, lack of financial guarantees, etc.; which are some of the financial constraints
faced by SMEs (Mittal et al., 2018).

SMEs in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia are not very different from their peers in other
countries, but the type of business and the surrounding environment in each region are
what make Saudi enterprises unique with particular characteristics that affect the form
and content of their operations. Therefore, the definition of SMEs can vary from one
country to another, each according to the criteria specified for it, such as the number of
employees and the annual revenue (Augustine & Asiedu, 2017). The Small and Medium
Enterprises General Authority (Monsha’at) in Saudi Arabia was established in 2016 by a
decision of the Saudi Government to support and direct the SME sector (Monsha’at,
2016) This body clarified that SMEs are defined by size according to their compliance
with the criteria for the number of full-time employees and the volume of revenue
together; in the case of exceptions, the higher classification between the two criteria is
taken (Monsha’at, 2021). Based on the data provided by Monsha’at in the Kingdom of
Saudi Arabia, enterprises whose number of employees ranges from 1 to 5 full-time
employees and who have annual revenues of 0 to 3 million riyals, are considered micro-
enterprises; while enterprises who have 6 to 49 full-time employees and annual revenues
of 3 to 40 million riyals, are considered small enterprises. Medium enterprises have 50 to
249 full-time employees and annual revenues ranging from 40 to 200 million riyals.




                                           34
2.4.1 Overview of the Saudi Context
This section provides an overview of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia (KSA) where the
study will be conducted and explains why this country was chosen as a location in which
to study the possibility of adopting cloud computing services by SMEs.

The establishment of the Third Saudi State was by King Abdul Aziz bin Abdul Rahman
Al Saud in 1902, and after that several stages took place in the formation of the country
until King Abdul Aziz bin Abdul Rahman Al Saud announced on September 23, 1932 the
unification of the Third Saudi State under the name of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, with
its primary language being Arabic and its constitution, the Qur’an and Sunna (UNDP,
2015). The KSA is one of the largest producers and exporters of petroleum worldwide,
and it is also rich in gold, silver, iron ore, copper, natural gas, and phosphates. All this
has made the KSA a fertile ground for attracting the private sector, whether at the local
or global level; and it is considered to be one of the largest economies in the Arab
countries in terms of SMEs (Al Sharekh, 2018).

There is also a recent transformation led by the Saudi government with the support of its
current monarch King Salman bin Abdul Aziz Al Saud who is officially known as the
Custodian of the Two Holy Mosques (Alsaif, 2014; The World Factbook, 2021) and direct
supervision from the second man in the country, Crown Prince Mohammed bin Salman,
who has directed most of his attention to the private sector and is interested in organizing
and supporting it. Especially after the drop in oil prices, the Saudi government was
determined to improve and develop its economy and diversify its sources of income
instead of relying solely on oil (Nurunnabi, 2017).

2.4.2 Location
The KSA is considered the dominant country in the Middle East and the Arabian Gulf,
and as well the largest country in the Arabian Gulf, with an area of 2,149,690 square
kilometers. It is located specifically in the southwestern part of the Asian continent and
its capital city is Riyadh (Worlddata, 2021). The country is bordered to the east by the
Arabian Gulf, the United Arab Emirates and the State of Qatar, to the west by the Red
Sea, to the north by Kuwait, Iraq and Jordan, and to the south by Yemen and Oman (see
Figure 2.9) (freeworldmaps, 2021). Saudi Arabia is characterized as the Qibla
(direction/focus) for Muslims, where the Masjid-al-Haram in Mecca and the Masjid-e-


                                            35
Nabwi (the Prophet Mosque) in Medina are located, and Muslims are directed to the city
of Mecca from all parts of the earth five times a day to perform their prayers. Moreover,
what distinguishes the geography of the KSA is its different nature and terrain. It contains
deserts of different shapes and colours, as is also the case with its mountains. A good
example of this is the Ar Rub‘ al Khali Desert, which occupies the largest part of the
eastern region (Edgell, 2006).

The KSA is a member of the Group of Twenty (G20) and is considered an important
member due to its active role in regulating energy markets, as it is one of the largest oil-
exporting countries in the world, and oil is a commodity in high demand and much traded
in (Yao et al., 2015). The KSA is also characterized by its economic strength and its
influence in the global market, and is the 17th largest exporting countries in the world, as
it has huge potential for supplying global markets and filling the deficit. It was also
classified by the World Bank in 2017 as a high-income country. In addition, the KSA
chaired the G20 meetings for a year starting from December 2019 until November 2020,
which were held virtually due to the Coronavirus pandemic (COVID-19) under the theme
of ‘Realizing Opportunities of the 21st Century for All’ (Kirton, 2020).




              Figure 2-9: Map of Saudi Arabia (freeworldmaps, 2021)



                                            36
2.4.3 Population
According to official data published by the General Authority for Statistics (GAS), the
total population in Saudi Arabia in mid-2020 was 35,013,414 (20,231,425 males
[57.78%] and 14,781,989 females [42.22%]). (see Figure 2.10). The total population
under the age of 40 reached 24,188,384, which represents 69.08% of the total population.
As for the non-Saudi population, the last report by the General Authority for Statistics in
2018 estimated the number of non-Saudi residents as 12,645,033, which represents 38%
of the total population (GAS, 2021a).

The Saudi people are generally receptive to using technologies, as the General Authority
for Statistics indicated in 2017 that 84.4% of the population used the Internet as an
educational tool. Additionally, more than half the Saudi population, aged 25 and over,
had obtained 12 years of education, while the percentage of those with a university degree
or higher was 28.1% of males and 25.5% of females. Having an educated population that
is receptive to technologies helps in the process of transition towards the adoption of
technologies such as cloud computing services, especially since a wide range of the Saudi
population is represented in all sectors of SMEs.




           Figure 2-10: Numbers of population in mid-2020 (GAS, 2021)




                                            37
2.4.4 SMEs General Authority
The introduction of small and medium enterprises to any existing market may expose
them to a number of risks that may lead to a decline and ultimately to closure unless there
is a regulatory body and general frameworks in which institutional work is organized.
Therefore, the SMEs General Authority (Monsha’at) has recently been established in the
Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, with modern strategies that support emerging enterprises, such
as a Kafala loan guarantee programme and a law to reduce the administrative burden on
the smaller companies, which supports SMEs to obtain adequate funding (Tripathi, 2019).
The Monsha’at was established in 2016 and its goals are to support, develop and follow
