up SMEs in Saudi Arabia to achieve a high level parallel to international practices to raise
the productivity of these enterprises and their contribution, and to increase GDP from
20% to 35% by 2030 (Monsha’at, 2021). In fact, the establishment of this authority was
a very judicious decision as it supports SMEs on various levels, in terms of facilitating
business, opening up financing horizons, increasing growth, developing capability,
promoting entrepreneurship (see Figure 2.11), and also, most importantly, supporting
them with technical expertise and technical regulations that facilitate the adoption of new
services such as cloud computing.




              Figure 2-11: Monsha’at strategic plans (Monsha’at, 2021)




                                            38
2.4.5 The Number of Saudi SMEs
In 2020 the Monsha’at announced that despite the circumstances that the world
experienced due to the Corona pandemic that came in late 2019, the number of Saudi
SMEs continued to increase, reaching a total of 626,000 enterprises in 2020 compared to
560,000 in 2019. Moreover, the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia advanced ten places in the
Entrepreneurship Status Index to occupy the seventh position, having only ranked 41st in
2018 (GEM, 2021). As for the number of workers, the number in micro-enterprises (that
employ 1 to 5 workers) reached 2,162,924 in the third quarter of 2019, which constitutes
26% of workers in the private sector; while the number of workers in small enterprises
(that employ 6 to 49 workers) in the same period was 1,819,753, which constitutes 22%
of workers in the private sector; and medium-sized enterprises (that employ 50 to 249
workers) employed 1,496,465 workers, which constitutes 18% of workers in the private
sector (see Table 2.1) (GAS, 2021). The General Authority for Statistics also indicated
that the revenues of SMEs amounted to 54% of the total revenues for the private sector
(see Figure 2.12).




     Table 2-1: Total number of Employees in the private Sector (GAS, 2021)




                                          39
         Figure 2-12: Percentage of revenues by private sector (GAS, 2021)



2.4.6 Vision 2030
Vision 2030 was launched with the generous blessing of King Salman bin Abdul Aziz
and implemented by the Crown Prince of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, Prince
Mohammed bin Salman on April 25, 2016. By 2030, the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia will
have completed the implementation of all projects and plans in this planned vision,
including the delivery of 80 giant government projects, costing from 3.7 billion riyals up
to 20 billion riyals (Vision2030, 2021). Vision 2030 is a reform plan designed to meet the
challenges that the Saudi government would face in the future from a drop in oil prices
and thus to alter the government’s complete dependence on oil for all its expenditure. The
Vision appeared accompanied by the National Transformation Program 2020 (NTP)
which aimed to balance the government’s income sources (Moshashai et al., 2020).
Moreover, Vision 2030 included the Saudi government’s great interest in the SME sector
as one of the most important drivers of economic growth by setting a goal to raise the
percentage of their contribution to the GDP from 20% to 35% in addition to competing
with the G20 countries, the largest economies in the world.

2.4.7 Information and Communication Technology (ICT)
       Infrastructure
The technical infrastructure of any country in the world has become one standard by
which the strength of the state is measured, as all countries are trying hard to build a


                                           40
national infrastructure that supports various aspects of life such as education, health,
entertainment, e-commerce and communication. This includes providing a suitable
infrastructure for the private sector, which includes SMEs; and which represents a large
part of the output of the GDP of any country. Accordingly, the Saudi government decided
in 2003 to restructure the authority responsible for communications and information
technology in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia to become the Ministry of Communications
and Information Technology and to include under its structure many institutions that
organize and implement communications and information technology policies (CITC,
2021).

In 2020, the Ministry of Communications and Information Technology adopted and
widely disseminated a cloud computing policy to all the beneficiaries of its services as
part of the ministry's development process towards an effective technical infrastructure;
and any government agencies calling for new investments in technical services and
infrastructure, were required to choose cloud computing services as their first choice
instead of traditional internal solutions (MCIT, 2020). Certainly, supporting the
organization responsible for the technical infrastructure in any country and giving it
higher powers and logistical and financial support means having a strong technical
infrastructure that can be relied upon to organize and run the work of all sectors, whether
public or private. In addition, the adoption of new technologies by the authorities
responsible for the technical infrastructure is a positive factor that helps to convince the
beneficiaries; and the recent adoption of cloud computing services by the Saudi Ministry
of Communications and Information Technology can be seen as a move that supports the
shift towards the adoption of cloud computing services.

This section begins by describing the state of the ICT infrastructure in the Kingdom of
Saudi Arabia 15 years ago, followed by a discussion of the radical changes that took place
in the ICT infrastructure and its continuous development. In 2007, Gharieb (2007)
conducted a study to identify the challenges facing the development of ICT in Saudi
Arabia and found that the important factors were the limited number of internet service
providers; and the high cost of their services which offered variation in connectivity and
bandwidth. The second influencing factor was the difference in institutional policies from
one organization to another, where the policy of one in the southern region differed from
another in the northern region, due to the situation where each region had a completely



                                            41
different ICT infrastructure. The other factor revealed by this study was that the King
Abdul Aziz City for Science and Technology (KACST) and the Saudi Telecom Company
(STC) monopolized the process of developing and building the technical infrastructure.
This was unlike what happens in some technologically advanced countries such as the
United Kingdom which has a general government policy implemented by bodies such as
the Joint Information Systems Committee (JISC).

The International Telecommunication Union (ITU, 2020) report revealed that the
Kingdom of Saudi Arabia was one of the most successful countries in the world at
harnessing the ICT infrastructure to confront the Covid 19 pandemic, and stressed that
Saudi Arabia's interest in ICT infrastructure and its keenness to adopt technology had
enabled it to deal with this pandemic based on its strong infrastructure. The report also
showed that this solid ICT infrastructure meant that the average per person data
consumption in Saudi Arabia (900 MB per day) massively exceeded the global average,
which is estimated at 200 MB per day. In fact, handling this huge amount of data could
not have been achieved without the presence of a strong ICT infrastructure and great
determination by the Saudi government, who increased the frequency spectrum allocated
to telecom companies from 340 MHz in 2017 to 1110 MHz in 2020, an increase of 226%
(ITU, 2020). Moreover, the ITU report reveals that the Saudi private sector has benefited
from the current development of the ICT infrastructure. For example, in 2020, the number
of Saudi delivery agents using a delivery app increased by 500%. It can be concluded that
there is a very big difference between the ICT infrastructure of the Kingdom of Saudi
Arabia 15 years ago and the current infrastructure, and this is an influential factor in the
adoption of modern technologies especially the adoption of cloud computing services by
SMEs.

Recently, the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, as a developing country, has been working hard
towards developing its technical capabilities and investing in ICT infrastructure to match
the existing technical systems and infrastructure in developed countries (Almalki &
Williams, 2012). Today, there is a great visible development on the ground, as the Saudi
government has worked to establish many initiatives within its Vision 2030 to develop its
technical capabilities and has established numbers of new government authorities such as
the Data and Artificial Intelligence Authority, which will be discussed in detail later, and




                                            42
the Digital Government Authority, which are all concerned with developing the field of
technology in general.

What was discussed above shows the importance of ICT infrastructure and its powerful
role in making the adoption of cloud computing services possible, as it has become the
backbone for employing any modern and new technologies, especially in the current
information technology revolution. Pradhan et al. (2018) explained that the economic
growth movement index has become linked to ICT infrastructure, especially in the current
era of the internet and mobile communications. At a time when commerce, industry and
support services are operating in a highly competitive environment, they require a strong
ICT infrastructure to meet increases in demand and make quick and appropriate decisions;
and it is this infrastructure that enables economies to change from a traditional form to a
digital one (Ángel Bañares & Altmann, 2018). Therefore, in developed countries,
governments have given ICT infrastructure priority, due to their importance in the process
of delivering services, whether in the public or private sectors; and this reflects positively
on the rest of the services provided because they have become completely dependent on
technology. In contrast, in developing countries, some governments still suffer from the
lack of ICT infrastructure that can be relied upon to provide services to all beneficiaries,
and the COVID-19 pandemic has proven this very well.

2.4.8 National Information Center (NIC)
In 1979, the Saudi government established the National Information Center (NIC), one
of the largest information centres in the Middle East, under the name of the Computer
Center, which was then changed to the General Administration of Central Information
(NIC, 2015). The current name was taken in 1982 to highlight the centre’s ultimate goal,
for which it was established, which is to collect information about the citizens and
residents in Saudi Arabia in a single centre that can provide its services in an easy and
flexible way (NIC, 2015).

The centre provides technical solutions and technological services for all public and
private sectors of the state, especially since it is now linked to all services in the Kingdom
of Saudi Arabia, and provides direct access to any government platform or private sector
linked to it (Alannsary & Hausawi, 2019). Therefore, the centre has become the most
powerful information provider in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia due to the amount of



                                             43
information it possesses about all the beneficiaries of technical services. It also owns the
famous Absher platform, which enables citizens and residents to submit, follow up and
complete their transactions 100% electronically without the need to visit the government
office in question (Alannsary & Hausawi, 2019).

2.4.9 Saudi Data & Artificial Intelligence Authority (SDAIA)
In 2019, the government of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia established the Saudi Data and
Artificial Intelligence Authority (SDAIA) in order to support and achieve Vision 2030
with the aim of regulating the data field and accelerating the growth in the field of data
by supporting innovation through the three arms of the SDAIA, which are the National
Information Center (NIC), the National Data Management Office (NDMO), and the
National Center for Artificial Intelligence (NCAI). Furthermore, the SDAIA is concerned
with several main points that represent its supreme mission to contribute to the national
wealth by collecting a huge amount of data, defining the strategic direction of that data,
supervising data governance and providing the possible tools related to it (SDAIA, 2021).

The National Data Bank was also established by the SDAIA to support the integration of
all government sectors in addition to the cloud computing platform (DEEM Cloud), which
is the largest cloud of its kind in the Middle East, to provide innovative technical solutions
to support entities towards digital transformation (Albogami et al., 2021). In fact, DEEM
Cloud helped reduce government spending on managing, protecting, operating and
maintaining technical infrastructure while ensuring high protection for all data by
providing the necessary modern technologies. As more than 150 data centres have been
hosted and integrated in the government cloud (GOV.SA, 2021), with the expectation that
this secure government cloud will provide services to the private sector. The private sector
will therefore benefit from the experience of the public sector in adopting cloud
computing services.

2.4.10 The Internet
Internet infrastructure is a fundamental requirement for the success of any modern digital
technology, and especially for the adoption of cloud computing services, whose services
are completely based on the Internet. In 1994, the Internet was officially permitted to be
used by the Saudi educational and research sector, and all users were able to use the
Internet for the first time in 1999 (Simsim, 2011). The number of users at the end of 2000


                                             44
reached 200,000, and the number increased steadily until it reached 27 million users in
2018 (CITC, 2021). Moreover, based on the data published by CITC in 2005, there were
3 million users (13% of the Saudi population), and this had increased to 9.3 million users
(36%) by 2008; until by 2020, 97.8% percent of the total Saudi population were reported
as using the Internet, taking into account users with more than one internet line (see Figure
2.13). Therefore, the increase in the number of internet users in Saudi Arabia, where this
research is conducted, is considered a positive factor, as the greater the number of internet
users and beneficiaries of its services, the greater their confidence in the services provided
through the Internet. Arguably, this high use will also mean a greater desire to adopt
services based on the Internet such as cloud computing services.

Alshehri and Meziane (2017) conducted a study to measure the current state of the growth
of internet use in Saudi Arabia and its contribution to the adoption of internet-based
services. The study showed that 44% of the population started using the Internet after
2006, while at that time the majority of people in developed countries used the Internet
and benefited from internet-based services. The authors also commented that those who
used the Internet late might be more hesitant in using new services and dealing with them
with confidence; this included sharing personal details, bank account information and
other sensitive information when dealing with internet-based services such as cloud
computing.

However, the Internet has become an essential component of modern Saudi society, and
has become integral to many aspects of life such as education, healthcare, the economy,
media and social communication, and the Saudi government is making great efforts to
develop internet services in all regions and is working to attract many internet service
providers (ISPs). The first ISP was the Saudi Telecom Company (STC), and in 2005, the
Mobily Telecommunications Company announced its entry to the Saudi market as an ISP
and became the second competitor, followed by the Zain Telecommunications Company
in 2008 (Alshahrani, 2016). The relationship between the number of ISPs and the quality
of service is positive, because whenever there is more than one ISP, the service is more
likely to be competitive, distinctive and at a reasonable price.




                                             45
Figure 2-13: Growth of Internet users in Saudi Arabia (drawn by the author using
                            the data by CITC, 2021)



    2.5 The Most Important Challenges Facing SMEs in Saudi
          Arabia Regarding the Adoption of Cloud Computing
          Services
Cloud computing is a new technology that brings with it a lot of technological facilities
for the business sector. Although there is worldwide attention, especially in the private
sectors, on gaining precedence through adopting new electronic services such as cloud
computing, there are still some who do not trust this rapid transformation. In developing
countries, the adoption of cloud computing services is a controversial issue that requires
deep research to find out exactly which obstacles are preventing the adoption of cloud
computing services by SMEs, and how these can be overcome in the future.

2.5.1 Confidentiality
The fact that cloud computing technology allows its users to remotely deal with, store,
view and access data through the Internet has made confidentiality one of the obstacles
facing the adoption of cloud computing services, as these services depend on abandoning
the possession of physical data. There should be ways and mechanisms to ensure the
confidentiality of data, which take into account that the more modern data encryption


                                           46
methods ensure data are untouchable before outsourcing them to cloud computing
(Sabeeh Mahmood et al., 2019). Alsafi and Fan (2020) conducted a study on the extent
to which cloud computing is being adopted by SME manufacturing in the Kingdom of
Saudi Arabia and showed that concerns about data confidentiality were considered one
of the main barriers resulting from the cloud service provider’s inefficiency in protecting
customers’ data. Therefore, SMEs' confidence in the confidentiality of data is still shaky,
as a result of their fears that their personal data may be passed to their competitors through
service providers, whether intentionally or unintentionally.

2.5.2 Integrity
Data have become an essential and highly dependable part of business conducted in
enterprises that store them in traditional technical infrastructures. Undoubtedly, data are
considered as a valuable and sensitive asset that cannot be neglected. Many cloud users
have been late adopters as a result of their fear of loss of data integrity and of putting their
sensitive data into the servers of cloud computing service providers (Al-Ruithe et al.,
2018a). Additionally, (Srivastava & Kumar, 2014) examined all the cloud consumers’
concerns related to data and showed that their biggest concern was the lack of integrity
for sensitive data, and how integrity was managed and preserved. However, integrity is
essential in any business where continuity is needed, integrity in cloud computing requires
that data be complete, accurate, consistent and in context. Thus, SMEs have had problems
with leaks from their customers' data, which are then sold on the black market, from their
traditional systems, and believe that adopting cloud computing services increases
complexity and therefore loses them data integrity (Srivastava & Kumar, 2014).

2.5.3 Availability
Availability is a major concern for many enterprises wishing to adopt cloud computing
services, as the service provider’s connection or power outage affects the operation of the
service for the consumer. Neamtiu and Dumitraş (2011) defined availability as the
“fraction of time that a system is ready to provide correct service and does not experience
planned or unplanned downtime”. Additionally, most researchers define availability
based on SLAs, and because of these definitions, the term ‘availability’ in cloud
computing can be defined as “probability of providing service according to defined
requirements”(Undheim et al., 2011). Whenever the availability of the system is visible



                                              47
and tangible by users through their use of the services provided, it is possible to measure
the availability of the service as:

              Service Availability = Service Uptime/ (Service Uptime + Service Outage)

‘Service uptime’ means the period when the system is providing the required service
continuously, while ‘service outage’ (or as some people called it, ‘downtime’) means the
period when the system is not ready to provide the service, or does not respond to the
service (Toeroe & Tam, 2012). Therefore, many enterprises believe that the provision of
cloud computing services by cloud service providers puts them under the management of
third parties that control the availability of services to many enterprises, and this may
cause a preference for one company over another, which may result in a service outage.

2.5.4 Security
Security is a worrying issue for many enterprises that intend to adopt cloud computing
services but fear loss or corruption of their data, especially with the spread of hacking.
Some of SMEs in Saudi Arabia are concerned about the security of cloud computing, due
to several reasons, the most important of which is the lack of reliability in the security of
cloud computing services due to its recent history in the world of technologies. In fact,
security concerns in cloud computing differ from those in traditional systems for specific
reasons, the most important of which is that the pooling of resources allows users to use
the same resources based on multi-tenancy technology (Ali et al., 2015). The same
authors mentioned that multi-tenancy technology can cause operations to be tracked by
other users and exposes data to visibility risks. Selecting a convenient and secure cloud
infrastructure is not as easy as it might be expected, the choice must first be made based
on the providers' terms of service including all aspects related to security such as location,
connectivity, restrictions and sharing (Ramgovind et al., 2010). Accordingly, data
security has become a major concern for a number of SMEs, which affects their decision
to adopt cloud computing services, especially with the increased hacking from hostile
countries that target the private as well as the public sectors in Saudi Arabia (see details
in chapters 5 and 7).




                                             48
2.5.5 The Regulation of Organizations and Organizational Policy
There are some factors that can be said to have a strong influence in hindering the
adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs, one of which is certainly the regulation
of enterprises. According to Amini and Bakri (2015), existing regulations and laws must
have an impact on organisational policy and the adoption of new technologies. Updating
policy in enterprises is not an easy thing to do, as it goes through several stages until it is
finally approved. However, in most countries, the enterprises’ regulations and policies
are derived from the general regulations of the state such as those on importation, supply,
communications, etc. That is why some enterprises face obstacles in adopting new
policies unless they are part of the general state regulations, and this applies to SMEs in
the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia.

Alsafi and Fan (2020) conducted a study about the barriers to the adoption of cloud
computing service by SMEs in Saudi Arabia and found state regulations to be one of
them. One of the participants in that study, an IT manager in a Riyadh-based SME was
frustrated and said that enterprises could not store their data outside Saudi Arabia due to
the strict government regulations which prevented enterprises from making choices in
this matter. In the past, the Saudi government used to store its citizens’ and residents’ data
in data centres outside the country, but recently, in order to preserve the data, the Saudi
government issued directives to transfer all that data to inside Saudi Arabia.

2.5.6 Size of Organization
The size of organizations is an influencing factor on the adoption of cloud computing
services in developing countries, due to several reasons, the most important of which is
the amount of the income and the ability to compete with other, larger organisations (Skafi
et al., 2020a). The size of the enterprise is measured by the size of its investments, the
number of its employees and its annual revenue. One study on cloud computing and the
risks related to it, confirmed that large enterprises are always better able to adopt cloud
computing services than small enterprises (Brender & Markov, 2013). Big enterprises are
more able to take the risk of adopting any new services, as they are more likely to have
the resources to boost their position (Thiesse et al., 2011; Zhu et al., 2006). Moreover,
these previous studies agreed that the size of the enterprise has an impact on the adoption
of cloud computing services. The larger the size of the enterprise, the more receptive it is
to adoption.


                                              49
2.5.7 IT Readiness
Information technology readiness is another way to measure the possibility of an
organization adopting new technical services, in particular cloud computing services. An
organization's technical readiness can be determined by its ICT infrastructure, human
resources and technical knowledge. Colby and Parasuraman (2001) defined technology
readiness as “people’s propensity to embrace and to use new technologies for
accomplishing goals in home life and at the workplace”. SMEs are no exception, and may
want to adopt new technologies but are sometimes delayed by their own technology
policies and the costs of adopting new services. Haug et al., (2011), discuss SME's IT
readiness from a managerial perspective, where they concluded that the top management
should stand by the technical management, meets its requirements, and take its
suggestions and opinions into consideration if they wanted to ensure a strong technical
readiness. Most enterprises set up departments and employees that plan for their financial
profits; on the other hand, some neglect or pay little attention to their IT issues, which
may be the reason that they are late adopters of cloud computing services.

2.5.8 Trust
One of the most significant influential factors in the adoption of cloud computing services
is trust. The lack of transparency in dealing with data and their primary source, and the
loss of control over data, is what raises enterprises’ concerns about adopting cloud
services which they do not completely trust. Thus, from the clients’ point of view, trust
is the level of confidence they have in their cloud provider (Ko et al., 2011). A study with
236 participants, conducted in the United States of America from the user's perspective,
revealed that 74% of participants did not have trust in cloud computing services, as the
government had not been able to enact strict laws that protected their data (Horvath &
Agrawal, 2015). SMEs are an integral component of a country’s economy and potential
end users of cloud computing services. A prime recent concern of SMEs is to enhance
trust among customers by selecting sustainable, trustworthy cloud services, and studying
the actual needs of these customers in the process of facilitating the adoption of cloud
computing services.




                                            50
2.5.9 Awareness
The lack of awareness of cloud computing services among many SMEs greatly affects
the utilization of those services, and make it a significant barrier to the adoption of cloud
computing services. A large group of those who work in the field of business need to
understand the role of cloud computing services; however, the majority of them are not
yet aware of the dynamics of cloud computing services (Raza et al., 2015a). Moreover,
in a study by Basahel et al. (2016), 45 SMEs in Jeddah, Saudi Arabia acknowledged that
there was a lack of awareness about cloud computing services among their enterprises.
Significant numbers of SMEs in Saudi Arabia may still be hesitating to adopt cloud
computing services due to their lack of awareness and lack of understanding about cloud
computing services, which delays them from reaping the benefits of these new technology
services.

2.5.10 Culture
The prevailing culture in most developing countries from the perspective of customers is
a preference for performing their transactions, business, etc. by face-to-face interactions.
As a result, some Saudi SMEs may want to retain these traditional customers in addition
to having a traditional organisational culture which prefers not to radically change their
ways of dealing, whether they have a traditional electronic system or a traditional manual
method. Leung et al. (2005) defined culture as “beliefs, values, habits, norms and
behavioural patterns of a group of people in a community”. Alkhater et al. (2018)
revealed that the culture in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia has a significant impact on the
adoption of cloud computing services by the private sector, and that the organizations'
decision to adopt cloud computing services is negatively and indirectly affected by that
culture. Thus, due to the COVID-19 pandemic that hit the world, populations in the
Kingdom of Saudi Arabia had only one option to benefit from the services of SMEs,
which was to use electronic services. Indeed, everyone adapted to electronic services in a
short time, and it can be considered a positive factor that the beneficiaries have the ability
to accept change.

2.5.11 Physical Location
Physical location is always one of the challenges that SMEs face when they want to adopt
cloud computing services. When data is stored by cloud computing service providers in



                                             51
unknown locations or outside the enterprises' country, this raises the concern of many
parties who want to adopt the Cloud. In developing countries, cloud computing service
providers suffer simultaneously from poor infrastructure and high costs of storage, so they
are sometimes forced to store data overseas (Fortino et al., 2018). However, the policy
and rules included in the SLA that is conducted between the service provider and the
beneficiary ensure users have confidence in the service provider by controlling the
physical location of the data (Chen et al., 2017; Lopes, 2017). SMEs may have
considerable trouble in dealing with the huge amounts of customer data which they have
in their traditional system, and when deciding whether to adopt cloud computing services
they are very concerned about the physical location of that data in order to make sure
those data are safe from any malicious activities.


    2.6 Benefits of Adoption of Cloud Computing Services in
           SMEs in Saudi Arabia
There are many benefits available to SMEs through the adoption of cloud computing
services; as cloud computing is an innovative technology that helps organizations to
streamline their computing operations. The benefits of adopting cloud computing services
by SMEs are numerous, but this section will discuss the most important of them which
are cost reduction, agility, scalability, availability, security, recovery, system integration,
pay-per-use, flexibility, accessibility and accuracy.

2.6.1 Cost Reduction
Cost reduction is one of the most important benefits of adopting cloud computing services
which reduce the costs by eliminating traditional data centres and their capital
expenditures and replacing them with operating expenditures through pay-per-use
services. Many enterprises are looking for alternative solutions to the traditional ICT
infrastructure and its high cost in order to focus on the core of the business (Alshamaileh,
2013). Thus, the adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs gives them the
opportunity to expand or reduce the scope of the service based on their business needs,
which contributes significantly to saving a lot of costs.




                                              52
2.6.2   Agility

Cloud computing services give enterprises the computational agility they need to respond
to changing customer demands, and the more agile the enterprises are in accepting
changes, in particular the technological ones, the more this is reflected in their internal
and external operations (Kim et al., 2009; Marston et al., 2011). Agility is therefore one
of the factors that can positively affect SMEs' business, and may influence their adoption
of cloud computing services because these services contribute to accelerating business
and adapt very quickly to changes. As. Khayer et al. (2020) showed, adopting cloud
computing services impacts positively on organizational agility, which can be divided
into: customer agility, operational agility and partnering agility. Customer agility works
proactively as a response to consumer needs by offering new items, and boosts income
and competitiveness (Roberts & Grover, 2012). Operational agility is known for
increasing operational flexibility, lowering costs and increasing customer retention
(Mikalef & Pateli, 2017); and partnering agility can be achieved by forming a strategic
network of trusted virtual relationships with partners (Sambamurthy et al., 2003).
Therefore, agility is one of the most important factors, and plays a vital role in influencing
SMEs to adopt cloud computing services.

2.6.3 Scalability
Cloud computing is characterized by being highly scalable in hosting systems in a way
that provides the appropriate environment for the user. Cloud computing, in comparison
to other IT systems, is becoming a more important trend in information and
communication technologies, with the potential to improve IT system dependability and
scalability (Alshamaileh, 2013). Scalability is a tool that is measurable in terms of the
numbers of requests that a cloud computing system can handle at the same time. In
addition, when examining the relationship between cost and system efficiency, scalability
is critical. Lehrig et al. (2015) defined scalability as “the ability of a service to increase
its capacity by consuming more resources in the resource space”. In developing
countries, such as the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, there is a rapid population and economic
growth, and this makes the systems require continuous scalability, which contributes to
the process of abandoning traditional systems and adopting cloud computing services.




                                             53
2.6.4 Availability
The adoption of cloud computing is also influenced by the computing services being
available anytime and anywhere. Availability is considered a key requirement for any
enterprise that wants to provide its services continuously and without delay. Availability
of cloud computing services increases customer confidence about systems and protects
revenues from losses on the one hand, while on the other hand, computing service
providers are keen to achieve quality of service (QoS) based on the SLA and prefer to
exploit all the systems to achieve good profits (Leslie et al., 2015; Mesbahi et al., 2018).
Moreover, the adoption of cloud services solves many of the problems of traditional
systems, perhaps the most prominent of which is when a system hangs or freezes, which
results in unavailability. Cloud computing service providers operate their hosted systems
through multiple data centres, which are located in more than one location, which in turn
ensures that the service is available and does not depend on a single centre.

2.6.5 Processing and Security
Cloud computing adoption by SMEs enhances the processing and security of systems in
the provision of service to their partners and customers. Processing and security in cloud
computing are provided differently to how they are done in traditional systems, in that
operations can be processed without installing special programs, and only happen through
the Internet on an allocated space in the cloud with a service provider who guarantees
high security (Ajith Singh et al., 2012).

Cloud computing cannot be assured as totally risk-free, as they are systems that operate
through the Internet and are therefore vulnerable; but what distinguishes them is that they
are subject to strong security standards that the service provider is keen to achieve due to
their commitment to SLA; also, that security is an advantage that service providers
compete for to win customers (Carroll et al., 2011). The adoption of cloud computing by
SMEs saves a lot of time, effort and money that would be spent in maintaining the security
of systems and processing operations in traditional systems, for example, it is possible to
dispense with protection software that are an annual cost for businesses.

2.6.6 Disaster Recovery
The ability of cloud computing to deal with disaster recovery (DR) and with out-of-
control problems in recovering lost data, is a valuable advantage for SMEs, and a


                                            54
significant factor in their adoption of cloud computing services. The dedicated
infrastructure and the shared approach are two separate strategies of disaster recovery
(DR) that limit the occurrence of network outages (Chang, 2015). Because a dedicated
infrastructure is committed to a single client, the cost and speed are high. The shared
approach involves having an assigned infrastructure for a large number of users, which
saves costs and reduces the speed of recovery (Khoshkholghi et al., 2014). A study on
disaster recovery and the adoption of cloud computing services conducted by Tamimi et
al. (2019), found that 2% of users lost data due to natural disasters such as earthquakes,
fires, etc. Due to the mechanism employed by CSPs to establish cloud backups in more
than one server, cloud computing services have distanced themselves from traditional
systems in terms of recovery, as data and systems may be restored, regardless of disasters.

2.6.7 System Integration
The adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs removes much of the burden from
these enterprises to monitor systems management and integration. One of the most
important approaches offered by cloud computing services is data and systems
integration, as current clouds have enabled users to benefit from the integration of
massive volumes of data and systems (Linthicum, 2017). Systems integration via cloud
computing is one of the solutions that will eliminate data redundancy that occurs in
traditional systems when integrating. However, the recent emergence of the integration
of cloud computing with the Internet of Things (IoT) has formed a new field called the
Cloud of Things (CoT), which is supported by cloud computing services through an
integration that does data processing instead of lightweight devices (Aazam et al., 2014).
Therefore, system integration gives data to authorized users on demand from anywhere
and at any time, and as it provides an integrated platform for businesses when they need
to collaborate.

2.6.8 Pay-Per-Use
This feature means dispensing with traditional technical infrastructure resources and
replacing them with cloud computing resources as needed. Pay-per-use frees enterprises,
whether they are large or SMEs, from building technological infrastructure and its high
costs by enabling them to pay for the use of resources provided by CSPs only when
required. Cloud computing service customers can use computing resources as they need
and pay for use through the application programming interfaces (APIs) (Malik et al.,


                                            55
2018). Using cloud computing resources has become like using utility services such as
electricity, water, gas, which are available upon request with payment-for-use (Buyya et
al., 2009). Furthermore, this is a significant economic factor that reduces the high cost of
building a traditional infrastructure and also removes the burden of supervision, follow-
up and maintenance.

2.6.9 Flexibility
The adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs creates flexibility in their systems’
environment and helps in the exploitation of resources at all levels. Cloud computing
provides flexibility to customers through CSPs who in turn offer many different service
models in addition to providing cost-effective price packages that suit the user's budget
and needs at the same time (Chase et al., 2014). By adopting cloud computing services,
SMEs are not tied to using a traditional IT infrastructure and can become more flexible
and thus more adventurous in their enterprises (Lal & Bharadwaj, 2016). The expansion of
trade at all levels and its dependence on electronic systems, may generate a large demand
that will result in large orders and many customers, which requires systems that are
flexible and meet the needs of their owners on demand without delay. The adoption of
cloud computing services is the solution to that continued rapid expansion.

2.6.10 Accessibility
Accessibility is an effective feature that supports the adoption of cloud computing through
making access to data very easy for authorized users from anywhere and anytime. Cloud
computing services provide data access via the Internet and any form of Internet-capable
electronic device, such as a laptop, smartphone, iPad, PC or other similar device (Mell &
Grance, 2011; Wang et al., 2010). However, because access to data in the Cloud is fully
reliant on the Internet, the presence of a fast and high-bandwidth Internet plays a crucial
role in positively influencing enterprises’ decisions to employ cloud computing services.
Furthermore, cloud computing services support mobile accessibility, which is especially
important as the use of mobile smart devices is rapidly growing, and are becoming
increasingly used to make transactions, especially by the younger generation (see Figure
2.14). Therefore, as one of the most important goals of SMEs is to maintain customers by
achieving their desires and obtaining their satisfaction; the adoption of cloud computing
services by SMEs is the solution towards achieving the goal because customers prefer
easy access to sources.


                                            56
Figure 2-14: Global smartphone ownership (drawn by the author using the data by
                 Smartphone Market Share 2021, Statista, 2021)



    2.7 Comparison of Relevant Studies
This section reviews the implementation and adoption of cloud computing services by
SMEs in developed and developing countries. It begins by discussing and investigating
the reasons and factors that helped SMEs in developed countries to replace traditional
systems with the adoption of cloud computing services, then provides a review of the
experiences of developing countries that are similar to the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia in
terms of infrastructure, environment, culture, religion, etc. This section also reviews and
explores the current state of SMEs in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, which is the context
of this research, exploring and identifying the barriers and challenges facing the adoption
of cloud computing services. Additional to the context of Saudi SMEs, this section
investigates to what extent is there an impact due to the adoption of cloud computing
services, and the most influential factors that impact the adoption of these services.

2.7.1 Relevant Studies from Developed Countries
In 2018, the adoption of cloud computing services by Australian SMEs was explored in
a survey conducted by Senarathna et al., and the results demonstrated a positive and
strong association between cloud awareness and cloud adoption. There was also a
favourable impact on adoption made by good service quality and the Cloud's relative



                                            57
advantage. Cloud security, privacy and resilience did not play a substantial role in the
typical outcomes; however, the size of the organisation played a role in intention to adopt
cloud computing services (Senarathna et al., 2018a).

In 2013, a survey investigated the adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs in the
UK using 300 UK SMEs as the study sample (Sahandi et al., 2013). Only 169 out of 300
SMEs in the UK provided valid responses, and those responses were for businesses with
fewer than 250 employees. After analyzing the data, it became obvious that using cloud
computing services helped to reduce the cost ratio by 45.5 %. Cloud computing services
had a spreading rate of 38.9 % and offered a lot of flexibility. While computer capacity
increased by 32.9 %, the access to apps increased by 44.9 %. The adoption of cloud
computing services resulted in a 31.7 % boost in IT efficiency (Sahandi et al., 2013).
According to the findings also, 32.5 % of SMEs had a plan in place to adopt cloud
computing services, while 27 % did not. On the other hand, 20.2 % of SMEs stated that
their vision was unclear and they didn't know whether or not to adopt cloud computing,
17.8 % said they expected to use cloud computing with new future firms.

The United States of America is a highly developed countries and a leader in the
revolution of modern technologies, especially cloud computing. The top three largest
manufacturers and providers of cloud computing services around the world are American
companies: Amazon Web Services (AWS), Google Cloud and Microsoft Azure, whose
spending on their infrastructure in the second quarter of 2021 amounted to 61% of the
total spending of cloud computing service providers around the world (US $47 billion)
(canalys, 2021).

Regarding the issue of organisational size, Klug and Bai, (2015) conducted a study
looking at the factors supporting the adoption of cloud computing in organizations,
whether governmental or private, in the United States of America and Canada. The study
found that large organizations often had a slow and complex process of adopting cloud
computing services and products, because they possessed sufficient human and financial
resources that qualified them to study and evaluate products and request products of
greater complexity, such as online conferencing tools, self-education tools, etc. Small
organizations however were often found to be keen to more speedily adopt cloud
computing services because they were looking for only one solution, not a group of




                                            58
solutions, for example, e-mail as an internal tool in the organization that manages the
communication process between workers through cloud computing.

Hamburg and Bucksch (2016) participated in the European project IN-CLOUD with a
scientific paper to demonstrate how cloud computing services could support the growth
of small and medium enterprises in Europe. The study included six European countries:
Germany, Greece, Italy, Portugal, Spain and the United Kingdom, and revealed that
SMEs that adopted cloud computing services benefited from adoption in terms of
scalability, flexibility and effective cost-reduction, as these three factors were ranked as
the top motivators, along with other motivating factors such as maintenance by the service
provider and improved service delivery, etc. In addition, the study looked at the economic
impact of cloud computing on a variety of SMEs and discovered that the impact varies
by sector and region, with the IT sector reaping the largest benefits.

Therefore, after reviewing these studies which were located in developed countries, it can
be concluded that developed countries have acquired technical capabilities at an early
stage, and worked to improve their infrastructure and keep pace with modern
technologies, making them ready to adopt new services such as cloud computing services.
Today, cloud computing services have made it possible for developing countries to
benefit from the capabilities and solid infrastructures of developed countries, and to have
access to the same ICT infrastructure, data centres and applications through the adoption
of cloud computing services.

2.7.2 Relevant Studies from Developing Countries
In 2017, the Dubai Silicon Oasis Authority (DSOA) conducted a survey among 100 start-
up SMEs in Dubai and found that 70 percent of start-ups had adopted cloud computing
services (DTEC, 2017). In addition, the report indicated that all sectors in the United Arab
Emirates (UAE) took the initiative towards a complete digital transformation without
hesitation. One of these initiatives was the transformation of the SME sector by thinking
about computing strategies and then adopting cloud computing services, but as the report
stated, that there was still a small percentage in the sector that was slow to adopt cloud
computing services due to concerns about data protection and security.

On the other hand, Alsharari et al. (2020) used a qualitative method (interviews) to
investigate the extent to which SMEs in the UAE were adopting cloud computing services


                                            59
through the implementation of cloud enterprise resource planning (ERP). One CEO for
one of the SMEs in the UAE was asked why their company had selected the Cloud ERP
system rather than a conventional one, and the answer was because of its ease of use,
relatively low cost, the convenience of control and management, the flexibility of access
from any web server, and because there were no maintenance costs.

In Lebanon, 139 employees in SMEs from four different regions in Lebanon, including
the capital Beirut, participated in a study that examined the factors influencing the
adoption of cloud computing services. The study's findings then helped to identify six
major barriers to SMEs that intend to adopt cloud computing services which were: lack
of top management support, poor technology experience, a lack of IT infrastructure,
complexity, a lack of government initiatives and security and privacy concerns (Skafi et
al., 2020b). In fact, the weakness of the economy that Lebanon was experiencing and the
problems surrounding it indirectly affected the ICT infrastructure, which had become
very weak. However, the government of Lebanon finally noticed this weakness and
announced a five-year plan that began in 2020 aimed at renewing the ICT infrastructure
and implementing a transition to fibre-optic networks in addition to 5G.

Bahrain has become one of the developing countries in the MENA that is working
continuously and at an accelerating pace to adopt modern technologies, especially cloud
computing. In 2021, the Ministry of Industry, Commerce and Tourism in Bahrain
announced its partnership with the Bahrain Association for the Development of Small
and Medium Enterprises to train more than 1,000 SMEs on AWS cloud computing (BNA,
2021). Accordingly, this partnership shows the concern of the government towards cloud
computing and its aim to raise awareness of cloud computing and provide SMEs with the
necessary skills to manage their businesses using cloud computing services.

However, there are still concerns about information security in the event of the transition
from conventional systems to cloud computing, and this raises the question of whether
the transformation to cloud services will solve the current challenges with the
conventional systems or will increase them. Awad (2020) examined these issues and
found that the adoption of cloud computing services produces cloud systems that are free
from vulnerabilities and cybersecurity breaches that exist in the local systems of Bahraini
organizations. Therefore, the Bahraini government pays great attention to modern
technologies and the shift to adopting cloud computing services; and it ranked 38th


                                            60
globally and 5th in Asia on implementing e-government (United Nations, 2020). Since
most of these services are completely dependent on cloud computing, this will positively
reflect on the business sector, especially SMEs, who will want to benefit from government
experiences and adopt cloud computing for the implementation of their business
processes.

2.7.3 Relevant Studies from Saudi Arabia
The Kingdom of Saudi Arabia is one of the leading countries in the Middle East in
supporting ICT sectors; as well one of the developing countries that, according to the
international Trade administration report, have spent billions of riyals on ICT
infrastructure from an early stage (Trade, 2019). However, the adoption of cloud
computing services seems to have not received abundant attention until 2020, when the
Ministry of Communications and Information Technology (MCIT) officially announced
the strategy of Saudi Arabia to adopt cloud computing. This strategy is called the ‘Cloud
First for Saudi Arabia’ policy, and it follows the NIST approach and definition of cloud
computing (MCIT, 2020). Then, in mid-2021, the CITC announced its support and
empowerment of cloud computing service providers in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia in
order to provide suitable cloud services to all private sector enterprises and individuals,
except for the financial sector and government agencies.

Alsafi and Fan (2020) conducted semi-structured interviews with 16 Information
Technology (IT) managers from SMEs in Saudi Arabia to investigate the challenges that
have prevented enterprises from adopting cloud computing services. The results revealed
that security was considered the most significant challenge, with 14 out of 16 respondents
agreeing that it was the biggest obstacle for SMEs towards adopting cloud computing
services. In addition, among those participants, a CEO of one of the SMEs in Jeddah said
that security concerns were the biggest challenge that hindered SMEs and lowered their
interest in cloud computing deployment, and specified that cybersecurity risks and
challenges, such as hacking, had a negative impact on businesses, whether the companies
in question were large corporations or small businesses.

In 2020, a study conducted in Saudi Arabia examined the obstacles and benefits of
adopting cloud computing services by SMEs and found that the benefits of adopting cloud
computing lay mostly in reducing the costs that were spent on traditional systems, and its



                                            61
effective contribution to promoting innovations, accelerating decision-making and
supporting communication with customers (Albelaihi & Khan, 2020a). However, the
majority of the challenges stemmed from security and privacy issues, as well as Saudi
technological culture, which discourages the usage of cloud computing technologies
(Albelaihi & Khan, 2020a). Moreover, investors in Saudi Arabia avoided adopting cloud
computing services because they did not have sufficient information about data centres
and their working mechanisms, as they had no knowledge, other than having some
awareness of challenges faced by cloud computing centres (Albelaihi & Khan, 2020a).
Therefore, the major change that is currently taking place in the Saudi government by
adopting Vision 2030 and its technical programmes under the Vision may improve the
working environment of SMEs in the near future, and the recent improvement in the Saudi
economy and its entry into the G20 countries is a catalyst towards improving the cloud
computing environment in the SME sectors.

Overall, the studies reviewed above cover cloud computing adoption in a range of
developed and developing countries along with relevant studies from Saudi Arabia; thus,
in each context we find a specific knowledge, culture, economy, ICT Infrastructure, and
internet penetration rate. The factors therefore change according to the nature of the
surrounding environment; and although each context needs to be studied separately to
identify the specific factors influencing cloud computing adoption, it is beneficial to gain
an overview from a range of studies and discern any patterns which emerge from such a
review.


    2.8 Research Gaps
The adoption of cloud computing services can serve as a catalyst and driver towards
achieving and facilitating a good business in the private sector especially in SMEs.
Despite previous research making recommendations to decision-makers in SMEs to move
to cloud computing systems, there are still concerns about making this shift. Most of the
previous researchers discussed the barriers faced by SMEs, but they focused on specific
factors and did not include all influencing factors; a number of them focused on security
and privacy concerns, such as (Albelaihi & Khan, 2020b; Alkhater et al., 2018a; Alsafi
& Fan, 2020c; Basahel et al., 2016b). Moreover, the majority of the previous studies
proposed frameworks and models which highlighted the cost of adopting cloud
computing services and its impact on the expenses of the enterprises along with the


                                            62
benefits of shifting to the Cloud without assessing all the factors influencing the adoption
of cloud computing services (e.g., Alshamaileh, 2013; Carroll et al., 2011). However, the
cost factors, security factors, and other factors (e.g., size of the organization, culture,
physical location, etc.) all have an impact on the process of transition from traditional
systems and can influence the adoption of cloud computing services, so to investigate one
factor and neglect the others does not provide the full picture, as it is necessary to research
all the factors. Nevertheless, to the author's knowledge, no previous research has
investigated the whole gamut of factors which may impact on the adoption of cloud
computing services by Saudi SMEs. In addition, cloud computing in Saudi Arabia is still
in its early stages, so the author will make a thorough investigation into all the previously
mentioned influencing factors, and provide a new comprehensive framework for adoption
of cloud computing model that can assist SMEs in Saudi Arabia to make informed
decisions about adopting cloud computing services.

In this research, after the deep investigation in the literature review and finding of the
knowledge gap, the author will attempt to fill that gap by creating a new comprehensive
model for the adoption of cloud computing services to investigate most influential factors
hindering the adoption of cloud services by Saudi SMEs. So, the next chapter will have
that along with providing hypotheses that confirm the predicted association between
cloud computing factors.




                                              63
Chapter
3 Theoretical Framework
    3.1 Introduction
As shown in Figure 3.1, this chapter reviews the theories and models that have been
widely adopted, such as the Technology-Organization-Environment Framework (TOE),
the Technology Acceptance Model (TAM), Unified Theory of Acceptance and Use of
Technology (UTAUT), Institutional Theory, and Diffusion of Innovation Theory (DOI)
to investigate the challenges that face the adoption of new technology, particularly the
adoption of cloud computing services. The most appropriate theories and models for
explaining the barriers to cloud computing adoption by Saudi SMEs are then identified
with a view to finding the most influential factors. Therefore, this research proposes a
new and unique model capable of measuring and identifying the factors affecting the
adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi SMEs.




          Figure 3-1 Stage 3: Developing Research Hypotheses and Model




                                          64
    3.2 Technology-Organisation-Environment (TOE)
          Framework
The TOE Framework was designed and developed by Tornatzky and Fleisher in 1990 to
discover the factors affecting an organization’s adoption of new technological
innovations through three contexts, which are: the Technological context, the
Organisational context, and the Environmental context (see Figure 3.2) (Tornatzky et al.,
1990). The technology context is key, in that the technical factors such as tools and
practices that affect the organization both internally and externally can be measured and
their potential for use in adopting innovative new technologies evaluated. The second
context is the organization itself and how it is managed and dealt with in making its
decisions based on its structure, size and communication process. The context of the
environment refers to the environment in which the organisation operates and is affected
by, such as regulations or market structure and characteristics (Tornatzky et al., 1990).




Figure 3-2: Technology-Organisation-Environment (TOE) Framework (Tornatzky
                                  et al., 1990)



                                           65
The TOE is a framework with a strong theoretical foundation supported by different
practical applications in several fields, in addition to being a comprehensive analytical
framework through which it is possible to predict indicators of the adoption of
technological innovations through three different contexts that may differ from one study
to another (Oliveira & Martins, 2011). In addition, the DOI theory developed by Rogers
in 1995 shares characteristics that appear in the TOE framework’s technological and
organisational contexts; whilst the TOE has the added the environmental dimension
(Oliveira & Martins, 2011). Therefore, it is clear that the three contexts of the TOE
framework mostly cover the main aspects of the life cycle of any organisation which can
impact on the organisation's decision to adopt innovative technologies.

The TOE framework is characterized by several criteria, the first of which is its flexibility
in accepting other influencing factors with the possibility of adding them alongside the
basic factors and investigating them. The second criterion is that the TOE framework has
been shown to be usable and applicable in many different fields, whether technical or
otherwise. The third criterion is that the TOE framework is very effective for measuring
the factors affecting adoption that are both internal and external to the organisation (Baker,
2012). Moreover, TOE Framework offers a theoretical lens with which to investigate the
adoption of new innovative technologies (Zhang et al., 2020). In comparison to other
theories and frameworks, the TOE framework arguably surpasses its peers, as it employs
human and non-human factors in one framework, which gives it the strength to deal with
the accumulated traditions in the organisation's surroundings and predict its future, and
identify the obstacles that prevent the adoption of modern technologies (Awa et al., 2017).
Therefore, after reviewing many studies, the use and application of the TOE framework
emerged as significant in the IS domain and related fields after its success in identifying
key factors affecting technology adoption in different contexts.


    3.3 Technology Acceptance Model (TAM)
The Technology Acceptance Model (TAM) is a framework that was created in 1985 by
Davis to examine the challenges that face adoption of new technologies and that delay
the users’ intentions to accept innovative technologies. The TAM consists of four main
factors, but the two key factors are Perceived Usefulness (PU) and Perceived Ease of Use
(PEU). PU is defined as “the degree to which an individual believes that using a
particular system would enhance his or her job performance” (Davis, 1985). While PEU


                                             66
is defined as “the degree to which an individual believes that using a particular system
would be free of physical and mental effort”(Davis, 1985). These factors work together
to assess the usefulness and ease of use of any technology that impact on the user’s
behaviours and attitudes and affect the intention of the user about adopting new systems
(see Figure 3.3) (Marangunić & Granić, 2015). In fact, the TAM was developed from the
Theory of Reasoned Action (TRA) that was developed to predict the human behaviour,
and to determine the relevance of the two main internal factors already mention above
(Buabeng-Andoh, 2018). However, the TAM is designed to investigate the influences on
the user’s intention whether the final decision is to accept or reject the new technology.

Hundreds of different factors have been added to the TAM, and a study conducted in 2019
which investigated student acceptance of e-learning using the TAM, identified 239
external factors that were collected and investigated in 120 scientific studies. This study
also revealed that eight factors were strongly connected with the TAM, namely:
‘playfulness’, ‘computer self-efficacy’, ‘system quality’, ‘information quality’, ‘content
quality’, ‘accessibility’, ‘enjoyment’, and ‘subjective/social norm’(Salloum et al., 2019).
Therefore, based on the investigation into the external factors that were conducted by the
above researchers, it can be said that the TAM has been used successfully to test the
relationship between human attitudes, beliefs and intentions in relation to computer
systems. In more detail, the TAM model contains two main factors (PU and PEU) to
identify and measure their effect on the user’s intention in order to predict their intention
to adopt new technology; however, there will be other environmental and organisational
factors that substantially affect this intention.




   Figure 3-3: Technology Acceptance Model (TAM) (Davis & Venkatesh, 1996)



                                              67
    3.4 Unified Theory of Acceptance and Use of Technology
          (UTAUT)
The Unified Theory of Acceptance and Use of Technology (UTAUT) created by
Venkatesh et al. in 2003, proposed a model to investigate and analyse the challenges that
hinder users’ adoption of new technology. The UTAUT model consists of eight factors
four of them considered as the core factors: (performance expectancy, effort expectancy,
social influence and facilitating conditions), and four others: (gender, age, experience,
and voluntariness of use) considered as the influencers on the core factors which all have
a potential and direct role in determining users’ acceptance and adoption of new
technology (see Figure 3.4).




    Figure 3-4: Unified Theory of Acceptance and Use of Technology (UTAUT)
                             (Venkatesh et al., 2003)
The following points clarify the meaning of each of the four core factors through
providing definitions (Venkatesh et al., 2003):

   •   Performance Expectancy is “the degree to which an individual believes that using
       the system will help him or her to attain gains in job performance”.



                                           68
   •   Effort Expectancy is “the degree of ease associated with the use of the system”.
   •   Social Influence is “the degree to which an individual perceives that important
       others believe he or she should use the new system”.
   •   Facilitating conditions is “defined as the degree to which an individual believes
       that an organizational and technical infrastructure exists to support use of the
       system”.

As we have seen, the UTAUT model focuses on the behaviours and attitudes of users to
measure their acceptance of the new technology. The theory was created after reviewing
and integrating factors derived from eight previous models namely: TRA, TAM, DOI,
Theory of Planned Behaviour (TPB), PC Utilizations, the Motivational Model,
Innovation Diffusion Theory (IDT) and Social Cognitive Theory (SCT) (Williams et al.,
2015). Therefore, this model paid great attention to user behaviours and their impact on
adopting new technologies, while not taking into account other influencing factors such
as the surrounding environment, the organisation and the technology itself in order to
understand all the related aspects of the adoption process.


    3.5 Institutional Theory
Institutional Theory is defined as: “Multi-faceted, durable, social structures, made up of
symbolic elements, social activities, and material resources” (Scott, 2001), whilst the
term ‘institution’ can be considered as “the formal rule sets” (North, 1990). According
to Institutional Theory, understanding and predicting the adoption of innovative
technologies in businesses requires an understanding of the impact of the environment
and the technical issues that exist in the organisation (Bjorck, 2004). Institutional theory
posits that institutions consist of cultural-cognitive, normative, and regulative aspects
(Scott, 2001) that, in conjunction with related behaviours and assets, offer consistency
and significance to social existence.

Although technology is associated with frequent and rapid social changes, Institutional
Theory focuses on stability and persistence. However, Institutional Theory was
established to support organisations and can be considered as a productivity tool that is
used to analyse the organisation’s needs as well as the environment around it, including
all the relevant social and cultural issues (Richard Scott, 2013). Moreover, after reviewing
the studies related to the Institutional Theory it appears that it shares environmental



                                            69
factors in common with the TOE framework and considers them as the external factors,
such as competitive pressure, that affect the organisation’s decision to adopt. Therefore,
this theory focuses on the external socio-cultural factors, including behaviours and
attitudes that can affect the organization’s adoption of new technologies. As the current
study aims to investigate factors that influence adoption of cloud computing which are
both internal and external to the businesses, Institutional Theory was not incorporated
into the proposed model.


    3.6 Diffusion of Innovation (DOI) Theory
Diffusion of Innovation Theory is a model that was developed by Rogers in 1995 to
investigate and examine the decisions to adopt innovative technologies whether
individually or organisationally (Rogers, 1995). Innovation was defined by Rogers as
“the degree to which an individual or other unit of adoption is relatively earlier in
adopting new ideas than the other members of a system”, while he defined diffusion as
“the process by which an innovation is communicated through certain channels over time
among the members of a social system” (Rogers, 1995).        Adopting a new technology
requires accuracy in decision-making for various reasons, the most important of which is
that modern technologies may be a high-risk investment for the organisation during its
adoption. Therefore, developing a model that verify the applicability of any new
technology will be valuable; and DOI is one of the best examples of models that can
examine the adoption of new technologies like cloud computing (Mohammed et al.,
2017). The DOI model consists of five factors that are considered as coherent and
meaningful attributes which influence the adoption of innovations like new technologies
(Al Ajmi et al., 2017). The five characteristics of innovation have been explained and
defined by Rogers (1995) as follows:

   •   Relative advantage is “the degree to which an innovation is perceived as better
       than the idea it supersedes”.
   •   Compatibility is “the degree to which an innovation is perceived as being
       consistent with the existing values, past experiences, and needs of potential
       adopters”.
   •   Complexity is “the degree to which an innovation is perceived as difficult to
       understand and use”.




                                           70
   •   Trialability is “the degree to which an innovation may be experimented with on a
       limited basis”.
   •   Observability is “the degree to which the results of an innovation are visible to
       others”.

When compared to other adoption models, DOI has a modest explanatory power, in
addition to its prediction process, it is also very concerned with the features and
characteristics of the system (Taherdoost, 2018). After a comprehensive review of the
DOI model, we can notice that the model concentrates significantly on the internal factors
while the adoption of innovative technology in an organisation relies on the decision of
the whole organisation which is affected by external factors. Therefore, from the author's
point of view, using the DOI model with its characteristics enhances the explanation of
the impact of the technological context which makes it complement the technological
context in the TOE model that cares about external factors much more in its other
contexts.


    3.7 Adoption Theories and Frameworks
A variety of adoption theories and frameworks have been established and refined to test
and examine the factors influencing the adoption of current technology in a certain sector.
This research conducted a comprehensive literature review of the papers that related to
the research context to find the theories that have been used to test cloud computing
adoption by both governmental and private organisations. The results of this research
found that most utilised theories which have been used individually or integrate with other
theories are as follows:

   -   Technology Acceptance Model (TAM) (Ali et al., 2018; Arpaci, 2017),
   -   Technology Organisation and Environment (TOE) framework (Low et al., 2011a;
       Tashkandi & Al-Jabri, 2015),
   -   Diffusion of Innovation (DOI) (Al Ajmi et al., 2017; Mohammed et al., 2017),
   -   TAM Integrated with TOE framework (Chiniah et al., 2019; Gangwar et al., 2015;
       Stewart, 2021),
   -   DOI integrated with TOE (Al Mudawi et al., 2019; Alkhalil et al., 2017; Alkhater
       et al., 2014; Hiran & Henten, 2020).




                                            71
After a comprehensive review of the previous studies, it was found that many researchers
successfully blended DOI theory with the TOE framework regarding cloud computing
adoption. Often, they used original form of the model or added factors to investigate a
particular factor hindering the adoption of cloud computing services (Karunagaran et al.,
2019). Therefore, this research aims to employ the DOI theory that was developed by
Rogers in 1995 (Rogers, 2010) along with the TOE framework that was developed by
Tornatzky et al. in 1990 (Tornatzky et al., 1990). Both the DOI theory and TOE
framework are combined in this research by adding a new main construct factor and some
new sub-factors to increase the SMEs’ ‘intention to adopt cloud computing services’.
Combining the two strengthens the factor investigation further. The DOI focuses on
evaluating innovations based on the adopter characteristics or that innovations with the
best advantage that are more likely to be used (Al-Rahmi et al., 2019). The TOE is
consistent with the DOI as it investigates the organisation's intention to adopt cloud
computing services from various aspects that are covered by the technological,
organisational, and environmental contexts (Oliveira et al., 2014a) by adding the new
social construct context.


    3.8 Conceptual Framework
This study has resulted in the development of a model known as the adoption of Cloud
computing model by Saudi SMEs (ACCM-SME) to investigate and analyse the factors
that influence the adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs in Saudi Arabia.
ACCM-SME was developed after careful analysis of the previous studies related to the
adoption of Cloud computing in SMEs. This is in addition to theories and models that
have been widely used in measuring the influence of factors in adopting modern
technologies. ACCM-SME also integrates some of the critical factors that have been
identified by the previous research on DOI theory with the TOE framework to offer a
comprehensive model that guides this research to find the factors that affect the adoption
of cloud computing. The framework then assesses the impacts and makes a contribution
to improving the image of cloud computing by increasing the intention of SMEs in Saudi
Arabia to adopt cloud computing services. This framework will focus on four main
constructs to represent the essence of the cloud computing challenges that will be
investigated in this research (see Figure 3.5):




                                             72
 Figure 3-5: The framework for the adoption of Cloud computing model by Saudi
                             SMEs (ACCM-SME)
The four essential constructs in this research reflect how the independent variables in the
technological, organisational, environmental and social contexts can be sensitive issues
for contemporary SME practices. They have an influence on the Saudi SMEs' adoption
of cloud computing services. The following sub-sections will go over all of the factors
that hinder Saudi SMEs from adopting cloud computing services by discussing each
factor separately with a thorough investigation.

3.8.1 Technological Context
The technological context is concerned with researching in-depth the technical factors
that influence an organization's decision to adopt cloud computing services. Therefore,
the following six factors investigate the most significant elements that impact the
adoption process of cloud computing services in Saudi SMEs.

3.8.1.1 Data Confidentiality

The significance of investigating the confidentiality of cloud computing practices stems
from the potential risks that can occur in the processes involved (AlSudiari & Vasista,
2012b). Relevant concerns have been investigated regarding the possibility of an external


                                            73
parties’ involvement in providing cloud services and having control over the
organisations’ sensitive information. This poses a significant risk to both privacy and
information confidentiality (Lorünser et al., 2015b; Rao, 2016b; Sahmim & Gharsellaoui,
2017b). In fact, most users of traditional systems, whether companies or individuals, have
very serious concerns about switching to cloud computing services regarding the
confidentiality of their information, when it is shared with the third party that manages
the services. This has caused delays in its adoption by many organisations. Perhaps the
creation of new and quality data encryption models will be a supportive solution to
maintain data confidentiality which will positively reflect on organisations in the rapid
