adoption of cloud computing services (El Makkaoui et al., 2019). Therefore, based on the
previous discussion, the hypothesis (H1) is proposed as:

   H1: Increasing the data confidentiality of cloud computing increases the Saudi
SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.1.2 Data Integrity

Data is currently considered to be a precious treasure whose integrity must be maintained,
especially with recently developed and developing countries relying on digital systems
almost entirely. The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) defined data
integrity as “… the property that data has not been altered in an unauthorised manner,
and data integrity covers data in storage, during processing, and while in transit” (NIST,
2021). Data integrity has been studied by various scholars in terms of the possible risks
that might occur in cloud servicing, such as preserving the data, finding effective solutions
to the risks and establishing a sustainable secure work environment (Aldossary & Allen,
2016b; Gaetani et al., 2017b; Kumari & Paul, 2016b; Yu et al., 2016b). The integrity of
both the data and information is a crucial matter, and it should meet the global standards
that ensure that the users’ data is not tampered with. A loss of integrity in cloud computing
services means that the services will no longer be considered viable. Therefore, based on
the previous discussion, hypothesis H2 is proposed as:

   H2: Increasing the data integrity of cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs’
intention to adopt cloud computing services.




                                             74
3.8.1.3 Data Security

Several researchers have discussed the matter of security as the most influential factor in
an organisations’ intention to adopt cloud computing services. Security is important in
any system or organisation wishing to protect their information from theft or destruction.
Data has a life cycle that is organised according to certain stages from creation to
destruction. All of these stages must be highly secured in order to provide a secure
environment for the data lifecycle (Kumar et al., 2018). Data security refers to the means
by which all information stored in the system is protected (Kosseff, 2016). Wahsh and
Dhillon defined data security related to cloud computing as the extent to which the
provider can secure the customers’ information and maintain it (Wahsh & Dhillon, 2015).
A lack of security creates an insecure environment for organisations and raises concerns
about the transition to cloud computing services. Therefore, techniques such as
authentication, authorisation and cryptography should be considered for use in a cloud
computing system to get a high level of data security. Therefore, based on the previous
discussions, hypothesis H3 is proposed as:

   H3: Increasing the data security of cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs’
intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.1.4 Relative Advantages

Relative advantages contribute to changing the negative image of modern technologies in
many enterprises. This results in the recognition of the benefits of new technologies.
Relative advantages have been defined as “…the degree to which an innovation is
perceived as being better than the idea it supersedes” (Rogers, 1995). The relative
advantage supports cloud computing and catalyses it to be used. This is what allows for
the utilisation of pooled resources, improves productivity, lowers costs, boosts sales and
saves time (Alkhater et al., 2018b). When the SMEs perceive the relative advantage of
cloud computing, it will encourage them to adopt cloud computing services in all of their
processes. According to Khayer et al. (2020a), SMEs are more inclined to incorporate
cloud computing in their operations and procedures when they consider it provides a
relative advantage Therefore, it is important to scientifically investigate relative
advantage and its impact on whether or not cloud computing services are adopted by
SMEs. Therefore, based on the previous discussion, hypothesis H4 is proposed as:




                                             75
   H4: The perceived relative advantages of cloud computing have a positive effect
that increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.1.5 Service Cost

The concept of cost is fundamental and must be discussed because the cost is proportional
to the services provided by the cloud service provider (CSP). The ‘service cost’ factor is
a general term that gives a hint as to the meaning of cost but in fact, the cost of services
is impacted by sub-factors such as server cost, software cost, network cost, support and
maintenance cost, power cost, cooling cost, facilities cost and real-estate cost (Li et al.,
2009). All of these costs (carried by the CSP) will result in saving a lot of money and time
for SMEs, thus we can say that in order to achieve a cost reduction, SMEs should use and
adopt cloud computing services (Hentschel et al., 2018). SMEs are always interested in
the actual cost of the transformation process to cloud computing services. Thus, the actual
cost depends on several variables such as the resources that will be used, the period of
use, the basic specifications of the resources used and the geographical location of the
resources used. Therefore, in relation to the previous discussion, hypothesis H5 is
proposed as:

   H5: Decreasing the cost of cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to
adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.1.6 Service Quality

Logically, service quality is a very influential factor when adopting any new services in
any organisation, whether technical or otherwise. This is because it plays a vital role in
the success of the adopted new service. There is a set of studies in the scope of technology
conducted to measure the quality of service through several different criteria. Pham et al.
(2019) discussed the quality of service in e-learning in Vietnam and its impact on student
satisfaction. They found that the quality of the service provided is a factor affecting
student satisfaction, and that there is a positive relationship between them (Pham et al.,
2019). Other researchers have said that the more there is the desire to accept cloud
computing services, the better the quality of the service, keeping in mind that quality of
service has been identified as an influential element in encouraging the management of
enterprises to adopt cloud services (Al Mudawi et al., 2019). Thus, paying attention to the
quality of service in cloud computing pushes organisations forward to adopt its services




                                            76
without delay. Therefore, based on the previous discussion, hypothesis H6 is proposed
as:

      H6: Increasing the service quality of cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs’
intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.2 The Organisational Context
The organisational context requires investigating the most influential factors that
significantly impact the process of adoption of cloud computing services in terms of
management practices and infrastructural factors of the organisation. Therefore, the
following four factors are examined to identify corporate practices that are built on
foundations and concepts that may impede the Saudi SMEs adopting cloud services.

3.8.2.1 Organisation Regulation

This refers to the rules and procedures implemented by businesses in relation to the
adoption of IT innovations (Mohammed et al., 2017). An organisation that wants to adopt
cloud computing services needs unambiguous internal regulations that define and identify
the responsibilities of the organisation regarding the cloud data, keeping in mind that
these regulations are strict regarding privacy laws (Marston et al., 2011). In addition,
external regulations cannot be neglected. There must be education and awareness of the
regulations of the countries in which the data centres of the organisation's cloud
computing service providers are located (Morgan & Conboy, 2013a). This factor is at the
forefront of the challenges facing Saudi SMEs when seeking to move to cloud computing
services due to the current insufficiency of support from the General Authority of SMEs
regarding their cloud data. This means that there must be clear and publicised standards
that support the transformation process. Therefore, in relation to the previous discussion,
hypothesis H7 is proposed as:

      H7: Increasing and updating the organisational regulations increases the Saudi
SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.2.2 Organisation Size

The size of the organisation refers to the size of the investments in that organisation and
its market value, number of employees, the size of its annual returns and the number of
branches. Organisation size is a crucial and influential factor that plays a powerful part in



                                             77
the cloud computing services adoption (Alkhater et al., 2018b). A study conducted in
2017 found that large organisations have a strong desire to adopt a lot of modern
innovations due to the robustness of their foundation and their ability and flexibility to
bear the risks due to the adoption of cloud computing services (Al-Sharafi et al., 2017).
In contrast, another study in the same year found that size of the firm does not
significantly affect the intention to adopt cloud computing services (Loukis et al., 2017).
Nevertheless, SMEs are in need of cloud computing services due to their limited
infrastructure. This is unlike large companies that have more ability and flexibility deal
with any problems. Therefore, based on the previous discussion, hypothesis H8 is
proposed as:

   H8: A smaller organisation size is more likely to increase the Saudi SMEs’ intention
to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.2.3 Technology Readiness

Technology readiness is defined as the level at which the technology infrastructure and
human resources inside the organisation are required to be, in order to facilitate cloud
computing adoption (Senyo et al., 2016). Technology readiness is one of the variables
that can influence the cloud computing services adoption, as organisations that have high
technology readiness would have a better opportunity to adopt cloud computing services
(Gutierrez et al., 2015). Technology readiness measurement is based on the organisation’s
infrastructure and the extent to which it can accept the transition to cloud computing
services. Organisations that have a strong ready technical infrastructure will be positively
encouraged to adopt cloud computing services (Alhammadi et al., 2015). However, SMEs
in Saudi Arabia do not pay enough attention to technical infrastructure as they still treat
it as a secondary issue in business success. This is while using free technical resources
with their employees such as Gmail, Google forms, Google drive, etc.
(Lakshminarayanan et al., 2013) Therefore, in relation to the previous discussion,
hypothesis H9 is proposed as:

   H9: Increasing technology readiness increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt
cloud computing services.




                                            78
3.8.2.4   Top Management Support

The decision makers who impact the adoption of an innovation are referred to as the top
management (Lai et al., 2014). Top management support is a critical factor that has a
significant influence in the process of adopting of cloud computing services. The
conviction of the top management about the advantages of cloud computing contributes
to the potential of cloud computing services adoption (Yigitbasioglu, 2015). The greater
the top management support received, the greater the adoption of cloud computing
services (Alshamaileh, 2013). If the adoption of cloud computing services is supported
by the top management, this will have a big impact on the transformation process.
Conversely, if the top management does not approve of cloud computing, this will be a
serious barrier to adoption. Therefore, based on the previous discussion, hypothesis H10
is proposed as:

    H10: Increasing the top management support of cloud computing increases the
Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.3 The Environmental Context
The environmental context addresses the external factors that potentially impact the
intention of the organisation toward cloud services. Therefore, the following four factors
discuss the most influential elements that may contribute to significantly influence Saudi
SMEs adoption of cloud computing services.

3.8.3.1 Technology Infrastructure

Cloud computing provides its services completely through the Internet (Amairah et al.,
2018). The lack of a good infrastructure thus reflects negatively on the cloud computing
services. Regarding this factor, one study revealed that the providers face problems when
it comes to providing their services to companies that are located in places where the
technical infrastructure is poor and does not carry the necessary provisions for cloud
computing services (Sabi et al., 2016). Any technical system that depends on the Internet
for its work needs a very strong technical infrastructure to ensure the continuity of its
service provision. Based on the literature review regarding information and
communication technology (ICT) infrastructure for adopting cloud computing services,
it was found that in the contemporary era of the Internet and mobile communications, the
current economic growth movement index is related to the ICT infrastructure (Pradhan et



                                           79
al., 2018). The coronavirus pandemic meant that countries that had a good technical
infrastructure for the most part were able to continue their work without interruption,
whether in education or otherwise. Having a good technical infrastructure nationwide
means the presence of renewable and continuous systems that keep pace with the
information technology revolution. Therefore, in relation to the previous discussion,
hypothesis H11 is proposed as:

   H11: Obtaining a high level of the technology infrastructure increases the Saudi
SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.3.2 Cloud Provider

The cloud computing service provider plays a vital role in the success of the process of
adopting cloud computing services as it contributes to overcoming the challenges that
face the consumers (Alghamdi et al., 2021). The success of the customer adoption process
is linked to the possibility of developing and improving the services of the cloud provider
itself. As a result, by providing an appropriate data storage audit trail, providers may be
able to aid their clients in regulatory and legal matters (Morgan & Conboy, 2013b). In
addition, Services Level Agreements (SLAs) may help the provider to achieve success
and avoid violations (Singh et al., 2017). However, the management of cloud service
providers is usually confronted with issues when it comes to renewable management
techniques such as agile project management (Oesterle et al., 2020). Moreover, the
physical location of the cloud provider is an influential matter regarding where the data
is stored by the service providers. As some providers store their client's data in many
countries, this can make the clients more concerned about their data. Therefore, based on
the previous discussion, hypothesis H12 is proposed as:

   H12: Increasing the number of cloud providers within Saudi Arabia increases the
Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.3.3 Legal Framework

The legal framework is considered to be a very important factor due to the problems that
can occur in this field. This is because sometimes the service provider is in one country,
the beneficiary is in another, and the data centre is in a third. This means that the rules
governing a particular service may differ significantly. Flexible and effective rules that
can meet the global standards are still required. It is very important to look for a legal



                                            80
framework that is suitable for both providers and clients, and that can meet the local
requirements as well (El-Gazzar et al., 2016b). However, both public and private
regulations are affected by the changing legal environment and the protection of
consumers and service providers' rights is a challenge for cloud computing. For all sides,
the laws and policies must be effective and equitable (Hourani & Abdallah, 2018). From
this study’s point of view, the adoption of cloud computing services must follow the
regulations of the country that hosts the services to be safe, and there must be trust in the
supervision of the legal organisation. Therefore, in relation to the previous discussion,
hypothesis H13 is proposed as:

   H13: Obtaining an organised legal framework of cloud computing increases Saudi
SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.3.4 Enterprise Competitiveness

Competitiveness is defined as the ability to perform distinctive commercial practices
through competing products (Krugman, 1994). From this study’s viewpoint, the concept
of competitiveness means that the enterprise is ready to compete in a specific market with
high motivation and the drive to be the best. However, in order to achieve the goals of the
enterprise and to compete strongly among its peers, it is necessary to maintain
technological competence (Saini & Kaur, 2017). Often, some competitiveness is positive in
that it pushes competitors to adopt technological innovations that meet the aspirations of
the field in which they work (Hermundsdottir & Aspelund, 2021). Therefore, competing
enterprises might see the advantages of adopting cloud computing services early on to be
able to gain the maximum benefit and to obtain better operational efficiency. Therefore,
in relation to the previous discussion, hypothesis H14 is proposed as:

   H14: Increasing the enterprise competitiveness increases the Saudi SMEs’
intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.4 The Social Context
The social context refers to the attitude and behaviours of the actual users, which are
affecting the organisation's decision to accept cloud services. Therefore, the following
three factors discuss deeply all the social elements that influence the intention of Saudi
SMEs toward adopting cloud services.




                                             81
3.8.4.1 Culture

Culture is an influencing factor as it reflects the attitude of the community toward the
adoption of cloud computing. More specifically, technological culture has been defined
as “… the increase in the efficiency and complexity of tools and techniques in human
populations over generations” (Osiurak & Reynaud, 2020). This, and other aspects of
culture such as traditional values, influence the enterprises’ intention to adopt new
technologies whether positively or negatively, especially if the enterprise provides a
service related to the community members (Alqahtani, 2016b). The current literature
regarding the influence of culture on adopting cloud computing services suggests that
Saudi Arabia's culture has a major influence on the private sector's adoption of these
services, and that traditional cultural values have a negative and indirect impact on a
firms’ decision to embrace them (Alkhater et al., 2018b). Thus, any understanding of the
barriers to the adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi SMEs necessitates an
investigation of the impact of culture. Therefore, based on the previous discussion,
hypothesis H15 is proposed as:

   H15: Increasing the technology culture for IT managers, top managers and
employees increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.4.2 Awareness

People's understanding of technology as well as the accessibility of internet technology
is referred to as awareness (Mofleh & Wanous, 2008). In the context of cloud computing,
awareness is the step that contributes to helping organisations benefit from adopting cloud
services (Chou, 2015). Community awareness plays an important role in accepting new
technologies for several different reasons, the most important of which is that people
cannot adopt new services without being educated about them and aware of their benefits.
One study investigated the reasons impeding cloud computing adoption by different sizes
of organisation in North America, Europe, Asia and Africa using the quantitative method.
It was found that 40 percent of participants did not believe in adopting cloud computing
services, and of these, 15 percent did not understand what cloud computing was (Raza et
al., 2015b). Although awareness of the Cloud has increased since then, lack of awareness
is always considered to be one of the main barriers to adopting new technologies. Thus,
SMEs must first educate the beneficiaries of their service before adopting cloud




                                            82
computing services for them to have sufficient knowledge and know-how about the
services. Therefore, in relation to the previous discussion, hypothesis H16 is proposed as:

    H16: Increasing the awareness of the IT managers, top managers and employees
increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

3.8.4.3 Trust

Trust is generally defined as “… the expectations about individual relationships and
behaviours” (Shockley-Zalabak et al., 2000). However, most of the research reviewed
indicates that in the IT field, ‘trust’ had a more specific definition. Thus, trust from the
perspective of cloud computing is an estimate of the capacity of cloud resources to
complete a job, based on variables such as resource processing power, dependability and
availability (Hassan et al., 2020). In cloud computing, trust is considered to be a crucial
issue and a lack of trust is an influential factor that may contribute to reducing the
adoption of cloud computing services. SMEs are concerned about how to avoid the lack
of transparency and control over the data in cloud computing services to keep their
reputation at a high level and to maintain people’s trust in their organisation (Khan &
Malluhi, 2010b). Therefore, a lack of trust does not simply refer to the service provider
but it also refers to a lack of faith in technology and its capacity to provide a decent service
without data loss. Therefore, based on the previous discussion, hypothesis H17 is
proposed as:

    H17: Increasing the trust of the IT managers, top managers and employees increases
the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.

    3.9 Summary
To summarise this chapter, cloud computing in Saudi Arabia is still in its early stages and
it is facing challenges in its adoption. To find out the challenges specifically facing Saudi
SMEs, this research investigated all the elements that may impact the conversion to Cloud
computing services. Moreover, this study developed a new comprehensive model that can
identify the most influential factors hindering the adoption of cloud services with 17
hypotheses. This study focused on explaining the model and discussing all of the factors
that may potentially affect the decisions of Saudi SMEs about adopting cloud computing
services. The next stage of the study will be to collect the data and analyse them to be




                                              83
able to demonstrate the most crucial factors currently affecting the Saudi SMEs' intention
to adopt cloud computing services.




                                           84
Chapter
4 Research Methodology
    4.1 Introduction
The literature review (Chapter 2) conducted a thorough investigation on the current status
of cloud computing adoption by SMEs in Saudi Arabia and its challenges, and discussed
the related issues. Chapter 3 discussed the theories and frameworks that have been created
and adopted in the field of technology adoption; and this discussion led to proposing a
new model for this study, called ACCM-SME, in order to examine all factors that may
affect Saudi SMEs' intentions to adopt cloud computing services. As shown in Figure 4.1,
this chapter introduces the methodology that was used to conduct this research based on
the literature review and the proposed model in order to achieve the study objectives.
Moreover, this research aimed to choose an appropriate and realistic methodology to
solve the main research problem based on the available resources and accessible
information; and taking into account the selection of the appropriate analytical tools for
it. This research was conducted to measure and identify the significant factors that impact
cloud adoption from the perspective of the IT managers, top managers and employees.
This entailed taking into account the research problem, research questions, research
objectives, investigation type, the study’s location and the designated time period when
selecting the approach for this research.

Before this chapter dives into the details, the researcher prefers to give a quick overview
of the difference between two similar words that are considered fundamental to scientific
research i.e., method and methodology, as confusion between these terms sometimes
occurs. In the field of scientific research ‘methodology’ refers to the philosophy used for
evaluating, interpreting, justifying and explaining scientific research inquiries to make
them understandable; and the people who do this are often called methodologists (Carter
& Little, 2007). In contrast, ‘method’ refers to the instruments that are used to collect data
for analysis from the sample, such as interviews and questionnaires (Viergever, 2019).
Therefore, determining a particular methodology (philosophy) is the first step that guides


                                             85
the researcher to choose the appropriate method for the research inquiries based on its
suitability for the chosen methodology (Slevitch, 2011). Churruca et al. (2021) argue that
choosing a particular methodology depends on several factors, including usability of
results, rigour, exploration of complex cases, practicability and the need for a
comprehensive approach. Therefore, there is no methodology or method that can be said
to be optimal, as each of them has its advantages and disadvantages. The selection of the
appropriate methodology and approach lies in the researcher’s insight and depends on his
or her research environment.




               Figure 4-1 Stage 4: Designing and conducting the study



    4.2 Design of the Research
Research design refers to the rules and procedures designed and selected to answer the
research questions and achieve the study objectives by following a set plan to deal with
data collection and analysis. This stage is critical because it aims to address the research
problem by linking the methodology to the appropriate research methods (Wahyuni,
2012). Crotty, (1998) determined the four main ‘pillars’ of research design to be: the
research philosophy, the research approach, the research strategy and the research
method. All of these will be explained in more detail in the following sections, as they
are fundamental to answering the research questions and achieving the research




                                            86
objectives. The research design aims to find proper answers to the research questions
which are as follows:

1. What is the current situation of the adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi
   Arabia SMEs? which is determined by a literature review of the barriers and
   challenges to cloud computing adoption in the current systems.
2. What are the most influential factors that impact the adoption of cloud computing
   services by Saudi SMEs? which is derived from the perspectives of the IT managers,
   top managers and employees.
3. What is an appropriate conceptual framework for understanding the adoption of
   cloud computing services by SMEs, specifically in Saudi Arabia?
4. To what extent is there an impact due to the adoption of cloud computing services by
   Saudi SMEs from the perspectives of the IT managers and top managers?

These questions were arrived at after the researcher investigated the facts about the
research problem, i.e., the adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs in Saudi
Arabia, and identified the research gap. Then, after a deep review and investigation of the
literature, a theoretical framework: The Adoption of Cloud Computing Model by Saudi
SMEs (ACCM-SME) was developed. This model generated many hypotheses which
could be used to test the intention of SMEs toward adopting cloud computing services.

This research is designed as a primary research study that employs both quantitative
(survey) and qualitative (semi-structured interviews) methods to collect the data. The
empirical part of this research will be carried out through a number of reliable approaches,
methods, instruments and techniques as well as having an underlying philosophical
position that reflects the nature and distinctiveness of this research. Therefore, the next
step is to establish the research philosophy and the research tools that will be used to
collect the data, and then verify their suitability, safety and ethical appropriateness
through the pilot study.


    4.3 Philosophy of the Research
The research philosophy can be thought of as the efforts made to bridge the knowledge
gaps and develop scientific research in order to obtain new, advanced research products
(Saunders et al., 2009a). This study aims to provide a comprehensive framework to assess
and analyse the intention of SMEs in Saudi Arabia regarding the adoption of cloud


                                            87
computing services, and then investigate the influencing factors and obstacles that hinder
the adoption process. Therefore, the contribution to knowledge is the new model that has
been developed to comprehensively study the effects of the internal and external factors
that influence Saudi SMEs’ adoption of cloud services.

When embarking on choosing a specific research philosophy, several variables that have
an impact must be taken into account, including the research objectives and questions,
the researcher’s beliefs and values, the ontological assumptions about the reality of the
phenomena, the data collection methods available and the ethical considerations (Al-
Somali, 2012). This is due to the enormous impact that can occur if the philosophy chosen
does not effectively work with research objectives and questions, resulting in an
inaccurate or incorrect result.

The researcher's beliefs about a particular phenomenon under study and how to identify
it can be described through two branches of philosophy known as ontology and
epistemology (Scotland, 2012). Ontology is concerned with the study of being (Crotty,
1998); which defines reality and the phenomena that exist within it, as well as how those
phenomena affect reality through their form, work and content. Epistemology, on the
other hand, is concerned with knowledge itself, as well as its nature and forms (Cohen et
al., 2007); and how knowledge of a phenomenon is defined and shaped by its socio-
cultural environment.

Saunders et al. (2009a) have classified the philosophy of research into four main
paradigms which are: interpretivism, positivism, pragmatism and realism (see Figure 4.2).




               Figure 4-2: The Research Onion (Saunders et al., 2009a)


                                           88
The above diagram is known as ‘the research onion’ and consists of six main layers which
help the researchers who use it to know the path of their studies very clearly. The ‘onion’
illustrates the different phases of research and the elements included in each of them. Two
main philosophical approaches that have been used widely in IS studies are Interpretivism
and Positivism (Saunders et al., 2009a); and this research is planning to use both of them,
and will discuss them in detail in the following sections.

4.3.1 Positivism
The positivist philosophy is based on the concept that the investigative process is separate
from an independently existing reality which is also separate from the researcher and from
what is being studied. The positivist philosophy aims to accept or reject theories based on
empirical research findings. Thus, positivism in research aids in the accurate
understanding of social phenomena through the presence of predetermined relationships
within a phenomenon that is empirically examined using specific instruments
(Alharahsheh & Pius, 2020). The positivist approach employs existing theories to develop
hypotheses, which are then tested, resulting in confirmation or refutation of the theory as
a whole or in part, that might also contribute towards further theoretical development
(Saunders et al., 2009a); and positivist researchers “assume that reality is objectively
given and can be described by measurable properties” (Myers, 2019). The positivist
approach uses quantitative scientific, objective and empirical methods; whereby social
reality can be investigated by the same methods as those used in the natural sciences (Bell,
2022). Thus, when there are statements, measurable variables and hypotheses that can be
utilized to generate conclusions about a phenomenon from a selected sample of the
population, the research is considered positivist (Orlikowski & Baroudi, 1991). This study
aims to build a proposed theoretical model about the intention of SMEs in Saudi Arabia
to adopt cloud computing services, and then verify the proposed model by testing
hypotheses based on influencing factors and variables which will need to be tested
quantitatively and qualitatively. Therefore, this research employs a positivist approach
insofar as it assesses the proposed model through testing the hypotheses, the result of
which will be given in detail later in Chapter Five.

4.3.2 Interpretivism
In contrast, an interpretivist approach is commonly employed in the field of social
sciences, and is based on the idea that phenomena and their meaning can be understood


                                            89
through people’s subjective perceptions; and that what is observed will be not always be
interpreted in the same manner. ‘Interpretivist approach’ and ‘qualitative research’ are
two linguistically different terms, but they are often used interchangeably, and, as this
approach does not test hypotheses or work deductively, some scholars agree that
qualitative research is characterized by an interpretivist approach (Williams, 2000). Thus,
interpretivism is commonly associated with qualitative and inductive methodologies that
can give profound insight into social issues.

Rather than relying on quantities of statistics, interpretivism requires a qualitative
methodology to understand phenomena through individuals’ perspectives and
experiences often by closely interviewing people to understand their perceptions (Thanh
& Thanh, 2015). Interpretivism is therefore an approach that seeks to comprehend the
context of an information system, as well as the process by which the context impacts and
is impacted by the information system (Walsham, 1993). However, although this research
employs positivism as its main approach, the semi-structured interviews are used to more
fully understand managers’ perceptions and experiences regarding the adoption of cloud
computing services by Saudi SMEs.

As stated above, the main research philosophy is positivism in addition to interpretivist
philosophy which underlies the aspects of the research which uses semi-structured
interviews. In information systems research, a methodology is chosen that aligns with the
research's main goal and achieves its objectives, as there is no specific philosophical
methodology to be followed and implemented (Mazaheri et al., 2020). Mingers (2003)
conducted a deeper investigation in the literature of information systems to discover the
most popular approaches that were used in the field and found that 75% employed a
positivist approach, 17% employed an interpretivist approach and 5% employed other
approaches. This shows that both positivism and interpretivism can be usefully deployed
in the field of information systems.

As a result, the following points highlight the rationale for choosing these two
approaches:

   •   The positivist approach is the most often employed in information system
       research, accounting for 75% of all research in this discipline (Mingers, 2003).




                                            90
       Therefore, the methodologies associated with this approach have been
       demonstrated to be useful many times.
   •   One of the objectives of this research is to investigate to what extent the intentions
       of SMEs in Saudi Arabia could be affected to adopt cloud computing services
       from the experiences and perceptions of the IT managers, top managers and
       employees, and the researcher should, ideally, stay impartial and distant from
       anything that can affect the result of the study. From an ontological standpoint,
       this explains why positivism was chosen as the primary paradigm.
   •   This research was aiming to create a new comprehensive framework, then this
       study developed a new comprehensive model (see Chapter 3) which mainly
       worked to investigate the factors that may impact on the Saudi SMEs' intention to
       adopt cloud computing services; therefore, the relationships between constructs
       in the model were clearly identified. One of the fundamental aspects of positivism
       is a supposedly objective reality that can be described by a conceptual framework
       that examines the interactions between independent and dependent variables.
       From an epistemological standpoint, this explains why positivism was chosen as
       the primary paradigm.
   •   This research also presented a set of carefully constructed hypotheses, to be tested
       and accepted or rejected by collecting quantitative data as the main method, in
       addition to qualitative data as an aid to understanding people's perceptions
       individually (see Chapter 3). Also, the deductive approach used will greatly help
       in knowing the factors influencing the intention of SMEs to adopt cloud
       computing services. This explains, from a methodological standpoint, the choice
       of positivism as the principal approach, along with qualitative methods associated
       with an interpretivist approach.
   •   Another feature of the positivist technique that attracts researchers is the
       possibility of generalising the findings from the target population's sample.

What has been discussed and justified above are the reasons behind why this research is
following the positivist paradigm as the main research philosophy, in addition, to
benefiting from the methods associated with an interpretivist paradigm to answer the
research questions and achieve the research objectives in a comprehensive manner.
Therefore, a mixed methods approach is considered the appropriate option for this
research because it enables the researcher to build a comprehensive systematic framework


                                            91
that examines the factors that affect the adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi
SMEs by collecting extensive data from the target group and then verifying the validity
of the proposed model to explore the existing factors which are affecting the adoption
process by analysing qualitative data obtain through interviews.

In the IS field, there are no specified approaches or philosophies that should be followed;
however, positivist and interpretivist approaches have been widely used together in
several studies (Orlikowski & Baroudi, 1991). Moreover, Al-Zaabi (2013) and Alghamdi
(2016) conducted studies in the field of information systems to investigate the factors
affecting the adoption of e-government services, and they employed both positivist and
interpretivist philosophies together to understand and identify those influencing factors.
Thus, in the next section, the deductive and inductive approaches will be explained and
discussed in detail.


    4.4 The Research Approach (Deduction and Induction)
Deductive and inductive approaches are employed by researchers in the process of
analysing research data. The deductive approach characterises research in which
theoretical frameworks and hypotheses are established and empirically tested (Collis &
Hussey, 2013). It can be described as an approach that starts from more general terms and
develops towards more specific terms as it starts from theory, then builds the hypotheses,
then tests them and revises the theory accordingly (Locke, 2007; Nola & Sankey, 2007).
Therefore, the deductive approach has been called a ‘top-down’ approach.

The inductive approach, on the other hand, starts from more specific terms and moves on
to more general terms, as it makes observations about some specific phenomena then
develops theories based on these empirical observations (Locke, 2007). Furthermore, the
inductive technique is used to draw broad conclusions from individual situations (Burney,
2008). Thus, the inductive approach can be called the ‘bottom-up’ approach. Therefore,
this study follows the deductive approach as it starts by developing the model and
hypotheses, then testing and analysing them empirically and taking the final findings as
a guide to potentially making revisions to the main theory.




                                            92
    4.5 Strategies Used by the Research
Research strategy is described as a collection of many methodologies and procedures that
the researcher employs to gather and evaluate research data, as well as how the researcher
perceives reality and plans the research project (Verschuren, 2003). Research strategy has
been described by Creswell, (2008)as “the plan or proposal to conduct research” for
looking to the proper answers of the research questions. Saunders et al. (2009a) divided
research strategies into seven categories which are: ‘case study’, ‘survey’, ‘experiment’,
‘archival research’, ‘action research’, ‘grounded theory’ and ‘ethnography’. Each
technique, according to Yin (2009), can be used for exploratory, descriptive or
explanatory research. However, the approach should be chosen depending on the study
aims and questions, as no strategy is superior to another, and the research can utilise one
or several strategies (Saunders et al., 2009a). This indicates that one method can be
employed in conjunction with another, and there are numerous tactics and strategies that
may be used and applied to overcome the challenges associated with this study. Therefore,
this research employed both the case study and the survey as strategies, in order to
conduct deep investigation in Saudi SMEs' intention toward cloud computing service
adoption. These strategies are described in more detail in the following sections.

4.5.1 Survey Strategy
The survey strategy seeks to provide statistics on the responses of the target group of
people to questions; and the internet has made it possible to gather data through an online
survey (Fowler Jr, 2013a). The survey is one of the most extensively utilised research
methodologies in social science due to its ability to collect a large amount of reliable data
reasonably quickly within a limited period of time (Neuman, 2013). Surveys are used in
various fields, but they are particularly widely used in information system studies
(Orlikowski & Baroudi, 1991). The deductive approach is associated with this strategy,
which collects quantitative data through questionnaires which can then be analysed and
presented in a descriptive and statistical manner (Saunders et al., 2009a). The findings of
the analysed data will help in investigating and indicating the links between the factors
and the strength of the relationship between variables (Saunders et al., 2009a).

This strategy is central to the process of the study as it produces findings that are relevant
to the target group of people (Saunders et al., 2009a). As mentioned earlier in the first



                                             93
chapter of this study, this study’s objectives include investigating the impact of the
adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi SMEs, and analysing and identifying the
most influential factors that impact this adoption in order to obtain the views of the target
population, it was necessary to collect data from a sample that was of a sufficient size to
be representative. Therefore, the survey was considered a suitable strategy for collecting
a large amount of data from the selected participants and achieving the goals of this study
in an effective manner.

The context of this study is Saudi SMEs and the challenges they face in adopting cloud
computing services. The targeted group of participants in this study are therefore all from
Saudi Arabia, regardless of their nationalities, and the survey will be conducted when the
researcher is conducting field research in Saudi Arabia and collecting data through the
survey and the case study. Moreover, a number of hypotheses have been developed (see
Chapter 3) based on the proposed model and they need to be accepted or refuted through
empirical tests, so the survey strategy is considered the best strategy for doing this. In the
early part of this chapter, the positivist approach was discussed and chosen to be the main
philosophy underpinning this research; and the survey strategy is related to that
philosophy (Saunders et al., 2009a). Therefore, for all these reasons, the survey is
employed as the main strategy complemented by the case study strategy that will be
discussed in the following section.

4.5.2 Case Study Strategy
The literature reveals that several researchers have used the case study as an auxiliary
method that contributes to understanding the phenomena under study more thoroughly
and clarifying any ambiguities resulting from the quantitative investigation. The case
study is most commonly characterised as the investigation of a specific instance
(Verschuren, 2003); and has been defined by Robson (2002) as” a strategy for doing
research that involves an empirical investigation of a particular contemporary
phenomenon within its real-life context using multiple sources of evidence”. For
investigating a phenomenon in-depth, a case study is commonly utilized, and this is a
prominent strategy in the IS field (Myers & Avison, 2002); as it is an appropriate way to
answer some of the questions that accompany certain phenomena, such as ‘why does this
occur (or not occur)’ from an individual point of view.




                                             94
The case study strategy contains two types, which are: the single case study and the
multiple case study; the single case study seeks to provide an answer to the questions and
findings from a single point of view, while the multiple case seeks to provide an answer
to the questions and findings from various points of view (Eisenhardt & Graebner, 2007).
Thus, utilizing multiple cases is more effective in investigating the study questions and
allows the researcher to make a theoretical assessment in a more comprehensive way
(Eisenhardt & Graebner, 2007). In addition, studies that present their results through
multiple cases are superior to those that present their results based on a single case due to
analytical conclusions that are based on multiple cases being considered to have more
validity (Yin, 2009).

This study is interested in the factors that impact the adoption of cloud computing services
by Saudi SMEs, as well as how these aspects lead to the successful adoption and
implementation of cloud computing services. Moreover, the aims of this study include
investigating and understanding the influence of the proposed factors through an
empirical examination within the Saudi environment, as well as gathering data from the
perspectives of IT managers, top managers and employees of SMEs. Therefore, the
multiple case study is more suitable for gathering the data from semi-structured
interviews with IT managers and top managers to understand the factors affecting
resistance to adoption more deeply.

One of the goals of this research is to deliver richly detailed outcomes from a variety of
perspectives. Studying and examining influencing factors from the views of top managers
and IT managers in SMEs are an assessment from two different perspectives that entail
the employment of the multi-case method in the case study strategy. The researcher
focuses in this study on investigating the impact of factors, analysing the resulting impact,
and then verifying the validity of the proposed model in adopting cloud computing
services by Saudi SMEs from several different points of view that represent potential
users of cloud computing services, which is why multiple case studies are used.
Therefore, employing multiple case studies in this study contributes to strengthening the
research results and helps SMEs to understand factors affecting adoption of cloud
computing, which is why this approach was chosen.

According to Orlikowski and Baroudi (1991), the survey strategy and the case study
strategy have been the most frequently applied strategies in information system studies.


                                             95
A range of studies found in the literature reviewed evaluated the different research
strategies applied in information system studies about technology adoption and
utilisation. The best example of these studies is arguably Choudrie and Dwivedi’s (2005)
investigation, as they reviewed a number of articles and studies published in well-known
journals in the information system field and other related fields. They discovered that the
case study and survey were by far the most often used techniques to examine technology
adoption.

As the current study aims to collect data from different perspectives and wishes to use
strategies that offer both breadth and depth, then those findings can be considered as
supporting evidence for applying both the survey and the case study in this study.
Saunders et al. (2009a) said that collecting data could be done by questionnaires,
interviews or observations. This study gathers data through questionnaires as the main
strategy, and semi-structured interviews as the auxiliary strategy. The next section will
discuss the different types of research method including the quantitative method,
qualitative method and mixed-methods.


    4.6 Research Methods
Research methods involve a series of processes which investigate a specific phenomenon
and comprise data, collection, processing and analysis. Based on the research objectives,
appropriate research methods should be selected to determine the route which the study
should follow to collect and analyze the data. Cresswell and Clark (2007) argued that
researchers are able to learn about phenomena such as views, attitudes and human
decision-making through appropriate research methods. Therefore, the next sections will
present the different methods, discuss them in detail and confirm the research method
utilized in this research.

4.6.1 The Quantitative Method
Quantitative methods are utilized to understand and measure phenomena numerically.
The quantitative method is defined by Creswell and Creswell (1994) as the method that
uses a large sample to test existing theories and hypotheses to understand a certain
phenomenon through a deductive approach. In addition, the quantitative method is
generally associated with the positivist philosophy and the acquisition of numerical data
for statistical analysis (Creswell & Creswell, 2017a). Furthermore, the quantitative


                                            96
method is the best strategy if the study objective is to find factors that impact a result or
to make predictions about how variables relate to each other; as well as being ideal for
any study that seeks to test an explanation or theory (Creswell & Creswell, 2017a).

The objectives of this research include investigating and examining the factors
influencing Saudi SMEs' intentions to adopt cloud computing services; and the study aims
to validate an appropriate conceptual model by exploring and evaluating hypotheses
about the proposed factors. Therefore, the quantitative method is utilized as the main data
collection method in this study. As aforementioned, the quantitative method involves
collecting data from a large sample of participants; and this study uses structured
questionnaires with potential cloud computing services adopters, which is one of the tools
related to this method, to collect solid and worthy data from various possible users.

4.6.2 The Qualitative Method
The qualitative method is used to investigate and comprehend the meaning that people or
groups attach to a phenomenon. Furthermore, the research process includes developing
questions and methods, data collection in the participants’ environment, inductive data
analysis developing from specific details to generic elements, and finally the researcher
demonstrating the significance of the findings through data interpretation (Creswell,
2008). In addition, Bryman (2008) defined qualitative methods as “a research strategy
that usually emphasises words rather than quantification in the collection and analysis
of data”. While Creswell (2008) noted that "Often the distinction between qualitative and
quantitative research is framed in terms of using words (qualitative) rather than numbers
(quantitative), or using closed-ended questions (quantitative hypotheses) rather than
open-ended questions (qualitative interview questions)". There are cases when the
qualitative approach is the best way to explore phenomena in more depth (Ormston et al.,
2014); as qualitative methods offer a comprehensive description through comprehending
and analysing social relationships.

The qualitative method employs an inductive approach, with its goal to develop theory
during the process of the research, and this study utilizes it as an auxiliary method. A
number of the study participants are given a brief semi-structured interview. The purpose
of using this strategy is to strengthen the conclusions of this study by providing more in-
depth evidence. Furthermore, this method gives the researcher the opportunity to



                                             97
understand the influencing factors more deeply, since it enables the participants’
perceptions to be obtained in more detail. Therefore, the qualitative method is used in this
study to analyse fully the influence of the given factors on the intention of the Saudi SMEs
to adopt cloud computing services from the viewpoints of top managers and IT managers.

4.6.3 Mixed Methods
Utilizing quantitative and qualitative methods with their different procedures and
techniques together in one study is known as a mixed-methods approach. Tashakkori and
Creswell (2007) defined mixed methods as “…research in which the investigator collects
and analyses data, integrates the findings, and draws inferences using both qualitative
and quantitative approaches or methods in a single study or a program of inquiry”. This
approach helps to develop a deep investigation into a variety of phenomena of interest
that may not be clearly realized and explored just by utilizing a single approach (Candy,
1991). Creswell (2014) claimed that using mixed methods was sometimes is better than
using one method as this strategy gave the researcher a chance to fully understand the
problem of study in different ways.

The phrase ‘mixed methods’ refers to the use of both quantitative and qualitative
methodologies in data collecting and procedures of analysis in one study (Saunders et al.,
2009a). The quantitative and qualitative data gathering approaches and sampling
procedures and their analyses in the mixed-method approach cannot be combined but can
be parallel (integrated at the same time) or sequentially (one after the other) (Creswell &
Clark, 2017). It is also important to consider the importance of each method in a particular
study (Saunders et al., 2009a). The amount to which each methodology is employed is
determined by the nature of the study; and there are three basic categories of mixed
methods that may be distinguished in this regard (Johnson et al., 2007). Figure 4.3 shows
that there are two pure methods (qualitative and quantitative), but these can mix in
different degrees so that they can be equal in importance, or one or the other can be
dominant.




                                            98
           Figure 4-3: Categories of mixed methods (Johnson et al., 2007)
Research that uses mixed methods where the quantitative method is the main method
while the qualitative method is an auxiliary method is referred to as ‘quantitative
dominant’; conversely, research where the qualitative method is the main method and the
quantitative method is an auxiliary method is referred to as ‘qualitative dominant’
(Johnson et al., 2007). Therefore, this study's mixed methods may be classified as
'quantitative dominant' main method with both approaches used to address the research
objectives and find the answers to the research questions.

Combining methodologies has the potential to improve research, because using one single
technique contains weaknesses as well as strengths (Creswell & Clark, 2017). Thus,
mixed methods have commonly been used in the information system field (Myers, 1997).
The mix of research approaches is known as ‘triangulation’ which is defined as “…the
application and combination of several research methodologies in the study of the same
phenomenon” (Denzin, 2007). Leedy et al. (2015) noted that: “Triangulation is also
common in mixed-methods designs, in which both quantitative and qualitative data are
collected to address a single research question”. Triangulation is used to acquire and
validate different points of view by employing different data collection methods including
different samples and data sources (Jick, 1979). In addition, triangulation must be viewed
as a method that aims to complement findings rather than contradicting them (Patton,
1999).




                                           99
As this research uses mixed methods to conduct this study, there are several scientific
reasons behind why two methods in parallel were selected as a mixed method. Denzin
(2017) justified using triangulation between methods as follows: firstly, it acts to reduce
bias; secondly, the findings will be an agreement on the truth concerning any social
occurrence. Jick (1979) argued that although triangulation is not suitable for all kinds of
the studies, it has many advantages, such as inspiring researchers to create new ways to
collect a range of data which will give them more confidence in their findings. Also,
employing triangulation can help in the integration or formulation of ideas and uncover
any conflicts thus acting as a decision-making tool between conflicting ideas (Jick, 1979).
Therefore, since triangulation involves collecting data by different methods in order to
understand the phenomenon deeply, then it is a suitable strategy to be applied to achieve
the objectives of this study.

There are also certain reasons for using mixed methods in parallel. Firstly, the researcher
has a limited period of time to conducted the research, and this means collecting both
quantitative and qualitative data in parallel in order to obtain it within the timeframe.
Secondly, this research is focused on collecting the data from SMEs in Saudi Arabia, the
researcher will need to travel to collect the data from the KSA and then come back to the
UK. Because it is difficult to go and come back again many times, the researcher chooses
to apply the different methods in parallel. Therefore, triangulation is utilized in this study
by collecting the data through surveys and semi-structured interviews in order to obtain
sufficient and powerful data about the Saudi SMEs’ intention toward adopting cloud
computing services.


    4.7 Data Collection
This section will discuss the data collection in this research through the two methods
(survey   and    semi-structured    interviews)    used   to   achieve   triangulation    and
complementarity. The objective is to broaden the viewpoints of participants involved in
Saudi SMEs from different perspectives. The survey gathered quantitative data from
Saudi SMEs employees. In contrast, the semi-structured interviews gathered qualitative
data from top managers and IT managers working in Saudi SMEs. The interview
questions were developed and aligned with the survey questions to explore and
complement the quantitative data from a comprehensive perspective. For instance, if the
questionnaire asked about the security challenges of cloud adoption by Saudi SMEs, then


                                             100
the interview would ask similar questions to complement and extend the first method.
The interdependence between quantitative and qualitative methods improves the study's
results and contributes to accomplishing its objectives. In addition, the following sections
will discuss how the data tools are designed, disseminated, and data collected through
both methods.

4.7.1 First Method: Quantitative
This method is the main one for this study and aims to collect data from Saudi SMEs'
employees, including all types of employees, whether regular, IT, supervisory or
otherwise to consider their beliefs and perspectives on challenges that affect Saudi SMEs'
intention to adopt cloud computing services. The survey was developed and widely
distributed electronically to potential Saudi SMEs in Riyadh to attract the maximum
number of respondents. Information sheets and consent forms were sent with the survey
to give potential participants an idea about the ongoing study and ask for their permission
before participating. The information sheets also assured them that their identities would
be anonymous, that their data would be confidential, and that they could withdraw from
the study at any stage. In addition, the study gave every participant a unique identifier
number that could be used in case a participant wanted to withdraw from the study before
the date stated in the information sheet. How the questionnaire was designed and tested,
the sample size selected, and the survey conducted is discussed in the following sub-
sections.

4.7.1.1 Instrument Design

The questionnaire includes items utilised and in previous studies and adapted and tested
for this study (Al Khater, 2017b; Al Mudawi, 2021; Liang et al., 2017; Martins et al.,
2014; Oliveira et al., 2014b; Raut et al., 2018; Senarathna et al., 2018b). The questionnaire
was designed carefully to ensure simplicity, validity and reliability of the questions. It
was designed to be easily understood by Saudi SME employees and unambiguous. The
questionnaire aimed to investigate all factors included in this study by developing at least
three items for each factor. It has two parts: the first is the demographic part, which has
11 questions, and the second is the study questions section about the factors considered
to be the challenges facing Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services,
which has 55 questions (See Appendix E).




                                            101
This study employs the Likert Scale, which ranges from 1 - Strongly Disagree to 5 -
Strongly Agree, to measure the participants' perspectives. The Likert Scale is commonly
used for surveys due to its simplicity and accuracy. It provides quantitative data that can
be analysed statistically to find the direction of a pattern for a targeted group of people
(DeVellis & Thorpe, 2021). After that, the questionnaire was sent to some researchers
and professors in the field for a peer-review process to ensure it was ready to disseminate
for the pilot study without any problems.

The questionnaire was designed on the Qualtrics platform using the Sussex University
account, which gives access to additional features and keeps the data in a secure
environment without any access by a third party. The Qualtrics platform is known for
developing and distributing questionnaires. as it is easy for both users: the participants
and the administrator (researcher).

The questionnaire was originally in English to satisfy Sussex University requirements.
Then, the questionnaire was translated into Arabic and checked by experts, because the
native language of the targeted populations in Saudi Arabia is Arabic. This method
has been used for both the pilot and main studies.

This survey was mainly a web-based questionnaire, and that was due to the COVID-19
pandemic, as most SMEs' employees worked remotely. Also, web-based questionnaires
saved time and cost in collecting the data and reaching a large number of potential
participants in the targeted populations.

4.7.1.2 Pilot Study

The pilot study is a small-scale experiment used to test the questionnaire before it is
launched and distributed to a large group of people to identify potential mistakes. The
purpose of collecting the data in this phase is to check for errors, such as ambiguity and
inconsistency, that the participants may be facing and to make sure how much time is
needed and how well the instrument is working (Bryman, 2016). In addition, the pilot
study allows the researcher to test a small sample early to investigate the validity and
reliability of the questionnaire before the actual data collection is run (Creswell &
Creswell, 2017b). Furthermore, the pilot study helps the researcher to detect any technical
issues prior to disseminating the main study on a large scale.




                                            102
The pilot study was conducted in two phases. The first phase involved sending the pilot
study questionnaire to professors and researchers in the field to get their perceptions and
feedback, as they are experts and are familiar with it. After getting their feedback and
resolving their comments, the questionnaire was translated into Arabic by the researcher
who is a native Arabic speaker, as it is the native language of the targeted population of
the study. Then, it was sent again to some experts in Arabic to ensure the language of the
questions was structured clearly and unambiguously. The second phase involved sending
the pilot study to some Saudi SMEs employees in Riyadh. It was distributed to collect 20
responses. However, 27 responses were completed and collected. The number of samples
for the pilot study was set based on the recommendation that 10-15 is a sufficient sample
(Hertzog, 2008). The number of responses collected met the threshold of recommendation
for the sample size for the pilot study.

Finally, to make sure the instrument designed was reliable, Cronbach’s alpha coefficient
was employed. Cronbach’s alpha coefficient is scaled from 0 to 1, where 1 is considered
perfect and below 0.60 is considered poor and unreliable (Hair et al., 2013). Based on
that, the Cronbach’s alpha coefficient for each questionnaire construct was above the
threshold. It ranged from 0.74 to 0.85 (see Table 5.18 in Chapter 5), which accomplished
the study's reliability.

4.7.1.3 Defining Sample Size

Gathering data from every member of the targeted population would achieve absolute
precision, however, this is rarely feasible or necessary, especially given the time and
financial constraints on most research. Academics commonly employ a sampling
technique, which selects a target group of people to participate in a study to measure a
phenomenon and address time and expense constraints. These strategies involve
gathering data from an individual or group of the population and using it to make
generalisations. Therefore, there is a slight decline in accuracy, but it should still be in an
acceptable and manageable range (Saunders et al., 2009). This kind of compromise raises
questions regarding the sample's representativeness for the intended population.
Consequently, the acquisition of a representative sample goes through stages that will be
discussed in the following points:




                                             103
   • Identifying the Research Population
Selecting the target population is considered a crucial aspect of the research endeavour.
Then, the researcher must choose a research plan and determine the study methodologies
to address the research questions. This study investigates the perspectives of Saudi SME
employees, IT managers, and top managers from prominent companies. The study
involved samples from different sectors of Saudi SMEs in Riyadh City. They were briefed
on the study objectives and requested to provide their perspectives on the ongoing
research.

   • Sampling Procedure
Selecting the proper sample method is based on many factors, including the study
objectives and the constraints that face the research, such as cost and time. Sampling
methods are divided into two primary categories: the first is probability sampling, and the
second is non-probability sampling (Saunders et al., 2009). In probability sampling, each
individual from a selected population has a chance and probability of being chosen and
participating in the study's sample, which means it is known. This category of sampling
is suitable for the quantitative method (survey). In contrast, in non-probability sampling,
the probability is unknown because a selected participant of the selected population is
unknown and cannot be determined (Groves et al., 2009). This category of sampling is
more relevant to qualitative method. Therefore, this study applied and used the probability
sampling category as it aligns with the study objectives and directions.

After using the probability sampling category, it is worth mentioning that many
techniques fall under this category, including Random sampling, Stratified sampling, and
Cluster sampling (Groves et al., 2009). Random sampling is based on equal chance for
everyone who can be selected to participate in the study, and that is what this study is
aimed at and has used. Stratified sampling divides participants based on criteria such as
(salary) and then selecting individuals. Cluster sampling is employed based on a selection
sample of groups, not individuals. Finally, random sampling was selected and used
because the chance of involving an individual is balanced for everyone. The last two were
unsuitable for this study because there was no equality among the targeted populations.
Potential participants were manually selected manually using systematic random
sampling and the sample was later augmented by the researcher asking friends and



                                           104
colleagues to pass on the link and visited SMEs in person. Although this meant that the
final sample was not purely probabilistic, having an additional snowball and convenience
sample meant that the response rate was likely to be better and the sample size required
achieved (see details below).

   • Identifying the Sample Size
The sample size refers to the number of people required from a targeted population to
participate in a survey while obtaining a certain level of data accuracy (Fowler Jr, 2013).
It is crucial for any study to decide the minimum sample size needed to achieve the
accuracy needed to generalise its results. In addition, any study should care about the time
and budget required when selecting a sample size to facilitate the execution process. Due
to that, many techniques have been developed and merged into the field to help
researchers define the optimal sample size for any population. Therefore, Hair et al.
(2013) suggest that any sample size larger than 300 will be appropriate for most studies,
while Saunders et al. (2009) calculated the minimum sample size for each specific
population in one table, which is shown in Table 5.1 (in Chapter 5). The sample size for
this study has been calculated based on the technique recommended by Saunders et al.
(2009); it was 384, with a level of confidence that attains 95% degree and a margin of
error that attains 5% degree. The survey obtained a total of 412 valid responses, which
meets the required level of sample size.

4.7.1.4 Conducting the Survey

The survey is the main method for this study and was conducted fully electronically due
to the COVID-19 pandemic. Firstly, the researcher developed a web-based survey link on
the Qualtrics platform, utilising the Sussex University account. Second, the researcher
started collecting contact details of the randomly selected Saudi SME employees in
Riyadh City (such as emails and WhatsApp numbers) from their SMEs' websites or even
social platforms (such as the X platform). Then, the survey link was sent to the employees,
IT managers and top managers of Saudi SMEs in Riyadh City. The participants were
invited to participate, and provided with all the necessary information about the study,
including the estimated time to complete the survey. Each participant was given a unique
identification number that could be used in case a participant wanted to withdraw from
the study before the set date to start analysing the data. The participants were also kindly
asked to distribute this survey to their colleagues within their SMEs in Riyadh City, who


                                            105
might not have been reached. In addition, the researcher visited the physical locations of
many SMEs in Riyadh city and shared the survey link with them to ensure that it reached
the largest possible group of the targeted population. The survey was distributed between
the 5th of September 2022 and the 3rd of December 2022.

The reasons for conducting a web-based survey are the following:

    •   Safety

The COVID-19 pandemic was a serious reason to avoid face-to-face meetings, and most
employees worked remotely. In addition, most SMEs were forced to impose severe
regulations on meetings and advised their employees to do them online to avoid any
spread of COVID within their organisations. The only way to collect the data was through
a web-based survey.

    •   Efficiency

Nowadays, a web-based survey is more efficient because it reaches many individuals in
the targeted populations through online tools by simply sending it to their emails and
social media accounts. Additionally, participants can be recruited by asking to forward
the link to the web-based survey to their colleagues easily and quickly through their work
groups, such as WhatsApp and Telegram. Overall, online tools save waiting time and
distribution costs.

    •   Storage

A web-based survey through the Sussex University account on the Qualtrics platform
enabled the researcher to store the data electronically and automatically after the
participants submit their responses in a secure environment. This technique saves the data
and prevents it from being stored as hard copies of the questionnaire and any errors in
entering it.

4.7.2 Second Method: Qualitative
This method complements the first method and aims to collect qualitative data from Saudi
SMEs. For this purpose, semi-structured interviews were conducted with IT managers
and top managers of SMEs in Riyadh city.



                                           106
Interviewing is a common method qualitative studies use to collect data. There are three
types of interviews: structured, unstructured and semi-structured (Rubin & Rubin, 2011).
For structured interviews, the questions are close-ended and predetermined. The
researcher asks the questions and receives the answers without asking for any further
explanation. Sometimes, it could be considered an effective type that is easy to conduct
and saves time. However, from a quality perspective, it limits the discussion that could
yield rich and interesting data and negatively affect the study's result. For unstructured
interviews, the questions are not predetermined, and there is no interview guide, which
means an open prompt for participants to share their experiences and then interact with
what they said. This type allows for collecting in-depth details, but it could also waste
time and collect many unstructured variables, complicating the analysis process. For
semi-structured interviews, this type combines the advantages of the previous two types.
The questions in this type are prepared and open-ended, which means flexibility exists.
This implies that any further discussion on participants' answers can be conducted.
According to the previous discussion and clarification for each type of interview, semi-
structured interviews were employed because they are flexible and allow for the
simultaneous collection of structured and in-depth data.

4.7.2.1 Instrument Design

The interview questions were developed to investigate the perspectives of IT managers
and top managers of Saudi SMEs on the factors that challenge the intention of adopting
cloud computing services based on the developing model in Chapter 3. The same criteria
applied to designing the quantitative instrument in section 4.7.1 were considered when
designing these questions. The developed questions for the interview are mainly similar
to the survey questions but with open-ended questions to find any in-depth details from
the company directors. The questions' validity and reliability are achieved through the
following steps. First, the developing questions were peer-reviewed by professors and
researchers. Their comments helped the researcher formulate some questions. Second, the
questions were translated into Arabic, as it is the native language of the target population,
and then again reviewed by an expert in Arabic to ensure they were structured and
unambiguous. Third, the pilot study with five participants was conducted to test the
reliability, validity and time needed to complete the interview questions before
conducting them on a large scale.




                                            107
4.7.2.2 Sample Selection

The targeted population for this part of the study is the top managers and IT managers of
Saudi SMEs in Riyadh City. Fifteen SMEs from different sectors with diverse experience
were selected to conduct the interviews. Those SMEs were chosen based on their rank
within the sector and size published by the Small and Medium Enterprises General
