Authority (Monsha’at). In addition, SMEs were selected based on the researcher's
knowledge as to whether those SMEs had adopted cloud services or not (Rubin & Rubin,
2011; Saunders et al., 2009b). This aims to guarantee that the sample consists of
individuals with a wide range of experiences and cultures to gain evident perspectives
regarding Saudi SMEs' challenges in adopting cloud computing services, and the sample
represented people in companies that had and had not adopted these services.

4.7.2.3 Conducting the Interviews

Between September 5th and December 3rd, 2022, one-to-one interviews were conducted
with IT managers and top managers of Saudi SMEs in Riyadh City. The study objectives
were explained to the participants as well as the time expected to complete the interview.
The information sheet and the study's consent forms were handed to the participants
before starting the interview; and the consent form was required to be signed. The
interview protocol was to audio-record, but if the participant did not give permission, the
researcher wrote notes. All participants were given a copy of the information sheet with
the researcher's contact details in case they wanted to make enquiries or withdraw from
the study. All of the interviews were conducted in Arabic, the native language of the
targeted group. The researcher then made transcripts in English to meet the study's
requirements. All interviews were conducted face-to-face, and socially-distanced
according to the instructions of the COVID-19 pandemic. The length of the interviews
varied, but the average time was 60 minutes.


    4.8 Data analysis
4.8.1 Quantitative Analysis
The data analysis stage is critical as it produces the results which answer the research
questions. Many types of analysis for quantitative data exist, and their selection should
be carefully done. However, according to Hair et al. (2013), two fundamental types of
analysis are recommended: descriptive analysis and statistical analysis. The former


                                           108
involves describing the key data characteristics of the sample, such as mean; and the latter
consists of using the data collected to calculate the relationship between variables to test
the hypotheses. Therefore, this study employs descriptive and statistical analysis to
analyse the survey data to comprehend the factors that affect Saudi SMEs' adoption of
cloud computing services. The pre-stage of descriptive and statistical data analysis is
cleaning and screening the data to detect missing data or outliers and to determine its
normality, which will be outlined in the following points.

    •   Missing Data Treatment

Missing data indicates that part of the variable is missing, which makes the variable value
invalid. An invalid value may consist of either an incorrect or non-existent value. Rubin &
Little (2019) defined the missing data as a variable that hides the meaningful value. Any
missing data that is less than 10% of the collected data is not considered significant and
can be removed (Parent, 2013). Since this research collected quantitative data through
electronic questionnaires, some responses may have missing data. Although the
researcher has focused on avoiding this problem by designing a questionnaire that
mandates answers to all questions, technical issues still can happen, resulting in missing
data. To tackle this issue, any responses that include missing data were excluded to avoid
biased or erroneous estimations. Section 5.5.1 exposes and discusses in more detail the
missing data in this study.

    •   Outliers Data Treatment

Outlier values are values that significantly diverge from the rest of the observations for
the same variable (Hawkins, 1980). Outliers are expected to occur in quantitative data.
They can be caused by mistakes or either by random and extreme responses. There are
several techniques that can be employed to ensure that the collected data are free from
the outliers' values, such as visual inspection and computing the Z-value (Hawkins, 1980;
Tabachnick et al., 2007). This study employed descriptive statistical analysis to calculate
the mean and standard deviation values to generate composite scores for each item,
facilitating the identification of outliers. Any potential outliers in this study were detected
using the standardized (Z) score approach. According to the recommendation of
Tabachnick et al. (2007), the Z score value must be in the range between 3.29 and -3.29




                                             109
to avoid outliers. Then, various approaches can be used to treat detected outliers,
depending on the nature of the data and the study's goal.

Three popular approaches for treating outliers are Trimming, Winsorization and Robust
Estimation (Kwak & Kim, 2017). Trimming can be used to exclude the outliers' values.
Winsorization can be used to adjust the weights of outliers or substitute the data being
examined for outliers with anticipated values. When the population distribution is known,
Robust Estimation can be used to provide consistent estimators that are robust to outliers.
The existence of the outlier values could affect the analysis procedure and produce a
biased conclusion. Therefore, fixing and treating outliers should be done before the main
analysis to ensure the conclusion is based on accurate and reliable data (Aggarwal &
Sathe, 2015). Section 5.5.2 declares and discusses in more detail any potential outliers in
this study.

4.8.1.1 Descriptive Analysis

The quantitative data uses descriptive statistics to generate understood information about
the study's target sample population. According to Mishra et al. (2019), descriptive
statistics is a method employed to extract and summarize meaningful information from a
large dataset so that it can be easily comprehended. The advantage of descriptive statistics
is that they can describe and represent a massive amount of data in an easily understood
way. A descriptive analysis test to assess the data's normality and distribution should be
conducted before the statistical analysis stage. The primary indicators that can be
employed to ensure the data quality and their distribution are measurements of central
tendency (such as mean and median) and measurements of variation (such as standard
deviation and variance) (Hair et al., 2013). Analyzing the frequency distribution for each
variable leads to determining individual respondents' scores. Using a measure of central
tendency can achieve a more comprehensive picture by briefly summarising the attributes
of a variable into a single statistical measure. In addition, during this stage of descriptive
analysis, it is crucial to assess the data normality by employing skewness and kurtosis
measurements to calculate the variables' values to ensure their normality. It can be
said that the data is distributed normally if the skewness and kurtosis values are by a value
of ± 2.0 (Hahs-Vaughn & Lomax, 2013; Piaw, 2008). Sections 5.5.3 and 5.6 will clarify
and highlight the descriptive analysis and the measurements used in more detail.




                                             110
4.8.1.2 Statistical Analysis

Statistical analysis is used to test the hypotheses. The results are then used to draw
conclusions about the intentions of the selected sample of the population regarding the
phenomenon under investigation. Before starting statistical analysis, it is crucial to verify
the validity and reliability of the model. For this purpose, the Confirmatory Factor
Analysis (CFA) Model is employed. The CFA model assesses each construct's uni-
dimensionality, reliability and validity. Then, any item with a loading factor of 0.50 or
below is discarded (Hair et al., 2006; Papanastasiou, 2005). In addition, famous metrics
that are known to measure how well the model fits the data can be used, such as Chi-
Square (CMIN), Comparative Fit Index (CFI), Goodness of Fit Index (GFI), Tucker-
Lewis index (TLI), Root Mean Square Error of Approximation (RMSEA), and P of Close
Fit (PCLOSE) (Awang et al., 2015; Eisenberger et al., 1997). The Statistical Software for
the Social Sciences (SPSS) version 28 was used for the consistent validity test. At the
same time, the CFA evaluated the model's fitness using the Analysis of Moment Structure
(AMOS) software version 28. Employing these instruments before the statistical analysis
provides evidence of the validity and reliability of the model's uni-dimensional,
discriminant and convergent features. For more details, see sections 5.8 and 5.9 in Chapter
5.

After that, the researcher moves to the next stage by employing the structural equation
model (SEM) to test the relationship between the hypothesised components of the
theoretical model and whether they are significantly related (accepted or rejected). The
assessment method of the structural model uses the previously mentioned indices to
assess the level of fit between the model and the data, as well as standardised versions of
regression weights. During these assessments, the hypotheses were examined to
determine which had significant effects and which did not; and ascertain whether the
evidence had substantiated the hypothesised connections. If there is a positive or negative
relationship between each independent variable and the dependent variable, it shows in
the direction of the regression coefficient (beta estimate). If the coefficient is positive, it
means that when the number of independent variables increases, the mean of the
dependent variable increases, too. A negative coefficient, on the other hand, implies that
the dependent variable's mean tends to decrease when the independent variable increases
(Chin, 1998). Additionally, the results must meet a significant level with a threshold of
p-value, usually P < 0.05, to be considered relevant (Ho et al., 2019). In this case, the p-


                                             111
value will be calculated then; if p < 0.05, the hypothesis will be accepted, and If p > 0.05
will be rejected. More explanations and details will be highlighted in Section 5.11 in
Chapter 5.

4.8.2 Qualitative Analysis
Qualitative analysis differs from quantitative analysis as no specific tests, general
techniques, or methods can be generalised for conducting qualitative data analysis. The
researchers' high skills in analytical thinking and observations are considered the key to
the success of qualitative data analysis. However, various methods may be used to
identify the appropriate patterns and relationships in qualitative data. These methods
include narrative, content and thematic analysis to identify recurring phrases and
frequently used terms (Braun & Clarke, 2006; Krippendorff, 2018; Riessman, 2008). This
research used thematic analysis to identify relevant themes from the data gathered via
semi-structured interviews. Thematic analysis is a method used to identify, analyse and
uncover patterns or themes within data, that can produce accurate findings in qualitative
data analysis (Vaismoradi et al., 2016). This approach involves executing a multi-step
process that involves the researcher becoming familiar with the data, generating initial
codes that describe the data to organise them, and searching for themes by transforming
the codes into themes. NVivo software was used as a tool for thematic analysis. It
examined the gathered data, identified their interrelationships, and constructed
appropriate themes.


    4.9 Ethical Considerations
Research ethics are the rules, standards and guidelines that should be followed when
conducting the research (Shamoo & Resnik, 2009). The research's ethical concerns adhere
to many existing criteria, including those contained in: “Internet Research: Ethical
Guidelines 3.0” (AoIR, 2019), “Data Protection Act of 1998” (UK Parliament, 2015), and
“Framework for research ethics - - ESRC” (UK Research and Innovation, 2022). In
addition, this research fully follows and sticks to the ethical rules and guidelines set by
the University of Sussex and received ethical approval for this study with application
number ER/MA2251/3 (See Appendix B).

The researcher has addressed all the concerns regarding ethical issues by ensuring the
confidentiality and privacy of participants while collecting data, either through


                                            112
questionnaires or semi-structured interviews. A consent form with an information sheet
accompanying the questionnaire and interviews provides the research objectives and all
the information needed by participants. Participants are informed of their right to
withdraw from the study at any time and how their data will be kept secure. All these
details are shown in the information sheet and consent forms (See Appendix C and D).


    4.10 Summary
This chapter explains the methodology followed and highlights the methods employed in
this study. This study employs a mixed-method approach, using quantitative
(questionnaire) and qualitative (semi-structured interview) methods to evaluate the
developed model (see Chapter 3) regarding the intention to adopt cloud computing
services by Saudi SMEs from the perspectives of top managers, IT managers and
employees. This chapter describes the techniques and tools used to collect and analyse
the data and how they are used. In addition, it describes the main method employed along
with the complementary method and the ethical considerations. Next, Chapter 5 will
discuss the survey findings, and then Chapter 6 will discuss the interview findings.
Finally, Chapter 7 will discuss the results and provide a conclusion for both stages of the
study.




                                           113
Chapter
5 Quantitative Data Analysis
    5.1 Introduction
As shown in Figure 5.1, this study applies the quantitative method as the primary method
by collecting primary survey data to test and investigate the factors that impact the
adoption of cloud computing services by Saudi SMEs. This chapter discusses the
quantitative data analysis and provides a general overview of the fieldwork that was
conducted to collect data from IT managers, top managers and employees of SMEs in
Riyadh, Saudi Arabia. Moreover, the chapter concentrates on explaining how data was
correctly collected using descriptive statistics, testing hypotheses using structural
equation modelling (SEM) in conjunction with the applied methods for confirming factor
analysis for the constructs, and confirming the proposed research framework using
AMOS 25 by applying the structural model fit method.




                          Figure 5-1 Stage 5: Data Analysis




                                          114
    5.2 The Pilot Study
The questions and instructions for the primary survey were tested in a pilot study first,
carried out in Saudi Arabia with the participation of IT managers, top managers and staff
members of Saudi SMEs. There was a total of 66 questionnaires used in the pilot project;
11 were used to collect demographic data on the participants and their respective SMEs,
and the remaining 55 were used to assess the influence of various factors on the adoption
of cloud computing services among Saudi SMEs. The pilot study was sent to the
participants with the intention of gathering 20 responses. However, 27 responses were
actually filled out and returned, which helped to evaluate the questionnaire draft. The
primary objective of the pilot study was to determine whether the questionnaires and
instructions could be easily understood and filled out without any problems and whether
the allotted time was sufficient to complete the questionnaires. Therefore, the pilot study's
findings were beneficial because they yielded interesting comments from the participants
but did not result in any change in the questions.



    5.3 The Sampling Procedure Design
The population chosen as the study's objective included IT managers, top managers and
employees of SMEs in Riyadh, Saudi Arabia, between the 5th of September 2022 to the
3rd of December 2022. The target population selected in Riyadh exceeds 100,000 IT
managers, top managers and SMEs employees, according to a report from the Small and
Medium Enterprise General Authority in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia. The sample size
can be calculated using various techniques following the determination of the confidence
level beside the margin of error. Therefore, based on the technique recommended by
Saunders et al. (2009), the sample size for this study was calculated as 384, with a level
of confidence of 95% and a margin of error of 5% (see Table 5.1).

    Table 5-1: Sample sizes for various population sizes (Saunders et al., 2009)

As shown in Table 5.1, selecting the correct sample sizes is straightforward when one
knows the target population number and determines the confidence level and margin of
error. The above table shows the sample sizes for various population sizes at a 95%
confidence level and with a margin of error from 5% to 1%. Therefore, according to the
study data available and the target population sizes, the sample size for this study should
be 384, with a 95% confidence level and a 5% margin of error.


    5.4 The Method of Sampling Chosen
This research stage uses a scientific method to choose the sample for quantitative data,
ensuring that bias is avoided to obtain reliable and accurate data. The study's target
population was all IT managers, top managers and SME employees in Riyadh city, Saudi
Arabia. Based on the distribution of Saudi SMEs in Riyadh and the report from the Small
and Medium Enterprise General Authority in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, the sample
was chosen randomly in 2022 from among Saudi SMEs, and later augmented with a
snowball sample using friends and colleagues with contacts in SMEs. The link to a web-


                                           116
based survey was made and sent to each SMEs sample member separately. Following
completion, each response was saved immediately into the database.


    5.5 The Screening of Data
The screening of the data focuses on the data collected before starting the analysis stage
to ensure the data is reliable and valid. Two processes need to be carried out to verify the
accuracy of the data obtained via this method. These include checking for missing data
and carrying out the normality test. These two procedures verify that the data is correct
and valid for advanced analysis before heading on to the next phase, which is doing the
SEM analysis.

5.5.1 The Missing Data

Having data missing means that there is an invalid value for a variable that would cause
an incorrect analysis, and an invalid value could be a wrong value or an inexistent value.
Rubin & Little (2019) defined it as follows: “Missing data are unobserved values that
would be meaningful for analysis if observed; in other words, a missing value hides a
meaningful value”. According to Parent (2013), any missing data representing less than
10% of the total data is a low percentage, does not make a big difference, and can be
disregarded. More than 500 questionnaires were randomly distributed over three months,
from the 5th of September 2022 to the 3rd of December 2022, to the IT managers, top
managers and SMEs employees in Riyadh, Saudi Arabia; 440 (88%) out of 500 were
filled out and returned. However, of these responses returned, 28 (6,36%) were
considered invalid, as they had missing data for variables and would affect the analysis
process, and 412 (93.64%) were complete and accurate, so were used.



5.5.2 The Outliers Detection Approach
Outliers are values that differ from the rest of the values. Outliers are defined as an
observation that differs so significantly from other observations that it raises questions
about whether a separate mechanism produced it, and is referred to as an ‘outlier’
(Hawkins, 1980). This part of this research used descriptive statistical analysis to examine
means and standard deviation values to compute the composite score for all items
involved for each factor to detect any outliers The standardised score (Z score) technique



                                            117
was used to identify outliers. Moreover, the Z score value must be in the range between
3.29 and -3.29 to avoid outliers (Tabachnick et al., 2007). Therefore, the Z score was
computed for all items, and no outliers were found as the maximum Z score was 2.02 and
the minimum was -2.95. Furthermore, the maximum standard deviation was 1.38, and the
minimum was 1.00.

5.5.3 The Normality Test
In this phase of the data analysis, it is essential to check the normality of the data through
the population data if they normal are distributed or not. The skewness and kurtosis are
used to compute the variables' values related to the factors and identify whether the
variables' values are normally distributed. The data may therefore be said to have a normal
distribution if the skewness and kurtosis values are normal by a value of ± 2.0 (Hahs-
Vaughn & Lomax, 2013; Piaw, 2008). Therefore, based on the computed data in the
following Table 5.2, the data are distributed normally, and within the acceptable range.

                                            124
      5.6 The Descriptive Statistics
Descriptive statistics are the technique used to summarise the data from the most
extensive data set to a condensed set that can be communicated and understood as much
as possible (Mishra et al., 2019). Moreover, descriptive statistics help understand the data
more comprehensively by summarising it in tables and graphics that enable the reader to
understand it smoothly and efficiently. Descriptive statistics include a group of statistical
methods such as mean, standard deviation, frequency, skewness, kurtosis and mode. This
study used descriptive statistics to describe the attitudes of Saudi SMEs regarding
adopting cloud computing services.

5.6.1 The Demographic Data
Furthermore, before diving into the depths of the statistical data, the following sections
will present the demographic data of the study participants to build a profile of survey
respondents and investigate whether these demographic factors in the sample were
representative of the target population.

Age

The results in Table 5.4 and illustrated in Figure 5.2 show that 15.5% were between 18-
25 years, 36.9% were between 26-35 years 31.1% were between 36-45, and 16.5% were
46 or older. Based on the last published data from General Authority for Statistics in
Saudi Arabia in 2021, the total population under the age of 40 was 24,188,384, which
represents 69.08% of the total population, and the median age of the population was
between 30-34 years (GAS, 2021). Therefore, the study participants' median age falls in
the age group between 26-35 years, which is consistent with the target population's
median age.

The participants were asked if their organisations were familiar with the term ‘cloud

These results indicated that in the case of participants who answered ‘yes’, their
organisations might be ready to consider the adoption of the Cloud as they already knew
about it. In contrast, the participants who answered ‘no’ could be considered as an
indication that their organisations needed to at least become more familiar with the term
‘Cloud’ before they would consider adopting cloud computing services.

Cloud Adoption

The participants were also asked whether their organisations had adopted cloud services;
and 92 (22.3%) answered ‘yes’, and 320 (77.7%) responded ‘no’ (see Table 5.13). The
participants who answered ‘yes’ were asked an additional question about the type of cloud
their organisation used. Out of 92, 20 (21.3%) used public cloud, 27 (28.7%) used private
cloud, 1 (1.1 %) used community cloud, 6 (6.4%) used hybrid Cloud, and 38 (41.30%)
did not know which type their organisation used (see Table 5.14).

Therefore, the percentage of the SMEs participants whose organisations had adopted
cloud services was 22.3% of all participants, which can be considered a low percentage.
In addition, 41.3 % of those whose SMEs had adopted the Cloud had yet to learn which
type their organisations had adopted, which hints that they may still be at an early stage
of adoption.

    5.7 The         Data       Analysis             and      Measurement          Model
          Assessment
It is essential to ensure the validity and reliability of the model utilised through many
measurement processes to identify its quality before going on to further statistical
analysis. Therefore, this section shows the details about the instruments used for
Confirmatory Factor Analysis (CFA), and describes both convergent and discriminant
validity. For the consistent validity test, the Statistical Software for the Social Sciences
(SPSS) version 28 was employed. At the same time, the CFA assessed the model's fitness
by employing the Analysis of Moment Structure (AMOS) version 28. The use of these
instruments assisted in confirming the validity and reliability of the model's
unidimensional, discriminant, and convergent properties, before going on to test the
hypotheses.

5.7.1 Confirmatory Factor Analysis (CFA)
Confirmatory Factor Analysis is considered an effective pattern that verifies the factors
through variable load testing, which is carried out and emphasised in order to determine
the relationship between observed variables and the theoretical definition of the factors


                                              135
(Perry et al., 2015). Before receiving any results, the researcher must identify the correct
factors to decide whether or not to accept the theory being investigated by picking the
proper theory being researched. Following the CFA, the results influence decisions about
the theory, and the procedure logically has two options: supported theory (accepted) or
unsupported theory (rejected) (Perry et al., 2015).

Furthermore, when the researcher decides to employ the CFA, it should evaluate the uni-
dimensionality, reliability and validity of each construct in the measurement model. To
determine if each variable measures a single underlying feature, a latent measurement
model for each construct, including external and internal factors, should be established.
This is a critical stage in carrying out a technique to determine uni-dimensionality. It is
critical to establish uni-dimensionality before proceeding with structural equation
modelling, which entails removing any signs with factor loadings less than 0.60
(Afthanorhan et al., 2014).

As illustrated in Figure 5.10, the CFA findings indicated 18 latent constructs, which are
the intention to Adopt Cloud computing services (ITAC), Data Confidentiality (DC),
Data Integrity (DI), Data Security (DS), Relative advantages (RA), Service Cost (SC),
Service Quality(SQ), Organisation Regulation (OR), Organisation Size(OS), Technology
Readiness (TR), Top Management Support (TM), Technology Infrastructure (TI), Cloud
provider (CP), Legal Framework (LF), Enterprise Competitiveness (EC), Culture (CL),
Awareness (AW), Trust (TU). To investigate the perceived efficacy of using cloud
computing services by Saudi SMEs, 18 constructs and 55 items were used. Table 5.15
shows the findings of the CFA that applied to each construct and its connected items,
revealing the latent variables and their relevant measurements.

                   Table 5-15:Items Associated with each construct

               constructs          Factor loading Range          Factor      Code for items

                                  Minimum         Maximum

                                                                                 TU3



As shown in Figure 5.10, the loading factors for all items were larger than 0.60. To ensure
that a measurement model procedure is uni-dimensional, any items to be employed must
have a loading factor of 0.50 or above, while any items with a loading factor of 0.50 or
below must be disregarded and removed from consideration (Hair et al., 2006;
Papanastasiou, 2005). Furthermore, the link between indicators and individual latent


                                           137
variables in AMOS is referred to as standardised factor loadings. The standardised
estimation parameters vary from -1.0 to +1.0, and for estimations of all factors loading, a
minimum common criterion of 0.5 should be used (Hair et al., 2006), as shown in Table
5.15, which displays standardised loading estimations for all variables.




           Figure 5-10: The Confirmatory Factor Analysis (CFA) Model
As a result, as shown in Figure 5.10 above, statistically, the loading estimate assures that
the model has a high measurement load rate.



    5.8 The Measures of the Model Validation
The model validation procedure is critical since it supports and reinforces the model as
well as making sure of the reliability of the model outputs (Kenny, 2015). In the context
of CFA or SEM, the Root Mean Square Error of Approximation (RMSEA) is commonly



                                            138
used to assess how well a model fits the data. It is now the most generally used measure
of model fit and appears in nearly all research articles that employ these approaches, and
some scholars even call it the "Ramsey" (Kenny, 2015). It is also called “Ramsey”, in
honour of its creator, Peter M. Ramsey. It is recommended to examine a model's validity,
which includes the two categories of validity—convergent validity and discriminant
validity, all of which fall under the category of construct validity—to determine the
model's overall fitness (Atkinson et al., 2011). Thus, construct validity is the method that
most researchers have used for assessing model fit indices. Many metrics may be used to
determine how well a model fits data, but the Comparative Fit Index (CFI), Goodness of
Fit Index (GFI), and Tucker-Lewis index (TLI) are all good places to start (Eisenberger
et al., 1997). Therefore, at this point, the model fit cannot be said to be fully reliable or
accurate until construct validity is achieved, which still has not been and needs some
constraints in the next phase to achieve it.

The result of the analysis revealed that a chi-squared (χ2) with a value of 1832.29 was
achieved for this study with 1277 as the value of degrees of freedom and the value of the
probability as less than 0.001. Nevertheless, the Chi-square χ2 test, as well as the p-value,
might be affected by the size of the sample, influencing how well the model fits the data,
even though this research has satisfied the ratio value of CMIN/DF 1.43 attained by it as
an outcome, which is less than 3 and considered as an acceptable fit (Kline, 2023).
Therefore, different indices and criteria were used to determine and assess how well the
model fits the data (Awang et al., 2015) as shown in Table 5.16.

            Table 5-16: Indices and Standards Assessments of Model Fit

5.8.1 Convergent Validity
To continue with the convergent validity evaluation, the model has to have a good fit, and
the AVE value needs to be more than 0.5. (Hair et al., 2006). A high AVE indicates a
robust relationship between the latent and single variables. Awang et al. (2015) and Kline,
(2013) proposed values for most indices as thresholds for them, which are as follows: the
CMIN/DF value should be between 1 and 5, the CFI value should be ³ 90, the RMSEA
should be £ 0.06, and the SRMR should be £ 0.08; and when the thresholds are met, the
model fit is considered acceptable. However, several fit indices can be used to determine
whether or not the provided sample data and hypothetical model are sufficient for the
analysis at hand. Still, those are the most used indices by most researchers, as they focus
on determining whether the model indicates a good fit based on the scales provided by
each indicator.

As a result, the model fit measurements for this research came out to be as follows:
CMIN/DF = 1.435, CFI = 0.949, SRMR = 0.053, RMSEA = 0.033, and P Close = 1.000.
These are the results that were accomplished. As shown in Table 5.17, the model was not
fit at that point because the measurements still had not satisfied the condition of a good
model fit. Consequently, the model could not be used for further analysis, as the findings
of the CFA and the AVE results across the model showed that this is not permissible.
Therefore, the model required additional refinement to reach the recommended level.

     5.8.2 Discriminant Validity
     When describing the ability to differentiate one construct from others of the same kind,
     the term "discriminant validity" is used. When dealing with two constructs or more, this
     study assesses the discriminant validity using the recommendation by Hair et al. (2006)
     to link the value of squared correlation estimates with Average Variance Extracted (AVE)
     values. Furthermore, the AVE measure is likely to outperform the squared correlation
     estimates. As shown in Table 5.18, there were no issues with discriminant validity.
     Fornell-Larker criteria and cross-loadings matrix were utilised by the researcher (Fornell
     & Larcker, 1981). Furthermore, the criteria for both discriminant and convergent validity
     were well met by the model's CFA assessment.

      constructs possess discriminant validity.

      5.8.3 Construct Validity and Reliability
      Before hypothesis testing and conducting in-depth research, Cronbach's alpha and
      Composite Reliability (CR) were used to ensure the quality and dependability of the data,
      thereby ensuring the validity and reliability of the construct. In this context, validity
      means “the degree to which a measure accurately represents what it is supposed to”, and
      reliability means “the degree to which the observed variable measures the ‘true’ value
      and is error free”(Hair et al., 2013). Furthermore, dependable and consistent outcomes
      are what is meant when discussing reliability. In this setting, it is possible to anticipate
      consistent findings, which increases the trustworthiness of a particular survey instrument.

      When evaluating consistency, looking at how similarly two scales measure a given point
      is beneficial (Hair et al., 2006). A reliable instrument will consistently provide the same
      result when tested over time. Cronbach's alpha was used to establish the reliability level
      of this research, and many researchers have suggested its value should be greater than
      0.70 (Taber, 2018) . It has been said that Cronbach's alpha is undoubtedly one of the most


                                                                142
significant and widespread statistics utilised in studies addressing test construction
(Cronbach, 1951). Also, a reliability assessment known as composite reliability (CR) was
used in this study. For the composite reliability to be considered satisfactory, the CR must
be greater than 0.70. Table 5.19 shows the construct validity and reliability.

In addition, random measurement error contributions to the variation in the latent
variables may be identified using the average variance extracted (AVE). Realistic average
AVE estimations need to be 0.5 or greater. The results show all AVEs for constructs
ranged from 0.70 to 0.78. Maximum Shared Squared Variance (MSV) is another
suggested criterion measurement for discriminant validity; it should be lower than the
AVE result as recommended by Hair et al. (2013). Table 5.19 displays the MSV data,
demonstrating the validity of the measurement methodology. The extracted average
variance was calculated using the following formula.

obtained.

    5.9 After Required Constraints for CFA
The original model shown in Figure 5.10, which displayed an unsatisfactory fit, has been
transformed into the model presented in Figure 5.11, which exhibits a good fit, acceptable
due to the covariation of error terms. Following the application of constraints. the CFA
model may be seen in Figure 5.11. Consequently, modification indices (MI) underwent
several changes due to these revisions. Multiple modifications were implemented to
achieve the desired level of fitness index, which involved covarying the error terms and
making necessary adjustments.




                                           144
 Figure 5-11: The Confirmatory Factor Analysis (CFA) Model After Constraints
Furthermore, a thorough evaluation of modification indices was conducted to mitigate the
potential for item redundancy in assessing a given construct. Thus, it was observed that
ITAC4 and LF3 exhibit a strong association with other variables that possess a
considerable amount of residual variance. Consequently, the error terms have been
adjusted through covariance.

5.9.1 Convergent Validity

As discussed, convergent validity can only be achieved if the model fit has been
satisfactory. The AVE has to be greater than 0.50. In addition, according to Hair et al.
(2013), in order to determine whether or not a model is a good fit for the data that was
obtained from more than 250 participants, the chi-square statistic must be less than 3. In
this particular investigation, information was gathered from a total of 412 participants,
resulting in a chi-square value of 1764.4. All of the model fit measurements reached the


                                            145
recommended values, including CFI = 0.956, SRMR = 0.053 and RMSEA = 0.031. This
means that the needed degree of model fit at this stage has been obtained, as shown in

5.9.2 Discriminant Validity

According to Hair et al. (2013), the evaluation of discriminant validity comprises three
distinct benchmarks, namely the Maximum Shared Squared Variance (MSV), the
Fornell-Larcker test, and the Average Shared Squared Variance (ASV). Table 5.21
illustrates that the obtained square root of the Average Variance Extracted (AVE) value
for the constructs was greater than the correlation estimates. The current stage
endeavours to conduct a comparative evaluation of the value of absolute correlation
among constructs and the square root of the average variance extracted (AVE) for each
construct. According to the criterion established by Fornell and Larcker (1981), if the


                                              146
      correlations are less than the square root of the construct's average variance extracted,
      then it is improper to argue that a model has attained discriminant validity. However,
      the model demonstrates discriminant validity as evidenced by the "diagonal cells" of
      the Average Variance Extracted square root exhibiting a greater value than the
      correlations among constructs.

reliability of each construct based on the standards of Cronbach's alpha. According to
the findings presented in Table 5.24, the composite reliabilities of all constructs in the
model estimates constructs' reliability to range from 0.74 to 0.84. Thus, the composite
reliability of the measurement model under investigation exhibits internal consistency
exceeding the widely acknowledged threshold of 0.70. At this point, the ultimate
structural - path model was successfully constructed. According to the Raykov (1997),
the present investigation employed the following formula to compute composite

    5.10 Structural Equation Model (SEM)
This section will test the hypothesis by the structural equation modelling testing that
shows the relationship between empirically-tested constructs and whether they are
significantly related or not to each other.

5.10.1 Criteria for Evaluation and Procedure

As the measurement model assessments were successful, the researcher moved to the next
stage, which is evaluating the structural model to investigate the connections between the
hypothesised components of the theoretical model. The structural and measurement
models are not identical; instead, there is a distinction between the two. In the
measurement model, the focus is placed on assessment, which demonstrates the links
between constructs. Still, in the structural model, measured variables are used to illustrate
how significant the relationships between constructs are. As discussed earlier in Chapter
3, and shown in the conceptual model of the TOE, the relevance of the hypothesised
linkages between the components are shown using causal arrows.


                                              158
Appendix A illustrates the comprehensive suggested structural model, which considers
both the component structures and the hypothesised linkages. According to Hayes and
Preacher (2014), the aim of hypothesis testing is to identify the extent to which predictors,
also known as independent variables, contribute to explaining the dependent variables
being studied. Overall, the model identified the following exogenous (independent)
constructs: Data Confidentiality (DC), Data Integrity (DI), Data Security (DS), Relative
advantages (RA), Service Cost (SC), Service Quality (SQ), Organisation Regulation
(OR), Organisation Size (OS), Technology Readiness (TR), Top Management Support
(TM), Technology Infrastructure (TI), Cloud provider (CP), Legal Framework (LF),
Enterprise Competitiveness (EC), Culture (CL), Awareness (AW), and Trust (TU). The
endogenous (dependent) construct is then identified as the Intention to Adopt Cloud
computing services (ITAC).

The structural model's evaluation process uses indices that measure how well the model
fits the data and standardised versions of the path coefficients. Through these evaluations,
it was possible to see which hypotheses have supporting evidence and which do not. In
this part, the criteria for the model fit indices used are similar to those adopted earlier in
this chapter, and determined whether the evidence supported the hypothesised linkages.
The p-value needs to be at a significant level with a threshold of p-value, typically P <
0.05, for the findings to be relevant(Ho et al., 2019). The next part is a presentation of the
results of the structural model evaluation.

    5.11 Results of the Structural Model Evaluation
The model indices are fitting as we discussed earlier and shown in Table 5.20, so they
should fit at this stage. However, the researcher prefers to review model fit indices to
ensure it remains fitting every time we modify the model. Therefore, the model indices
in this stage assure that it is at a good level of fit as follows: (χ2 = 1.764, df = 1275, CFI
= 0.956, SRMR = 0.053, and RMSEA = 0.031). In addition, as shown in Table 5.21, AVE
values for every construction show that the standard is over the threshold of 0.50.
Estimates of standardised loadings for all elements are more than 0.5. Error variances
remain positive and related.

The evaluation of the structural model revealed that 12 of the 17 hypotheses are supported
and accepted within the conceptual model that has been developed. In addition, the model




                                              159
was updated to establish the outcome model that matched and described the research data
in the best possible way, which led to even more substantial advances.

5.11.1 Testing Hypotheses and Statistical Analysis

Section 5.5 described the findings of a descriptive analysis of the data collected from
Saudi SMEs regarding cloud computing adoption. The findings highlight the elements
that affect cloud computing adoption. This section makes an effort to provide context for
the data analysis by determining whether or not the assumptions are validated and by
finding the links between crucial elements impacting the adoption of cloud computing in
Saudi SMEs.

5.11.1.1 Data Confidentiality

Three items assess the influence of data confidentiality in Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt
cloud computing. The range model fit was acceptable in the first CFA. In addition, the
degree of model fit was assessed using a variety of metrics. Additionally, all loadings
were more significant than 0.5, indicating a strong enough model fit to include all
components in the final model. Table 5.25 displays the results of the study. The
correlation between DC and ITAC is a positive (β = .094 and P < 0.032). Values of the
crucial ratio for each item were more significant than 1.96. The analysis also indicates
that Saudi SMEs see increased data confidentiality leading to the adoption of cloud
computing. Organisations looking to enhance their IT infrastructure quickly and
effectively should keep Saudi SMEs' data confidential. Therefore, the positive link
hypothesised was validated by the descriptive analysis results for the Data Confidentiality
component and the correlation result. Therefore, based on the previous discussion, the
hypothesis (H1) was supported:

   H1: Increasing the data confidentiality of cloud computing increases the Saudi
SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services. Accepted

           Table 5-25: Hypothesis Path Analysis for Data Confidentiality

5.11.1.2 Data Integrity

Three items were taken into account while evaluating the importance of data integrity on
Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing. The range model fit was found to be
satisfactory in the first CFA. A variety of factors also determined the degree of model fit.
All item loadings were found to be more than 0.5, indicating a satisfactory model fit, as
shown by the statistical analysis. Furthermore, item loadings for the Data Integrity
construct were greater than 0.5 in the confirmatory factor analysis, indicating that all
items should be included in the study. Table 5.25 displays the data showing the positive
correlation between DI and ITAC. According to the data gathered and analysed using the
descriptive test, DI and ITAC are related and positively influenced by one another (β =
.125, P < 0.020). Each item's critical ratio was greater than 1.96 as shown in Table 5.26.
Consequently, there was evidence for the hypothesised positive link. Therefore, in
accordance with the previous discussion, the hypothesis (H2) was supported:

   H2: Increasing the data integrity of cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs’
intention to adopt cloud computing services. Accepted

5.11.1.3 Data Security

Three items were used to assess the impact of data security on Saudi SMEs’ intention to
adopt cloud computing services. Different criteria were used to evaluate the degree of
model fit in the first CFA to see whether the findings indicated a satisfactory model fit.
According to the findings of the descriptive study, the DS factor had no impact on SMEs'
decisions to move to the Cloud. Table 5.27 displays the statistical analysis results
investigating the link between DS and ITAC among Saudi SMEs; the results demonstrate
no statistically significant association (β = -.061, p < 0.289). Table 5.26 demonstrates that
none of the calculated values, including the critical ratios for each component, exceeded
1.96. The results demonstrated no significant effect of the Data Security factor on cloud
computing adoption among Saudi SMEs. The support and assistance the Saudi


                                             161
government has provided to SMEs in data security is mainly responsible for this outcome.
Therefore, based on the previous discussion, the hypothesis (H3) was unsupported:

   H3: Increasing the data security of cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs’
intention to adopt cloud computing services. Rejected

favourable impact on Saudi SMEs' use of cloud computing. Between RA and ITAC, a
positive association exists. The results shown in Table 5.28 suggest that the model was
satisfactory and well-fit (β =.136, p < 0.020), with all derived values being above the
threshold ratio for each item. Therefore, depending on the previous discussion, the
hypothesis (H4) was supported:

   H4: The perceived relative advantages of cloud computing have a positive effect

satisfactory in the first analysis. There were no loadings below 0.5. Table 5.29 reveals
that the critical ratio for each item was not more than 1.96, which is outside the
appropriate and acceptable range. There was no statistically significant link between SC
and ITAC (β = -.191, p < 0.153). Although it was expected that lower service costs would
encourage cloud computing use among Saudi SMEs, this has not been the case, and from
the researcher's point of view, it may be due to Saudi SMEs dedicating a high budget for
IT services and not looking at the cost so much as at the quality. Therefore, in accordance
with the previous discussion, the hypothesis (H5) was unsupported:

   H5: Decreasing the cost of cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to

5.11.1.13       Legal Framework

Three measurement items were developed to assess the impact of the legal framework on
the adoption of cloud computing by Saudi SMEs. A confirmatory factor analysis (CFA)

that the model fit was deemed satisfactory. Furthermore, distinct criteria were employed
to ascertain the degree of data conformity exhibited by the proposed models. The load
values for LF1, LF2, and LF3 were 0.73, 0.86, and 0.67, respectively, as shown in Figure
5.8. Nevertheless, LF3 demonstrated a robust correlation with other variables that exhibit
a substantial degree of residual variance. As a result, the error terms have been modified
using covariance. The decision was made to covariate LF3 with LF1 to achieve a good
model fit, as shown in Figure 5.9. Subsequently, the load values changed: LF1 is 0.75,
LF2 0.85, and LF3 0.69. The results of the second analysis indicated that the model fit
was deemed acceptable. Upon conducting a subsequent analysis, as shown in Table 5.37,
the researcher observed a noteworthy and statistically correlation between LF and ITAC
(β = .154, p < 0.023). Furthermore, all obtained values, including the critical ratio for each
item, exceeded the threshold of 1.96, indicating that the model was deemed appropriate
and satisfactory. This implies a positive relationship between Legal Framework and Saudi
SMEs' adoption of cloud computing. Therefore, in accordance with the previous
discussion, the hypothesis (H13) was supported:

   H13: Obtaining an organised legal framework of cloud computing increases Saudi
SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services. Accepted

acceptable range of values. The findings therefore indicated that the model was acceptable
and fit, but there was no effect or relationship between Enterprise Competitiveness and
adopting cloud computing in Saudi SMEs. Therefore, in view of the previous discussion,
the hypothesis (H14) was unsupported:

   H14: Increasing the enterprise competitiveness increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention
to adopt cloud computing services. Rejected

       Table 5-38: Hypothesis Path Analysis for Enterprise Competitiveness

5.11.1.15       Culture

Three items were used to evaluate the culture of Saudi SMEs staff regarding cloud
computing adoption. The results of the first CFA indicated that the range model fit was
satisfactory. In addition, the degree to which the data matched the model was determined
based on various factors. The study's findings showed that the loadings for all items were
more than 0.5, indicating that the model fit was satisfactory. Table 5.39 summarises the
findings, indicating no relationship or association between CL and ITAC. The descriptive
analysis results showed no influence correlation between CL and ITAC (β = .145, p <
0.446). In addition, the findings demonstrated that all of the acquired values, such as the
critical value for every one of the variables was not (>=1.96), which is outside the
permissible range. Therefore, the results of the descriptive analysis for this component
and the correlation result shows no relationship between Culture and the adoption of cloud
computing in Saudi SMEs. Therefore, based on the previous discussion, the hypothesis
(H15) was unsupported:




                                            169
   H15: Increasing the technology culture for IT managers, top managers and
employees increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services.
Rejected

5.11.1.16       Awareness

Three questions were used to assess Saudi SMEs' awareness of cloud computing
adoption. The range model fit was acceptable, according to the initial CFA. Furthermore,
multiple criteria were employed to establish the level of model fit. Moreover, the research
revealed that the loadings for all items were more significant than 0.5, indicating that the
model fit was good enough to include all elements. Table 5.40 displays the findings. The
route from AW to ITAC had a positive effect (β = .139, p < 0.043), indicating that all
derived values, including the crucial ratio for each item, were substantially within the
range, indicating that the model was acceptable and fitting. This implies that Saudi SMEs
feel embracing cloud computing depends on their employees' awareness. Saudi SMEs
should be aware of the necessity to embrace cloud services effectively to fulfil the
demands of organisations.       Consequently, the descriptive analysis results of the
Awareness component and the correlation result stated a positive correlation with cloud
computing adoption. Therefore, in relation to the previous discussion, the hypothesis
(H16) was supported:

   H16: Increasing the awareness of the IT managers, top managers and employees
increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services. Accepted

5.11.1.17       Trust

Three items were used to investigate the relationship between trust and the adoption of
cloud computing by Saudi SMEs. The results showed that all of the item loadings were
more than 0.5. The CFA was carried out so that model fitness could be evaluated. The
analysis was carried out, and a satisfactory model fit was found. According to the findings
shown in Table 5.41, there is a positive relationship between Trust and the adoption of
cloud computing in Saudi SMEs. Furthermore, the noteworthy related relationship, as
shown by the critical value obtained between TU and ITAC (β = .105, p < 0.005),
demonstrates that all acquired values, including the crucial value for every single item,
are greater than or equal to 1.96, and were effectively in the range of values, which
indicates that the model appropriately fitted and seems to be acceptable. This indicates
the Trust construct significantly impacts the level to which Saudi SMEs adopt cloud
computing. Therefore, in accordance with the previous discussion, the hypothesis (H17)
was supported:

    H17: Increasing the trust of the IT managers, top managers and employees increases
the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services. Accepted

    5.12 Proposed Modified Framework (ACCM-SME)
The framework was adjusted in light of the data analysis that showed the hypotheses to
be correct. The reported findings are ranked according to the many hypotheses tested.
Table 5.42 shows the results, which reveal that 12 out of the 17 hypothesised path
coefficients were supported and statistically significant. In addition, it shows the
structural model's path loadings, P values, Critical Ratios (C.R) and Standard Errors
(S.E.).

Data Security (DS) (P=0.289), Service Cost (SC) (P=0.151), Organisation Size (OS)
(P=0.948), Enterprise Competitiveness (EC) (P=0.768), and Culture (CL) (P=0.446) were
the independent factors that did not significantly affect Saudi SMEs' adoption of cloud
computing, as shown in Table 5.42. In addition, the value of P=0.289 indicates that the
null hypothesis H3, "Increasing the data security of cloud computing increases the Saudi
SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services" was rejected, indicating that the data
security had no impact on the decision of the Saudi SMEs to adopt cloud
computing services.



                                                172
Similarly, the null hypothesis H5, "Decreasing the cost of cloud computing increases the
Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services", was rejected with a value of
P=0.470, indicating that the cost associated with cloud computing had no significant
impact on the decisions of Saudi SMEs to adopt cloud computing. This outcome may be
attributed to the Saudi government's assistance and expertise in information technology
(IT), which has helped the private sector improve its data security practices. In addition,
the fact that the Saudi organisation did not prioritise saving money when it came to
transforming into new technologies is evidence that they value innovation and
competitiveness above all else.

Moreover, the null hypothesis H8, “A smaller organisation size is more likely to increase
the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services”, was rejected with the
value of P=0.948, showing that the organisation size does not influence the intention of
Saudi SMEs toward adoption of cloud computing services. In addition, the value of
P=0.768 showed that the null hypothesis H14, “Increasing the enterprise competitiveness
increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services”, was rejected,
indicating that the enterprise competitiveness does not have an impact on the intention to
adopt cloud computing services by Saudi SMEs. The Culture factor also seems not to
affect the intention of Saudi SMEs to adopt cloud computing services since the null
hypothesis H15, “Increasing the technology culture for IT managers, top managers, and
employees increases the Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services”, was
rejected with the value P=0.446.

Table 5.43 illustrates which hypotheses were accepted and which were rejected. The
literature review revealed that nations with varying levels of IT experience had varying
degrees of concern about adopting cloud computing. Consequently, the empirical data



                                                  174
findings have special significance and add to our knowledge of the essential components
of Saudi SMEs. Thus, this research helps to close a knowledge gap and expand our
understanding of cloud computing's penetration among Saudi Arabia's SMEs. These
findings also show notable variations from previous research, which may be attributable
to regional distinctions in organisational climate and intended audiences. Based on a study
of the actual data and the structural equation model, the researcher concluded that most
hypothesised relationships were supported and accepted, as shown in Figure 5.12 below,
which represents the study framework after analysing the data.




       Figure 5-12: Modified Conceptual Framework Based on the Findings



    5.13 Summary
This chapter has outlined the quantitative data results from the primary method of this
study. It has explained and illustrated the approach applied with the advanced quantitative
analysis for the proposed framework (ACCM-SME) to assess whether the hypotheses
were supported and whether the model is fitting. Specifically, the researcher used SPSS
28 and AMOS 28 software analysis programmes to conduct deep quantitative data
analysis investigations. To do a descriptive analysis, SPSS 28 was used. Before
proceeding to more sophisticated quantitative analysis, leading indices had to be


                                           175
conducted in the descriptive analysis to examine the data. Standard deviation, mean,
normality, kurtosis and skewness were used to determine whether or not the data were
normally distributed and free from any outliers. In addition, the researcher used SEM with
AMOS 28 to build the model and check whether it was a good fit. The CFA was used in
the model fit measurement, which measured each construct of the proposed framework
regarding reliability, convergent validity and discriminant validity. Therefore, the path
analysis was assessed based on the structural model fit criteria. This research suggested
the framework (ACCM-SME), which consists of 17 different components and 17
different relationships that were hypothesised based on the literature. The empirical data
analyses revealed that 12 of the 17 hypotheses tested had significant correlations and
positive impacts; however, five hypotheses did not satisfy the criteria specified for this
research and were rejected.

The next chapter will outline the qualitative data analysis from the perspectives of Saudi
SMEs' top and IT managers.




                                           176
Chapter
6 Qualitative Data Analysis
    6.1 Introduction
The outcomes of analysing the qualitative data gathered by semi-structured interviews
are presented in this chapter, see figure 6.1. This technique relies on developing the
questions, putting them between participants' hands, and letting them talk as they want by
recording everything they say. This chapter also provides an analysis and discussion of
the variables and factors identified early by the researcher through the conceptual
framework that was developed.




                        Figure 6-1 Stage 6: Thematic Analysis



NVivo software was used as a theme analysis tool to review the collected data, identify
common ideas in the data and build themes from them. Interviews with 15 top Saudi
SMEs managers and IT managers (see Table 6.1) were conducted and examined


                                           177
thematically to address the study objectives. After conducting a thematic analysis of the
participants’ data, the researcher discovered themes in the respondents' viewpoints and
organised the interview transcripts based on these points. The interviews gathered data
that may be better understood by identifying these themes, which can then be
contextualised using the conceptual framework.

                             Table 6-1: Interviewee Details

This chapter will clearly show the respondents' perspectives on implementing cloud
computing for Saudi SMEs. The participants' opinions about how the implementation of
cloud computing might affect their respective organisations are then investigated. Finally,
questions concerning the proposed framework's components were asked and analysed
based on the following factors, namely: Data Confidentiality (DC), Data Integrity (DI),
Data Security (DS), Relative Advantages (RA), Service Cost (SC), Service Quality (SQ),
Organisation Regulation (OR), Organisation Size (OS), Technology Readiness (TR), Top
Management Support (TM), Technology Infrastructure (TI), Cloud Provider (CP), Legal
Framework (LF), Enterprise Competitiveness (EC), Culture (CL), Awareness (AW), and
Trust (TU).



                                           178
After conducting a deep investigation into the data gathered from interviews using NVivo
software, the researcher found that seven themes showed the perceptions of the
participants, as shown in Figure 6.2, namely: (1) customers’ sensitive data and privacy,
(2) software incompatibility with the Cloud, (3) regulations and top management support,
(4) employees’ expertise in the Cloud, (5) internet outages and shortage of cloud
providers, (6) employees’ resistance to the Cloud and (7) loss of trust in the third party
holding the data (see figure 6.2). This study seeks to discuss each theme individually to
check and identify the challenges that may prevent SMEs from adopting cloud computing
services.




                     Figure 6-2: The Themes from the Interviews



    6.2 Customers’ Sensitive Data and Privacy
The majority of participants were worried about their customers’ data sensitivity and
privacy and believed that this factor played a significant role in adopting cloud
computing, especially when it related to financial data for competitive projects. They
agreed that the guarantee of data sensitivity and privacy should be greater, and this would
encourage the adoption of cloud services, for example P1 explained:




                                           179
    In fact, we do not trust the cloud, as we have sensitive information about the costs of
    projects and quotations that we present to our clients, and we are afraid of accessing
    it and passing it on to competitors in the market, which threatens our success and
    could mean losing projects.

Another interviewee said:

    Sometimes we work with sensitive government agencies that hold us responsible for
    preserving their data and impose legal penalties within the terms of the contracts if
    they are leaked, which makes us maintain that data in a traditional manner that
    ensures that it is not accessed by any third party. (P2)

Data privacy is considered a top level of priority to any customer, especially when dealing
with health:

    In the health sector, we put the privacy of our patients at the top of our list of
    priorities, as most customers do not want their health data seen by any party
    whatsoever, and they prefer that it be kept individually between the patient and the
    health organization, without the presence of other parties to share the data and
    sickness history of the customers. This is what forces us to use our own servers to
    maintain this data. (P8)

Another participant added that:

    Recently, the Saudi government stressed that organisations must not store their data
    outside the borders of Saudi Arabia in order to maintain data privacy and
    confidentiality. This made SMEs confused, as the number of cloud service providers
    in Saudi Arabia is very small, and data privacy and confidentiality services are
    modest. (P3)

Another believed that shifting to the Cloud needs changing some data privacy strategies:

    Some people believe that moving to cloud computing services is very easy, especially
    for organisations. When it comes to data privacy and sensitivity, there is a strategy
    built on the basis of traditional systems, and moving to the Cloud requires
    reformulating those strategies and also formulating a model that helps migrate data
    and preserve it during migration without compromising it. (P15)



                                           180
P4 stated that data privacy with the Cloud is now better than before but needs
enhancement to make all SMEs confident:

    Data privacy and sensitivity is a major concern for many organizations, but at the
    present time we find that the Cloud has improved the tools used more than before,
    and also the government decisions that were issued recently that stress the
    importance of data and its confidentiality have also become a contributing factor that
    forces all service providers to preserve it.

Three participants (P5, P6 and P7) agreed that although the Cloud has made it possible to
access data from anywhere and at any time, it is more vulnerable to hacking and data-
related security threats. They pointed out that this meant a significant challenge for data
privacy, especially if the data were sensitive, and that customers’ data needed to be stored
in a private server to protect it from illegal accessing by third parties. They mentioned
that multiple-factor authentication (MFA) could be used for this purpose. Despite these
safeguards there were still concerns in their organisations that there remained threats to
data privacy related to using cloud services.

Moreover, one of the participants mentioned that exploiting vulnerabilities in service
providers' systems costs a lot:

    Cloud service providers sometimes have security vulnerabilities in their systems that
    enable hackers to obtain customer information, including username and password,
    enabling them to falsify and manipulate sent and received data, which costs
    organisations their reputation and the loss of their customers. (P8)

However, some participants were concerned about data ownership:

    When storing data with cloud service providers, we lose a degree of ownership and
    control of the data, as we do not have direct control over it, but rather through an
    intermediary. This is in addition to the problems related to transferring the data when
    there is a need to transfer it or to end the relationship with the service provider. (P10)

All participants confirmed that increasing the degree of data privacy and protecting it
from illegal access would encourage the adoption of cloud computing services. As
everyone expressed their concern about the sensitive data that their organizations possess,



                                            181
which may affect them financially or reputationally if accessed, and which would incur
huge losses that could lead to the organisation’s deterioration and closure. Therefore,
participants suggested that cloud service providers’ tools be developed regarding data
privacy and that MFA techniques be emphasised, in addition to reviewing data ownership
strategies, which preserves everyone’s rights and helps create a secure environment.


    6.3 Software Incompatibility with the Cloud
The second theme generated from participant interviews was their concerns about
software incompatibility with cloud services. Organisations' top management and
information technology managers explained that an obstacle they sometimes face is the
incompatibility of the programs and interfaces they use for their work with the systems
of cloud computing service providers, which delays the process of adopting cloud
computing. Three participants (P7, P11 and P14) claimed that increased compatibility
leads to a higher rate of cloud adoption. However, there was an issue with modern cloud
computing systems not being compatible with traditional systems. For example, financial
software may have been being used for many years, and could be considered
indispensable by the finance teams, but the software may not be compatible with the
modern versions of most cloud service providers.

Another participant stated that:

    In our organisation, we have had a bad experience with cloud computing with regard
    to software compatibility. In the past, we transferred one of the software programs
    we use to cloud computing and encountered a slow response. At other times, there is
    an error in receiving requests, which costs us a lot of effort and time. (P12)

The proprietary system is considered a barrier to implementing compatibility between
traditional systems and cloud computing. This was mentioned by P13 and P15 who both
mentioned having software that was purchased from a third party, with terms of
agreement not to publish or share any of that software on the Cloud. This was an example
of an obstacle that limits compatibility between SMEs traditional systems and the systems
of cloud service providers.

The design and architecture of some software often requires review and modification
before transferring it to the servers of cloud service providers, which poses a challenge


                                           182
for many SMEs, and slows down the transformation process or may lead to them
abandoning cloud adoption and remaining on traditional systems. Participants P11 and
P14 both mentioned this issue and commented that most SMEs did not have a unified
architecture, due to the acquisition of software from more than one supplier.

IT managers emphasised that each business possesses a distinct work environment and
regularly modifies it to ensure ongoing compatibility with its software. However, these
upgrades are not implemented automatically in order to mitigate potential risks to the
system and its contents. Participants P2 and P11 both remarked that one of the hindrances
impeding the widespread use of cloud computing was the absence of consistent
infrastructure updates by cloud service providers, rendering their systems incompatible
with those of the organisations. Regrettably, cloud service providers' operational
protocols necessitated a systems administrator's involvement in testing and subsequent
updates, which excluded the SMEs from this decision-making process.

Another participant said the testing environment was critical for testing the system before
launch:

   In traditional systems, a subnet is usually used to test new updates through a group
   of computers in the system or virtual computers before launch to ensure their
   compatibility with the system. However, this is not found in many cloud service
   providers in Saudi Arabia. (P13)

Therefore, most participants believe that the compatibility of the infrastructure of cloud
computing service providers with the organisations’ systems is extremely important,
ensuring that errors or incompatibilities do not occur and contributing to the smooth
transmission and processing of data. Participants also pointed out that having a virtual
testing environment for updates and systems allows organisations to simulate their
systems before taking risks.


    6.4 Regulations and Top Management Support
Three participants (P7, P10 and P15) agreed that organisations' regulations and top
management support are considered the barriers that may prevent the organisations from
adopting cloud computing services, as both play a role in the decisions made within
organisations. These participants all commented that their organisations’ regulations had


                                           183
yet to be updated for a transition to cloud computing. The reason for the delay was that
the rules had to be considered from several perspectives, whether legal or commercial, to
ensure that no decision was made that might affect the SME’s workflow. On top of which
they noted that some top managers did not want to adopt cloud computing services and
preferred to stay with traditional systems.

One participant believed that the top management was the key to whether cloud services
were adopted or not:

    Suppose top management desires to bring about any change in the organisation, such
    as adopting cloud computing. In that case, there is no doubt that it has a vital role in
    this, as it can accept updating and accelerating regulations and participate in
    deciding and pushing the whole organisation to adopt cloud computing. (P8)

Another participant added that:

    The top management in our organisation is still very afraid of moving to cloud
    computing due to the currently excellent functioning of our traditional systems, and
    their belief that any transformation process will be accompanied by an interruption
    of services and their impact would negatively affects the organisation. They want to
    avoid any of this happening in their term of management. (P2)

However, one of the participants believed that the process of updating regulations was
better than before:

    No one disagrees with me that updating regulations took a long time in the past,
    making it difficult to adopt any new technologies except after many investigations
    and meetings. But today, the situation has become easy and requires only an
    administration that believes in modern technologies and pushes its organisations to
    adopt them. (P12)

One of the participants stated that some organisations do not want to adopt cloud
computing services:

    With some of the organisations I worked with, I faced the obstacle that they did not
    have a plan or desire to switch to cloud computing, and this was based on the
    organisation’s plan and goal set for it since its launch. When trying to make any


                                              184
    change, we face a challenge from the board of directors, which sometimes reasons
    that the Cloud will cost the company financially and threaten data security and is
    considered a risk. (P10)

Moreover, most of the participants were concerned about the organisations' regulations
and consider that updating regulations will help organisations adopt the Cloud. In
addition, they mentioned the vital role of top managers within organisations, assuming
any change starts from the top managers, and they should accelerate their organisations'
transition to new technologies such as cloud computing services, which has eased the
work for millions of organisations worldwide.


    6.5 Employees’ Expertise in the Cloud
Participants explained that employees' experience in the cloud plays a vital role in
adopting cloud services, understanding the organisation's needs and what is compatible
with its existing services, in addition to studying the feasibility of adopting the cloud from
a technical perspective. Many participants (e.g., P1, P7 and P11) claimed that whenever
there are employees with expertise in the Cloud, it would increase the SMEs' intention to
adopt cloud computing services. However, in their organisations there was still a lack of
understanding about cloud computing services and whether they were needed or not or
whether the traditional systems were sufficient for what was required. Top managers were
often unable to make such decisions and SMEs needed to have employees with computing
experience who could evaluate the situation and propose solutions.

Another participant added that:

    The presence of technical expertise in the organisation is considered essential,
    especially with cloud computing. The expert’s role is not only technical, as he studies
    the technical structure of the organisation and its systems’ engineering, in addition
    to the cloud computing strategy, the extent of its effectiveness, and its economic and
    technical feasibility for the organisation. (P9)

Another participant insisted that employees' expertise saves the organisation from taking
risks:




                                             185
    Organisations often adopt new cloud services as soon as they are announced and
    marketed without verifying their effectiveness. This costs the organisation loss in its
    projects and customers. […] This proves that having technical expertise and
    practical experience in cloud computing helps the organisation adopt the Cloud at
    the right time and in the right way. (P4)

Moreover, this participant declared that the job of the technical expert had become as
crucial as the job of the financial expert to audit the organisation's accounts:

    It is common practice for organisations to engage the services of a financial auditor.
    Similarly, having a technical auditor who possesses expertise in the technical domain
    is imperative. This individual plays a crucial role in striking a harmonious
    equilibrium between the operational requirements of the organisation and the
    economic viability of incorporating new technical services. Additionally, the
    technical auditor ensures the long-term feasibility of investing in these services. (P6)
