Participant P10 stated that technical expertise helps organisations stay ahead of the
competition, saying: “Nowadays, technologies are developing very rapidly in a fiercely
competitive environment, and the presence of technical expertise familiar with cloud
computing will undoubtedly help the organisation always stay ahead of its competitors”

Participant P12 described the benefits of having personnel possessing exceptional
technical skills: “The inclusion of suitable technical proficiency within an organisation
contributes to the preservation of the organisation's financial resources and the
prevention of expensive errors. In addition, it facilitates organisational growth by
supplying the technical expertise required for its operations.”

As a result of this, it is possible to conclude that all participants reached a consensus about
the crucial role that people within their organisations who possess technical competence
in the Cloud play, as well as their influence in the process of adopting cloud computing
services and comprehending the fundamentals and tactics underlying them.


    6.6 Internet Outages and Shortage of Cloud Providers
Frequent internet outages and unstable speeds constitute a concern among organisations
when it comes to adopting cloud computing services, in addition to the deficiency of the



                                             186
number of cloud service providers in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia. Most participants
raised that concern and clarified that they were late in adopting cloud services for these
reasons. They stated that when the number of cloud providers increased within Saudi
Arabia, and the quality of the internet service provided was assured, it would lead them
to adopt cloud computing services. One participant commented:

    Indeed, the Internet is considered the primary key to adopting cloud services, as we
    in our organisation often suffer from Internet outages, whether due to internal
    pressure in the organisation or because of the Internet service provider, and both
    reasons delay and affect our work and may even cost us financial losses. (P7)

Another participant agreed with him and added that:

    For a long time, organisations have suffered from having only one service provider,
    which is the Internet service provider, but now we have another service provider that
    they suffer from as well, which is the cloud service provider. The services provided
    by cloud service providers in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia are very modest and not
    befitting the size of the Saudi market. This even worse as the Saudi government
    prevented organisations from storing their customer data with any service provider
    outside the borders of Saudi Arabia. (P1)

If the organisation is keen on having continuous data backups, using cloud computing
services will slow down operations and affect the business, and P11 commented that was
especially so owing to: “…the lack of high bandwidth to accommodate the amount of
data flowing to the servers.”

Existing cloud service providers' manipulation of service level agreements (SLAs) poses
an obstacle for organisations in adopting cloud services:

    In fact, what is presented and marketed by cloud service providers in the service level
    agreement (SLAs) does not reflect reality at all, as cloud service providers aim to
    attract new customers. Their care ends after that, and the customer is shocked by the
    services provided, whether in terms of slow speeds, loss of backup storage, security,
    etc. (P13)




                                           187
Three participants (P11, P8 and P4) noted that cloud service providers circumvent Saudi
government regulations stipulating not to store customer data outside the country. They
were aware, however, that several new computing service providers had emerged who
were exploiting these regulations and potentially jeopardising the security of
organisations' customers' data. These cloud service providers had a few servers within
Saudi Arabia, which they could utilise as an interface in front of Saudi regulations, but in
practice stored data outside the KSA. However, a practical approach to the problem might
involve buying or leasing servers outside Saudi Arabia's borders, where customer data
could be stored.

The issues businesses encounter in this domain mostly pertain to service providers,
including those operating on the Internet or the cloud. Service providers must be aware
of the impediments contributing to the delay in adopting cloud technology. Resolving
these difficulties enhances organisations' propensity to embrace cloud computing and
fosters a harmonious working environment among all stakeholders.


    6.7 Employees’ Resistance to the Cloud
Most of the participants clarified that employees significantly influence organisations in
making decisions about adopting new services, whether positively or negatively. When
there is a lack of acceptance of modern technologies from employees, such as cloud
computing, this results in an adverse effect and clash between employees and their
organisations, resulting in a delay in adopting cloud computing services, which may
sometimes even be abandoned. Moreover, one participant remarked that employees who
resist cloud service adoption are not technologists:

    Our organisation suffers from the resistance of employees who do not specialise in
    technology to adopt cloud services; due to their lack of trust and belief they do not
    add anything new to the organisation. Instead, from their point of view, they may be
    a reason for the slowdown in the organisation’s work and the loss of its data. (P3)

While another participant added that the reason for employees’ resistance was the
language barrier:

    Employee resistance to adopting cloud computing services is due to the lack of
    Arabization in some control panels and services provided by cloud computing service


                                            188
    providers, as they are in English, and some employees only speak their mother
    tongue, Arabic. (P14)

Lack of training and education for employees on cloud computing technologies may be a
reason for resistance:

    The majority of non-specialised employees lack a complete understanding of cloud
    computing. At the same time, there is negligence on the part of organisations in not
    providing scientific and practical training sessions and a practical guide to educate
    employees and make them receptive to cloud computing, and this results in their
    resistance. (P13)

Another participant added that:

    Fear of losing control is the most crucial cause of employee resistance. Some
    employees believe that giving up a certain level of control over the organisation's
    operations and work environment in exchange for adopting cloud services is the
    beginning of handing over control and leadership to a third party without their
    intervention. (P5)

One participant attributed employee resistance to adopting cloud computing services to a
lack of awareness:

    One of the primary obstacles encountered in adopting cloud computing services is
    the lack of awareness inside the organisations. Employees may exhibit resistance
    towards change, particularly in the context of embracing novel technologies or
    altering well-established procedures. The successful use of cloud computing services
    necessitates overcoming resistance and cultivating a culture that embraces change
    and innovation wholeheartedly. (P9)

Therefore, the consensus among all participants was that employee resistance towards
cloud computing services could be attributed to a limited awareness and familiarity with
the concept of the Cloud, as well as insufficient training and educational opportunities
provided by organisations for their employees. Consistent educational provision to
employees regarding the most recent technological advancements fosters a strong digital
work environment and leads to embracing cloud computing services.



                                          189
    6.8 Loss of Trust in the Third Party Holding the Data
Unfortunately, most SMEs were concerned about the third party who would offer cloud
services and hold the data. They showed their fears, specifically when dealing with
government ministries, due to the sensitive data they passed on. Additionally, Some
SMEs (P2, P7 and P14) were worried about the financial tenders they provided for
projects, which might be accessed by third parties, and if passed to competitors they could
be undercut and lose the contract. Moreover, if their tender was accepted by any
government agency, they became responsible for protecting any government data and this
meant they were nervous of having it stored by a third party.

Participant P13 agreed and added that not knowing where cloud providers' servers are
also makes people less likely to trust them: “…This leads to the loss of confidence in the
third party, but that will become a permanent doubt in the event that any data is lost or
leaked.”

The data of citizens and residents in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia is confidential and
cannot be published or shared in any way except by the relevant authorities. However,
when there are projects with government agencies, they work to share them with the party
receiving the project, but with terms and conditions that ensure their preservation. This is
what made the SMEs very careful, as one explained:

    We work on a lot of projects for government bodies. For some of them, especially
    technical ones, we need to process and build systems based on data of citizens and
    residents of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia. Still, we have to keep the data safe and
    private, so we don't like working with cloud service providers because we don't trust
    them with the data we are responsible for. (P11)

Participant P3 indicated that the lack of a data insurance policy among cloud service
providers was one of the factors that caused a loss of trust: “The presence of an insurance
policy with solid requirements for organisations’ data with cloud service providers
encourages the adoption of cloud computing services by SMEs. It enhances the value of
trust between them.”

Therefore, all participants agreed that trust in cloud service providers is still weak and
needs to be strengthened and providers must be seen to guarantee the rights of


                                            190
organisations in order to promote the adoption of cloud services. As was evident from
most participants, the more trust is raised, the greater the adoption of cloud computing
services.


    6.9 Factors Affecting the Intention to Adopt Cloud
        Computing Services
This section looks at the technological, organisational, environmental and social context
that encompass the factors that impact intention to adopt cloud computing services.

6.9.1 Technological Context
This section covers data confidentiality, integrity and security as well as relative
advantages and service cost and quality.

6.9.1.1 Data Confidentiality

Data confidentiality is crucial for SMEs, especially when dealing with sensitive data, such
as government projects. In addition, SMEs are worried about their data that is controlled
and accessed by the cloud services providers. Due to that, they trust traditional systems
over cloud services, which delays their adoption of cloud computing services, as one
participant explained.

    The failure of cloud service providers to adhere to the rules for storing confidential
    data has reduced our trust in them and raised our concern about our data, especially
    confidential data, as cloud service providers ask the customer to determine the
    confidentiality of the data to be stored on a private server or not, but in reality, it is
    stored randomly with a group of other data (P5).

Three participants (P9, P11 and P15) commented that data protection and its policy
imposed by the government on organizations that deal with citizens’ and residents’ data
and is the organisations' responsibility. They pointed out that these regulations were very
strict and they were concerned that if cloud providers did not sufficiently safeguard data
confidentiality, it was their organisation that would be blamed and penalised. This
concern meant that there was hesitation in transitioning to the Cloud.

According to the comments provided by the participants, a majority concurred that the
challenge associated with adopting cloud computing stemmed from concerns over the



                                            191
confidentiality of data, mainly owing to apprehensions around cloud service providers
and government rules about this matter. The participants believe that the existing quantity
of privately leaked data by cloud providers, either directly or indirectly, enough evidence
to hinder their adoption of cloud computing. Therefore, organisations that intend to adopt
cloud computing require stringent data confidentiality standards and effective monitoring
of these standards. The cloud service provider ought to improve their service to address
this requirement.

6.9.1.2 Data Integrity

Most participants felt that data integrity was a critical factor for adopting cloud computing
services as it should be maintained during all the stages within the organisation, starting
from receiving the data to processing it and then storing it.

    Protecting data from being accessed, altered or intercepted without permission has
    become necessary for any organisation. This makes sure that the data is actual and
    can be used correctly, and it also makes sure that a lot of data is linked
    together without any interference. (P5)

Promoting the principle of data integrity and contributing to improving its laws and
regulations enhances the technical environment for cloud computing and helps in
adopting its services:

    Undoubtedly, the active participation in enhancing and refining frameworks that
    govern data integrity, guide its trajectory and leverage cutting-edge technologies like
    data encryption and blockchain services to ensure both security and efficiency
    significantly promotes the adoption and utilisation of cloud computing services. (P3)

Clearly, from the view of the point of participants, applying data integrity quality
standards is considered a roadmap for organisations that need data to be safe from
tampering. Applying these standards creates trust in cloud service providers and reflects
positively on SMEs. Additionally, the participants expressed that the optimal approach to
rectify any deficiencies in data integrity is to revise the existing laws and techniques used,
enhancing their efficacy in delivering services. Therefore, participants think improving
the data integrity level will increase Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing
services.



                                             192
6.9.1.3 Data Security

Data Security was investigated in this study as one of the factors that may affect SME
cloud computing adoption by asking the participants whether they believed that the
Cloud’s security was strong enough and how the cloud provider addressed security. The
findings show that most participants agreed that fears about data security did not affect
their intention to adopt cloud services because security was the cloud provider's mission,
not theirs. There is a service level agreement (SLA) which maintains the level of security
for the services, as one participant explained.

    Data security must be an essential factor in any technical system that must be
    instigated and constantly enhanced. However, in cloud computing services, data
    security is the responsibility of cloud service providers and central to their
    commitment to the SLA, so from my point of view as leader of this organisation I think
    it will not affect our intention to adopt cloud services. (P15)

Another participant agreed with what was mentioned above and added:

    I concur with the assertion that data security is a pivotal consideration. However, I
    believe it will not significantly impact our willingness to adopt the Cloud since cloud
    providers have constructed their services with a strong emphasis on securing
    customer data. (P10)

Therefore, it is noteworthy that a majority of the interview participants expressed
consensus regarding the potentially limited impact of organisational data security on the
adoption of cloud computing, provided that cloud providers possess an enhanced
understanding of novel technologies and possess the ability to address challenges through
security restructuring and other enhancements. The consensus among participants was
that establishing security standards for both SMEs and cloud computing service providers
is of the utmost importance.

6.9.1.4 Relative Advantages

The participants highly consider the Relative Advantages factor as one of the key
advantages that makes cloud services more profitable and desirable than traditional
systems and how this positively affects the intention of SMEs to adopt cloud services.
One of the participants added that:



                                            193
    Among the relative advantages that encourage us to adopt cloud computing services
    is the presence of a pay-per-use service, as well as flexibility in storage space,
    availability of the service at all times, and a customer relationship management
    system. These services are undoubtedly relative advantages that lead and guide us
    to adopt cloud computing. (P6)

Another participant highlighted the importance of the relative advantages:

    The existence of relative advantages is what pushes organisations towards adopting
    computing services because they have created a distinctive mark at various levels,
    whether in their characteristics or service deployment models, which are
    characterized by the presence of several options for the customer according to his
    desires, as well as the architecture of service models. (P14)

The comments above indicate that businesses possess an understanding of the relative
advantages associated with the adoption of cloud computing for their systems. They
believe that such adoption has the potential to enhance efficiency in terms of time and
effort while also facilitating the improvement of online services for SMEs. Thus, the
participants believed that rapid expansion in the field of cloud computing offers numerous
benefits, including a diverse range of cloud configurations, flexibility in storage capacity,
convenient software accessibility from any location and uninterrupted maintenance
services. These capabilities are expected to facilitate the identification and resolution of
IT infrastructure challenges and foster the intention towards the adoption of cloud
computing services inside their respective businesses.

6.9.1.5 Service Cost

The service cost is considered a significant key in adopting cloud computing services
since it directly impacts the realisation of required services. Consequently, it was
identified and investigated as one of the factors influencing the adoption of the Cloud.
Participants P4, P7 and P12 considered the service cost as a hindrance to adopting the
Cloud as traditional systems were not as expensive.

Another participant claimed that organisations did not consider cost compared to quality:

    Always in our organisation, we do not put the cost of services at the top of our
    priority; we look for the quality of any new service that we will adopt and assess it


                                            194
    and whether we are going to benefit from it or not because the price for any new
    services is significantly based on competition. (P2)

Most organisations aim to adopt new creative technologies; however, one of the
participants stated that cost could be an issue:

    From my experience of many organisations I have worked with, we often try to be
    among the first adopters of any new technologies that emerge in the market to
    maintain organisational creativity from a technical perspective, but sometimes when
    the matter is dealt with by the finance team, they reject the high cost of any new
    technology like the Cloud. (P9)

Most of the participants generally indicated that the cost of services was a significant
concern for their organisations when considering the adoption of cloud computing
services. There was a clear understanding of the benefits offered by emerging
technologies like cloud computing. Additionally, this observation demonstrates that the
present cost of cloud services is occasionally higher than the expenditure on traditional
systems.

6.9.1.6    Service Quality

The significance of service quality in the adoption of cloud computing by SMEs is well
acknowledged. In addition, top managers and information technology (IT) directors were
aware of the potential advantages of cloud computing in addressing service quality
concerns. Three participants (P3, P10 and P15) strongly believed in enhancing service
quality inside their organisation's systems by adopting cloud computing services; and
acknowledged the potential of using cloud computing as a viable approach to tackle the
issue of poor quality that sometimes beset traditional systems.

One of the participants said that a lack of IT infrastructure and expertise within
organisations leads to a deficiency in service quality:

    From my perspective as the top manager, the quality issue does not come from a
    deficiency in staff knowledge or the non-use of IT professionals but rather from the
    quality and capability of the IT infrastructure. Therefore, it depends on the capability
    of any organisation, but the easy option is in our hands; implementing cloud




                                            195
    computing with our systems can be an attractive option, enabling the delivery of high-
    quality services to our customers through adopting cloud services. (P4)

Furthermore, based on an analysis of participants' feedback, it is evident that businesses
seeking to adopt cloud computing must possess a comprehensive understanding of service
quality and recognise the need to address technical challenges for improvement. The
cloud service provider should provide a clear elucidation of the service quality
requirements that can benefit SMEs. If this was the case, most participants concurred that
adopting cloud computing services is an appropriate solution to enhance the reliability
and stability of service quality.

6.9.2 Organisational Context
The organisational context comprises organisation regulations, organisation size,
technology readiness and top management support.

6.9.2.1 Organisation Regulations

Organisation regulations play a significant role in adopting cloud computing as these
comprise the internal regulations inside the organisations and the external regulations
between the organisations and the cloud providers. When regulations are continuously
improved and revised in line with the technical revolution and modern tools, this helps
organisations adopt cloud computing services. Participants P6 and P12 specifically
indicated that SMEs suffered from the fact that regulations were not continuously
updated, which postponed the adoption of cloud computing services.

Another participant (P7) agreed with the previous statement and added that insufficient
resources: “…may be the reason why we do not keep pace with the development of
regulations, which results in hindering cloud adoption.”

While another participant attributed the reason to external regulations:

    When we come to deal with cloud service providers, we are shocked by the reality of
    the complex regulations they have, which are not in line with our own regulations, or
    what we might expect, and this results from neglecting the aspect of the regulation by
    organisations. (P10)




                                           196
Therefore, the participants expressed an awareness of the importance of regulations in
cloud adoption. Their heightened level of concern over this aspect, primarily stemmed
from what they saw as a lack of clarity surrounding the laws and regulations set forth by
both internal stakeholders and cloud computing providers. This factor was perceived as a
highly influential determinant in the transition of SMEs towards adopting cloud
computing. It suggests that a third-party needs to establish suitable regulations between
organisations and cloud providers to streamline the adoption process.

6.9.2.2 Organisation Size

The size of organizations might be considered an influential factor in adopting cloud
computing as SMEs have systems that are less complex than those of large organisations.
However, participants P3, P6 and P14 mentioned that the organisation's size was not a
challenge in adopting cloud computing services. It required one person, who might be the
owner or a designated top manager to make the decision and find a cloud provider to
make the transition.

The lack of big data and complex systems in small organisations may encourage them to
adopt the Cloud but is not exactly a key reason, as stated by one of the participants:

    Often, at start-up for any SME, the concept of organisational agility is available, and
    this must be exploited positively to shift to cloud computing services before the
    business develops, systems branch out and become complex, and extensive data is
    obtained. Still, at the same time, it does not mean any complex system for large
    organisations cannot move to the Cloud, it is easy nowadays. (P7)

Overall, most participants agreed that organisation size has no influence on their
inclination and propensity to use cloud computing. In the context of Saudi Arabia, there
exists a substantial number of newly-established SMEs and large organisations that aim
to attain and leverage the objectives outlined in the Saudi Arabia Vision 2030, which
involves utilising newly emerged technologies such as cloud services. Hence, the ease of
adopting cloud computing services is not directly influenced by the size of the
organisation.




                                           197
6.9.2.3 Technology Readiness

Technology readiness varies from one organisation to another based on the technological
capacity and infrastructure, which make it a crucial factor that plays a significant role in
adopting cloud computing services. Most participants believed that free resources from
technology companies play a role in organisations’ reluctance to pay attention to their
infrastructure and technical personnel, which makes them face challenges during their
business expansion and finally creates a technical lack of preparedness that hinders
adopting cloud services. Participants P4, P8 and P13 specifically mentioned that in their
organisations they relied heavily on free technical tools such as Google Drive, Chat,
Gmail Form, etc., as these were easy to access and use. However, they also mentioned
that if SMEs did not pay the necessary attention to technical infrastructure, this, in
conjunction with a lack of technical expertise, would impede cloud adoption.

According to this participant, the absence of technical skills and experience created poor
technological readiness:

    I believe from my experience that most organisations that do not specialise in
    technology do not care much about technical skills but instead focus on providing
    their employees with the necessary commercial and administrative skills, placing us
    at lower technological readiness levels. We must pay attention to this aspect to help
    us facilitate the adoption of cloud computing services that will improve our
    productivity. (P9)

In conclusion, based on the observations made among the participants, SMEs lacking
adequate IT infrastructure preparation must allocate financial resources towards
constructing their infrastructure rather than relying on free resources. Furthermore,
acquiring IT skills and employing individuals with IT experience contributes to the
enhancement of technological readiness and the facilitation of cloud adoption.

6.9.2.4 Top Management Support

Most participants insisted that top management support is critical to adopting cloud
computing services, as decision-making depends mainly on top management. In addition,
the IT directors expressed apprehension regarding the decision of top management about
whether to implement cloud services, perceiving it as a significant factor that necessitated
attention and resolution. One of the participants (P1) who works in top management


                                            198
expressed that: “Top management support is required and is considered the main driving
factor for cloud computing adoption.”

One participant P12 noted that concerns about system failure and taking risks were
reasons why top managers avoided adopting new services “…especially when internal
systems exist.” Another participant claimed that instability in management was the reason
for losing top management support for cloud adoption:

    Constantly changing managers is considered one of the obstacles due to which we
    lose the support of top management, as the manager must continue to manage the
    organisation for an entire period, for example, four years, as it helps him understand
    the organisation and its work and helps in making appropriate decisions, such as
    adopting the cloud computing. (P9)

Based on the above, it is evident that the support of top management is regarded as a
crucial determinant in cloud computing adoption for SMEs. The top management
possesses the requisite authority to make critical decisions, so it should be utilised to
benefit the organisation, not hinder it. However, resistance to innovations by top
management could impede the transfer of SME systems to cloud providers. SMEs should
consider the significance of this particular issue when they intend to adopt cloud
computing inside their organisations.

6.9.3 Environmental Context
The environmental context includes technology infrastructure, the cloud provider, the
legal framework and enterprise competitiveness.

6.9.3.1 Technology Infrastructure

Technology infrastructure is an influential factor that impacts SMEs in adopting cloud
computing services. The Internet is considered the core component of cloud computing
infrastructure, and its role in driving adoption cannot be overlooked. Participants P2, P5,
P10 and P14 agreed that the Internet can pose a challenge for SMEs; as the internet service
in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia can be plagued by slow speeds and frequent outages,
which can delay the adoption of cloud computing services.




                                           199
Another participant’s concerns about the cloud services were wholly based on the
internet:

    What delays the process of adopting cloud computing services is our concern about
    their complete dependence on the Internet, and since our services require agreement
    among all employees in the organisation daily on all operations, switching to the
    Cloud without a strong and secure Internet is considered an adventure. (P11)

Backups are a problem caused by weak internet infrastructure, as stated by one of the
participants:

    Operations on our systems require massive backup copies on an ongoing basis, which
    weakens the Internet and affects our business operations, especially since SMEs do
    not have high bandwidth or speed. Internet service providers and legislative agents
    must address this concern first, which will contribute to supporting the process of
    adopting cloud services. (P13)

Overall, SMEs encounter challenges related to their technology infrastructure, which
hinder their adoption of cloud computing. The issue of inadequate internet services is
evident in the current landscape of internet service provision. A notable level of concern
is expressed by participants from organisations in various sectors. However, participants
also reported that they anticipated improved internet services, as outlined in Saudi Vision
2030, which aims to expand the country's technology infrastructure with high-speed
internet services and high bandwidth.

6.9.3.2     Cloud Provider

In Saudi Arabia, cloud providers are considered a big challenge for SMEs due to the Saudi
government's regulation that requires them to adopt only provider services whose servers
are based in Saudi Arabia. The number of cloud providers in Saudi Arabia is still modest
and unprofessional, making this an influential factor in the adoption of cloud computing
services.

    There is competition between service providers in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia to
    provide the best possible services, but when we talk about that competition, it is
    between a few local companies whose servers are actually located within the borders




                                           200
    of Saudi Arabia, so the private sector hopes that there will be more service providers,
    which will create better services and greater competition. (P6)

Another participant (P12) added that: “The Saudi market still suffers from the lack of
companies specialising in cloud computing services, as all current service providers
consider their service to be secondary, as their primary work is telecommunications
services.”

According to this participant, cloud providers should have more flexibility in their
services:

    The lack of flexibility of service providers and as well their lack of response to their
    customers upon request creates a gap between the service provider and the customer.
    Thus, this becomes an influential factor that must be addressed in an established
    framework that saves all parties. (P3)

Therefore, the participants recognised the importance of the role played by cloud
providers. However, they expressed heightened apprehension regarding this aspect due
to the limitations imposed by the Saudi government, which prohibit the utilisation of
cloud services hosted on servers located beyond the borders of Saudi Arabia. This element
was perceived as a substantial determinant in the inclination of SMEs to adopt cloud
computing. Many participants believed that the number of cloud providers available in
Saudi Arabia was limited. Furthermore, a notable disparity exists between cloud
providers and SMEs due to a lack of flexibility and specialisation in the cloud services
offered by these providers.

6.9.3.3 Legal Framework

The legal framework plays a significant role in adopting cloud computing services where
the government established the framework to govern the policy regarding data and the
relationship between SMEs and cloud providers—implementing stringent restrictions and
procedures that may impede or prolong the adoption of cloud computing services.

    From my point of view, the legal framework for the Cloud was established to protect
    all parties. Instead, the framework aims to encourage enterprises to adopt cloud
    services in a controlled environment, but what is flawed with this is the lack of




                                             201
    monitoring and follow-up by the legislative authorities on cloud service providers
    and ensuring that they implement the framework to the letter. (P3)

One of the participants suggested that the regulatory framework should be constantly
updated and developed to meet the aspirations of the beneficiaries:

    The legal framework for cloud computing was established in the Kingdom of Saudi
    Arabia in 2018. It was updated in 2020, but it still needs to be updated and developed
    in line with the aspirations of organisations and the technological revolution that
    occurred after the coronavirus pandemic, such as data classifications and the
    possibility of transferring content to the customer and protecting it. (P15)

The legal framework is a significant challenge for SMEs for many reasons associated
with the regulations governing the interaction between cloud service providers and SMEs.
SMEs exhibit apprehension in embracing cloud services owing to the absence of
government oversight on cloud providers and the inadequacy of the existing framework.
Most participants said their technical endeavours to implement cloud computing services
have been postponed or terminated for these reasons. Thus, governmental authorities
must consider this matter and enact legal remedies that save them from taking risks.

6.9.3.4 Enterprise Competitiveness

Competition between enterprises creates a positive environment that helps adopt modern
technologies like cloud computing. The competition factor was considered vital and
influential for competition. However, participants P4, P7 and P13 believe that
competitiveness influences SMEs' intention to adopt cloud services; it was often the lack
of technological readiness that led an organisation out of the competition and not the
competition itself.

While another participant said:

    The absence of top management support is considered one of the reasons that delay
    organisations in competing with their counterparts and adopting cloud computing
    technologies and benefit from the modern technical revolution, especially since the
    market requires it. (P2)

As another participant explained:



                                           202
    I think the competition plays a role in pushing us towards adoption of the Cloud or
    delaying it. Competition is related to business matters; however, the absence of this
    competition may result in not adopting new technologies like the Cloud. (P11)

Thus, participants expressed that competitiveness has influence and that many reasons
related to it hindered their organisations from adopting cloud services. These reasons
included the need for more preparedness in terms of technology, insufficient support from
top management, and a shortage of staff competence in cloud computing. The participants
also showed that they may be inclined to adopt cloud computing when competitiveness
is present, provided their skill level is comparable. However, addressing the other
challenges associated with each organisation must be addressed before considering
strategies to enhance competitiveness.

6.9.4 Social Context
The social context comprises culture, awareness and trust.

6.9.4.1 Culture

The culture of a community varies from one environment to another based on several
variables. Technical culture is an influential factor because society is affected by the
technical approach adopted in its environment. Participants P1, P5 and P10 stated that the
technical culture of employees did not affect SMEs' adoption of cloud computing services
as generally their employees had a good background in new technologies due to the
government support of them. There was however, occasional resistance to cloud adoption,
by people worried that it could make them redundant, but there was not a significant
number.

While another participant added that:

    It is natural that employees are generally influenced by the technical values and
    beliefs that they have learned and worked on before. Therefore, the global
    technological culture must be integrated with the local culture of employees to blend
    experiences and technical and cultural diversity within the organisation and that is
    what we did to ensure the success of accepting any emerging technology (P13).




                                           203
The participants are not worried about technical culture when adopting cloud computing
services. The prevailing viewpoint among participants was that integrating global
technological culture with local culture is desirable and was already happening due to
government support. The responses provided by the participants indicated a favourable
technical culture among top managers and IT directors about the benefits associated with
implementing cloud computing. Collaborative efforts with service providers can assist
SMEs in addressing these challenges.

6.9.4.2 Awareness

Awareness is a critical factor due to its vital role in making people aware of cloud
computing and its benefits. It depends on several variables, including education and
training. The decision of societies and organisations to adopt cloud computing always
depends on the correct and sufficient awareness that precedes it to clarify the advantages
of the cloud in facilitating business. As one participant commented:

    There is no doubt that awareness is still lacking in us, especially we managers, as we
    are not specialists in technology, and we do not know about the Cloud except that it
    is a service obtained through the Internet. I think it is time to educate everyone from
    the top of the pyramid to the bottom. (P8)

Another participant said the employees need to be educated and trained about Cloud to
raise their awareness:

    From my point of view as a manager, employees have only modest information about
    cloud computing due to the absence of technical awareness. As organisations whose
    primary goal is their work, they have neglected to train and educate their employees,
    whether through workshops or training courses, on the modern technologies that
    have recently appeared in the market. (P4)

While another participant added:

    I know and realize that adopting cloud computing services will save us a lot of effort
    and financial costs over traditional systems. I believe everyone who works with us is
    aware of this, except for a small percentage of old employees who have no technical
    experience or lack awareness. (P10)




                                           204
The participants' opinions indicate that there is a need for education and training among
employees regarding the advantages of cloud computing services. Insufficient knowledge
and a lack of comprehension of the advantages of cloud computing were witnessed. IT
managers possess a high level of awareness regarding the benefits of cloud computing.
However, it has been shown that top-level managers often lack this awareness and require
further explanations of the various features and future prospects of cloud technology.

6.9.4.3 Trust

Trust is considered an essential and influential factor that must be present when intending
to adopt cloud computing services due to its significant impact on organisations’
decisions towards adopting new technologies, not just cloud services. The trust factor
here comes in two parts, the first is trust in the cloud technology and the second is trust
in the cloud service providers, as this participant explained:

    From my point of view, I see that we have confidence in cloud computing technology
    itself, but confidence in the cloud service providers is missing, because there are no
    obligations or fines allowed by the service provider to be imposed when any of our
    data is leaked, and this is what delays our adoption of the cloud computing service.
    (P14)

While another participant (P7) attributed the loss of trust to poor services: “We lack trust
in cloud computing due to Cloud providers' weaknesses and poor services compared to
traditional systems.”

Insufficient cloud providers within Saudi Arabia was another reason for loss of trust,
according to this participant:

    Recently, the Saudi government imposed the necessity of dealing only with cloud
    service providers whose servers are located within the borders of Saudi Arabia. This
    undoubtedly enhances trust that our data is with local service providers whose
    locations are known, unlike what it was before. Still, the modest number of cloud
    service providers makes us lose trust. There must be large numbers of service
    providers whose services vary and compete to provide the best products to customers
    that align with global services. (P3)




                                            205
Trust is a pivotal element in the acceptance and implementation of cloud computing
among SMEs. Thus, the issue arises wherein top-level management, who own
confidential information, faces challenges in relying on international third-party entities
to manage data centres due to the Saudi government's ban on not dealing with cloud
computing service providers whose servers are located outside the Saudi border. The
participants showed that the limited number of local cloud providers raises this problem.
They highlighted the significance of trust as a crucial factor for SMEs, which must be
taken seriously to increase the trust in cloud computing services.


    6.10 Summary
This chapter illustrates the results of the qualitative data analysis conducted from the
viewpoints of top managers and IT managers of Saudi SMEs in Riyadh and obtained
through semi-structured interviews with 15 of these individuals. The participants engaged
in a comprehensive discussion regarding all the factors outlined in the conceptual
framework of this study on four dimensions (technological, organisational, environmental
and social), as discussed in Chapter Three. The participants in the study highlighted the
significance of these factors and their influence on the adoption of cloud computing
among SMEs firms in the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia.

Furthermore, the results indicated that within the context of technology, it is crucial to
consider several critical elements, namely data confidentiality, data integrity, relative
advantages, service cost and service quality, as they substantially impact the intention of
adopting cloud computing services by Saudi SMEs. Moreover, the findings within the
organisational context indicate that factors such as organisation regulation, technology
readiness, and top management support are deemed highly critical and substantially
impact the intention to adopt cloud computing among SMEs. Moreover, the findings
about the environmental context indicate that factors such as technology infrastructure,
cloud provider, legal framework, and enterprise competitiveness were deemed to exert a
substantial influence on the decision-making process of top management and IT directors
about adopting cloud computing inside SMEs. Regarding the social context, the
participants placed great emphasis on awareness, and trust as influential aspects in the
decision-making process of top management and IT directors regarding the intention of
adopting cloud computing for SMEs. On the other hand, regarding data security,
organisation size and culture, participants did not show much concern, indicating that


                                           206
none of these variables were deemed relevant in influencing the intention to adopt cloud
computing services for SMEs.

The following chapter will discuss the findings derived from data analysis presented in
chapters 5 and 6. This analysis includes both quantitative and qualitative approaches,
aiming to triangulate the results and explain the reasons for them.




                                           207
Chapter
7 Discussion
    7.1 Introduction
This study provided an extensive investigation perspective on the relationship between
Saudi small and medium enterprises’ (SMEs') intention to adopt cloud computing
services and the challenging factors, see figure 7.1. It aimed to enhance understanding
and demonstrate that merely examining a few factors in isolation would lead to a partial
knowledge of the critical factors. Additionally, thoroughly investigating the connection
between essential factors and cloud adoption was necessary because of gaps in existing
research. The findings of this study suggest that Saudi SMEs are more receptive and open
than before to adopting cloud computing services.




             Figure 7-1 Stage 7: Discussing the most influential factors




                                          208
This study has developed a framework called the Adoption of Cloud Computing Model
by Saudi SMEs (ACCM-SME) that consisted of factors that had been found in prior
literature to have a significant impact, i.e., Data Confidentiality, Data Integrity, Data
Security, Relative Advantages, Service Cost, Service Quality, Organisation Regulations,
Organisation Size, Technology Readiness, Top Management Support, Technology
Infrastructure, Cloud Provider, Legal Framework, Enterprise Competitiveness, Culture,
Awareness and Trust. The study framework blended the TOE model and DOI theory to
develop a framework that can examine the factors that impact Saudi SMEs' adoption of
cloud computing. A total of 17 crucial factors were investigated, out of which 12 were
shown to have a statistically significant correlation with Saudi SMEs' adoption of cloud
computing services. The conclusions for these factors along with their hypothesis results
and the interview results and related themes are summarised, triangulated to see if they
are aligned and discussed as follows:

7.1.1 Data Confidentiality
The quantitative data from the surveys shown in Chapter 5 support the hypothesis (H1):
Increasing the data confidentiality of cloud computing increases Saudi SMEs' intention
to adopt cloud computing services. Data confidentiality refers to protecting the data and
information stored from unauthorised access. This research measured this factor using
three items, as mentioned in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.1. The findings indicated that Data
Confidentiality (DC) is a crucial element strongly associated with Saudi SMEs' intention
to adopt Cloud computing (ITAC). The relationship between DC and ITAC is positively
significant (β = .094, P < 0.032), and this finding aligns with the findings of previous
studies (e.g., Chen & Zhao, 2012). Thus, the statistical analysis findings of the Data
Confidentiality component and the correlation result confirmed the existence of the
hypothesised positive link. The findings, which were made in the context of Saudi SMEs,
indicated that cloud computing services have yet to reach high quality.

The interviews findings shown in Chapter 6 supported these quantitative findings, as a
theme called ‘customers' sensitive data and privacy’ emerged from the qualitative data.
Most interviewees were concerned about their customers' data sensitivity and privacy and
believed this played a significant role in adopting cloud computing. Most interview
participants agreed that the data confidentiality guarantee should be more significant, and
this would help in adopting cloud services. Findings from both chapters 5 and 6 show that


                                           209
Saudi SMEs need to care more about data confidentiality within their organisations and
consider that increasing data confidentiality increases the intention to adopt cloud
computing services.

7.1.2 Data Integrity
The survey findings supported the hypothesis (H2): Increasing the data integrity of cloud
computing increases Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services. Data
integrity refers to keeping the data safe and maintaining its accuracy and integrity during
its life cycle, from the moment it enters the Cloud, and throughout processing until
deletion. This research measured this factor using three items (see Chapter 5, Section
5.11.1.2). Data Integrity (DC) was identified as a significant determinant strongly
correlated with the intention of Saudi SMEs to adopt cloud computing (ITAC). Based on
the data collected and analysed via the statistical test, there is a positive correlation and
influence between ITAC and DI (β =.125, P < 0.020). This result is consistent with the
conclusions drawn in prior research (Chen & Zhao, 2012). Therefore, the correlation and
statistical analysis results of the data integrity component validated the existence of the
postulated positive relationship. The findings in the context of Saudi SMEs interpreted
that cloud services could be better and need some improvement.

The interviews findings reinforce this finding. As one of the participants in the interviews
said:

Protecting data from being accessed, altered, or intercepted without permission has
become necessary for any organisation. This makes sure that the data is actual and can
be used correctly, and it also makes sure that a lot of data is linked together without any
interference. (P5)

Thus, IT managers and top managers in the SMEs agree that data integrity is a critical
factor in the adoption of the cloud process. Both the quantitative and qualitative findings
demonstrated that Saudi SMEs need to consider data integrity within their organisations
and improve it as increasing data integrity increases the intention to adopt cloud
computing services.




                                            210
7.1.3 Data Security
The survey findings do not support the hypothesis (H3): Increasing the data security of
cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services.
Data security means all stored information is protected from damage, loss or unauthorised
access. This research measured this factor using three items mentioned in Chapter 5,
Section 5.11.1.3. Therefore, the results of the survey suggest that data security does not
seem to be an issue which influences the adoption of cloud services. This lack of concern
may have something to do with the recent requirement stipulated by the Saudi government
that businesses should store their data with Saudi boundaries. The hypothesis regarding
data security was rejected with a value of P=0.289, indicating that the data security had
no impact on the decision of the Saudi SMEs to adopt cloud computing services. This
was different to previous findings from a study conducted in the UK on SMEs (Sahandi
et al., 2012), which found that most participants were concerned about the data protection
and privacy of cloud-based systems, which delayed them from adopting cloud computing
services. In contrast, Saudi SMEs believe they already have a reasonable data security
level which means it is safe to adopt cloud services.

The findings from the interviews align with this finding. As one of interviewees (a top
manager in the IT sector) mentioned:

    Data security is an essential factor in any technical system that must be instigated
    and constantly enhanced. However, in cloud computing services, data security falls
    on the responsibility of cloud service providers and the extent of their commitment to
    the SLA, so from my point of view as leader of this organisation I think it will not
    affect our intention to adopt cloud services. (P15)

The findings from Chapter 5 (the survey) and Chapter 6 (the interviews) highlighted that
Saudi SMEs' data security is not a challenging factor in their intention to adopt cloud
computing, which led to the rejection of hypothesis H3.

7.1.4 Relative Advantages
The survey findings support the hypothesis (H4): The perceived relative advantages of
cloud computing have a positive effect that increases the Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt
cloud computing services. Relative advantages refer to the perceived innovation and



                                           211
benefit compared to the existing idea or system. This research measured this factor using
three items mentioned in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.4. Therefore, relative advantages
significantly affect Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services. The
findings show that Relative Advantage (RA) positively affects Saudi SMEs' intention to
adopt cloud services (ITAC). A positive association has been found between RA and
ITAC (β =.136, p < 0.020), which is consistent with previous results by Khayer, Talukder,
et al. (2020b). The statistical analysis results for the Relative Advantages component and
the correlation result supported the hypothesis (H4). In the context of Saudi SMEs, cloud
computing services are more attractive to adopt when they have relative advantages.

The findings from the interviews support the survey findings. As one of the top managers
interviewed said:

    The existence of relative advantages is what pushes organisations towards adopting
    computing services because they have created a distinctive mark at various levels,
    whether in their characteristics or service deployment models, which are
    characterized by the presence of several options for the customer according to his
    desire, as well as the architecture of service models. (P14)

The findings in both Chapters 5 and 6 demonstrated that Saudi SMEs need to focus more
on the relative advantages within their organisations and adopt new features so that the
perceived relative advantages of cloud computing have a positive effect that increases
their ability to transition to cloud computing services. Any government initiative to
increase Saudi SMEs adoption of cloud services could usefully encourage SMEs to focus
on their relative advantages.

7.1.5 Service Cost
The survey findings did not support the hypothesis (H5): Decreasing the cost of cloud
computing increases the Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services.
Service cost means the money charged for the cloud services as the Cloud is based on the
pay-as-you-go model. This research measured this factor using three items mentioned in
Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.5. Service Cost (SC) was not found to be a concern or a
challenge. It was expected that lower service costs would encourage cloud computing
use; however, this was not the case, which may be due to Saudi SMEs prioritising
improvements in quality, whatever the cost. Hypothesis H5 was rejected with a value of


                                           212
(P=0.151), indicating that the cost associated with cloud computing had no significant
impact on Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud services. These findings conflict with
those of Leavitt (2009), who argues that people recognise the significant potential of
cloud technology in decreasing the cost of IT for organisations and helping them avoid
the costs and difficulties associated with on-site systems The survey results for Saudi
SMEs suggest that cost is not a critical factor and cannot hinder or delay their intention
to adopt cloud computing services.

The findings for the interviews are clash with the above findings, and this will be justified
in the across-methods findings (see Section 8.4 in Chapter 8). One of the interviewees
(P9), who was currently working as a top manager in the estates and utilities sector,
remarked that from his experience or working in several organisations, management
generally wanted to be one of the first to adopt new technologies in order to boost the
capabilities of their organisation. However, the finance officers often vetoed the
introduction of innovations such as the Cloud on the grounds that the cost was too high.

Nevertheless, the findings for the primary method (the survey) highlighted that Saudi
SMEs' service costs are manageable and do not pose a challenge to their intention to adopt
cloud computing, resulting in the rejection of hypothesis (H5).

7.1.6 Service Quality
The survey findings support the hypothesis (H6): Increasing the service quality of cloud
computing increases Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services. Service
quality refers to the performance of the Cloud, including the reliability of cloud services.
This research measured this factor using three items mentioned in Chapter 5, Section
5.11.1.6. Based on these findings, Service Quality (SQ) is an essential factor to consider
due to its impact on the adoption of cloud services A positive correlation between SQ and
the intention to adopt cloud computing services (ITAC) (β =.116, p 0.019) was found,
which aligns with findings by Khayer, Talukder, et al. (2020b). Accordingly, the
correlation result and statistical analysis of the SQ component both demonstrated the
presence of the positive association hypothesised. In the context of Saudi SMEs, the
quality of cloud computing services has not reached the required level, and some
enhancements are needed to encourage SMEs to adopt cloud computing services.




                                            213
The interviews support the above findings. Three of the interviewees (P3, P10 and P15)
highlighted that the services in their organisations (in the education, administration and
IT sectors) were based on traditional systems and therefore variable in quality. All three
were hopeful that making cloud computing service providers responsible for the quality
of service would be a solution to this issue.

Findings from quantitative data analysed in Chapter 5 and qualitative data analysed in
Chapter 6 provided evidence that Saudi SMEs need to improve the service quality within
their organisations and understand that increasing service quality can facilitate the
adoption of cloud computing services.

7.1.7 Organisation Regulations
The survey findings support the hypothesis (H7): Increasing and updating the
organisational regulations increases the Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing
services. Organisation regulations refer to the set of rules that govern and structure the
organisation's operations. This research measured this factor using three items mentioned
in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.7. The results revealed that Organisation Regulations (OR)
are a crucial factor, and increasing and updating them has a positive relationship with
Saudi SMEs’ intention to adopt cloud computing services (ITAC). The analysis of survey
data shows a positive correlation between organisation regulations and the adoption of
cloud computing among Saudi SMEs, as measured by the correlation coefficient (CR)
between OR and ITAC (β = .135, p < 0.001). This result is consistent with findings by
Alharbi et al. (2016), who confirmed that keeping updated organisation regulations
reinforces the adoption of the Cloud. The results of the current study can be interpreted
to mean that Saudi SMEs regulations are not aligned with global regulations of
organisations to support the adoption of cloud services.

The interview findings with IT managers and top managers (see Chapter 6, Section
6.9.2.1) confirmed what was found by analysing the quantitative survey data (see Chapter
5, Section 5,11,1.7). Interviewees P6 and P12 both commented that SMEs, were
negatively impacted because regulations were not regularly updated. This dereliction
resulted in delays to digital development and meant that the adoption of cloud computing
services might be postponed. Both survey and interview findings confirmed that Saudi
SMEs need to regularly update their regulations. They need to consider the global rules



                                            214
and benefit from them by applying them within Saudi organisations if they want to adopt
cloud computing services.

7.1.8 Organisation Size
The survey findings did not support the hypothesis (H8): A smaller organisation size is
more likely to increase the Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services.
Organisation size refers to the size of the business (micro, small, medium or large). This
research measured this factor using three items mentioned in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.8.
Organisation size (OS) was found not to have a significant relationship with Saudi SMEs'
intention to adopt cloud computing services (ITAC). The hypothesis H8 was rejected with
the value of (P=0.948), showing that the size of the organisation does not influence the
intention of Saudi SMEs toward adoption of cloud computing services. This result was
different to what Hassan, Mohd Nasir, et al., (2017) found regarding the importance of
organisation size in adopting cloud services. However, since this prior study was before
the implementation of Saudi Vision 2030, a more recent investigation with a large sample
size was necessary to show whether its conclusions had changed in the presence of Saudi
Vision 2030. This is exactly what the current research has accomplished.

The interviews findings support the survey findings. As three of the interviewees (P3, P6,
P14) suggested cloud adoption was something that should be the responsibility of a single
individual. This could be the owner of the SME or a top manager and it was their liaising
with the cloud provider that was instrumental in whether adoption took place rather than
the size of their organisation.

Both the quantitative and qualitative findings emphasised that the organisation size of
Saudi SMEs appears to be separate from and not related to the intention of adoption cloud
computing services, which resulted in the rejection of hypothesis H8. It is of interest at
this point to note that no top managers or IT managers from micro companies were
questioned and size may be a factor with organisations of that size (one to five employees)
because the smallness of their operation may make adopting the Cloud unnecessary
and/or their budget does not stretch to it.




                                              215
7.1.9 Technology Readiness
The survey findings support the hypothesis (H9): Increasing technology readiness
increases Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services. Technology
readiness refers to the level of development and maturity of technologies, humans and the
environment in which they perform tasks. This research measured this factor using three
items mentioned in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.9. The findings regarding technology
readiness (TR) show that it positively affects Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud
computing services (ITAC). The correlation coefficient between TR and ITAC was (β =
.295, p < 0.041), indicating that technology readiness positively affects cloud adoption.
This result is consistent with the outcomes found by Hassan, Nasir, et al. (2017), which
confirms that this element has a noteworthy impact on Saudi SMEs' decisions to move
their operations to the Cloud. The findings indicate that Saudi SMEs' technology
readiness is not at the required level and that improvements are needed to achieve the
level of readiness that will help push them toward the adoption of cloud computing
services.

The interviews with IT managers and top managers, revealed agreement with the survey
results mentioned above. Three of the interviewees (P4, P8 and P13) all mentioned that
their organisations (which operated in the health, IT and retail sectors) relied daily on the
presently free technical services such as Gmail Form, Chat and Google Drive. As a result
of this, SMEs did not pay sufficient attention to improving technical infrastructure or
giving staff technical training and focused instead on improving other aspects of their
businesses. The upshot was that they did not now find themselves in a position to easily
adopt cloud computing services.

Both the qualitative (survey) findings and the qualitative (semi-structured interview)
findings demonstrate that Saudi SMEs need to enhance their technology readiness level.
Saudi SMEs need to consider humans, technology and environment as the three
components of technology readiness to be addressed in order to ease transition to cloud
computing services.

7.1.10 Top Management Support
The survey findings support the hypothesis (H10): Increasing the top management
support of cloud computing increases the Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing


                                            216
services. Top management support refers to the level of support the organisation receives
from the top management regarding the migration process from traditional systems to
cloud adoption. This research measured this factor using three items mentioned in Chapter
5. Section 5.11.1.10. This study found that the top management support (TM) factor
significantly correlates with Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services
(ITAC). There is a significant correlation between TM and ITAC (β = .066, p < 0.016).
This result is consistent with the outcomes of Tarhini et al. (2017), who investigated how
top management affects the adoption of cloud computing services and discovered that
they relate positively to each other. The results for the current study revealed that in Saudi
SMEs the current top management support needs to be increased to reach the level
required for effective adoption of cloud services.

The findings for the interviews agreed with those of the survey. One of the interviewees
reflected that top management support could be negatively affected by constant changes
of management. This top manager in the estates and utilities sector believed that, in order
to make appropriate decisions about adopting innovative technologies such as the Cloud,
a manager needed to have managed the same organisation for a considerable time in order
to fully understand its needs and make the appropriate decisions.

Both the findings from the quantitative data in Chapter 5 and the qualitative data in
Chapter 6 show that Saudi SMEs need to pay more attention to the top management
support factor within their organisations and consider that increasing top management
support facilitates the adoption of cloud computing services.

7.1.11 Technology Infrastructure
The survey findings support the hypothesis (H11): Obtaining a high level of technology
infrastructure increases Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services.
Technology infrastructure refers to the base of IT and its resources within the
organisation, including software and hardware. This research measured this factor using
three items mentioned in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.11. Therefore, technology
infrastructure is a vital factor that plays a big role in Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud
computing services. This study has assessed how the solid technology infrastructure (TI)
helps organisations migrate from traditional technology to the Cloud and raise their
intention to adopt cloud services (ITAC). It found that there is a substantial correlation



                                             217
between TI and ITAC with value (β = .115, p < 0.018). These results were consistent with
those of a previous study by Cardoso et al. (2018), which confirmed the technology
infrastructure substantially impacts the Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing
services as the critical ratio demonstrated a significant connection between them. The
results of the current study emphasise that the technology infrastructure in Saudi SMEs
is not sufficiently solid yet, and needs a critical plan to enhance it.

The findings from the interviews with the IT managers and top managers agreed with the
quantitative survey findings. One of the IT managers interviewed (P13) argued that SMEs
generally required to continuously back up their data, which was not always easy given
that many had internet provision with restricted bandwidth and speed. This could have a
significant negative impact on their business operation and policy-makers and internet
service providers needed to address this issue, which once resolved, would facilitate the
adoption of cloud computing services.

Findings from the interviews and the survey both emphasised that Saudi SMEs need to
examine their technology infrastructure and make a plan to strengthen it, as obtaining a
high level of technology infrastructure is of fundamental importance in facilitating the
adoption of cloud computing services.

7.1.12 Cloud Provider
The survey findings support the hypothesis (H12): Increasing the number of cloud
providers within Saudi Arabia increases Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing
services. A cloud provider is a third party that provides the servers to host the SMEs'
systems and store their data. This research measured this factor using three items
mentioned in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.12. Therefore, cloud providers are crucial in Saudi
SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing services. The result found a positive
relationship exists between the Cloud Provider (CP) and the Saudi SMEs' intention to
adopt cloud services (ITAC), as indicated by the obtained critical ratio (β = .131, p <
0.015). Al-Ruithe et al. (2018) confirmed that organisations in Saudi Arabia trust the
cloud provider based in Saudi Arabia, which increases their confidence and results in
adopting the Cloud. The results of the survey also indicate that cloud providers within
Saudi Arabia have not reached the quality of the global market. They need to improve
their services and care more about innovation.



                                             218
The interviews findings support the survey findings discussed above. One of the IT
managers (P6) who was employed in a manufacturing SME noted that there was
competition between service providers in Saudi Arabia. However, this competition was
restricted to the few local companies that had their servers located inside Saudi borders,
as required by the Saudi government. He believed that SMEs in the private sector would
prefer a wider selection of service providers to choose from and that the competition
would generate better services.

Both survey and interview findings highlighted that Saudi SMEs believe that allowing
the global cloud providers to enter the Saudi market would increase their intention to
adopt cloud computing services.

7.1.13 Legal Framework
The quantitative data findings support the hypothesis (H13): Obtaining an organised
legal framework of cloud computing increases Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud
computing services. The legal framework refers to the government rules and instructions
that govern the business market of SMEs within the Saudi borders. This research
measured this factor using three items mentioned in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.13. This
study found a positive link between the Legal framework (LF) and the Saudi SMEs'
intention to adopt cloud services (ITAC). It observed a noteworthy statistical correlation
between LF and ITAC with a value of (β = .154, p < 0.023). Alsafi and Fan (2020) also
found that when the legal framework and strategies within organisations are regularly
revised, it results in good quality performance and encourages adopting cloud services.
Nevertheless, the results for the current study also indicate that the legal framework for
Saudi SMEs is not yet of sufficiently high quality and needs some enhancements.
However, the Saudi Vision 2030 is making everyone optimistic year after year with the
positive changes it has made.

The interviews findings supported these aforementioned findings. As one interviewee
(P15) explained. There was a legal framework for cloud computing in Saudi Arabia,
which had been established in 2018. Although the framework had been updated in 2020,
further amendments were still required. This top manager worked in the IT sector and he
believed that the coronavirus pandemic had shifted expectations and SMEs were now




                                           219
required to classify data, transfer it digitally to customers and protect it, which needed
clear guidelines.

The findings from analysing both the quantitative and the qualitative data show that Saudi
SMEs are eager for government authorities' updates and follow-ups in the legal
framework to encourage the adoption of cloud computing services.

7.1.14 Enterprise Competitiveness
The quantitative survey findings do not support the hypothesis (H14): Increasing
enterprise competitiveness increases Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud computing
services. Enterprise competitiveness refers to the level of competition between enterprises
in the Saudi market that can affect SMEs. This research measured this factor using three
items outlined in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.14. The findings of this study regarding
Enterprise Competition (EC) showed no significant effect on the Saudi SMEs' intention
to adopt cloud services (ITAC), as the statistical analysis disclosed no influence or
relationship between EC and ITAC (β = .020, p < 0.768). This result contradicts the
findings of Low et al. (2011b), who found that competitive pressure is an influential factor
in adopting the Cloud. Results from the current study indicated that although competition
among SMEs exists, it does not influence the intention to adopt cloud services.

The qualitative data findings from the interviews contradicted the above conclusions,
which could be due to the interviewees having different perceptions to those of the survey
participants. This difference will be justified in the across-methods findings in (see
Chapter 8, Section 8.4). Three of the interviewees (P4, P7 and P13) emphasised that the
lack of technological readiness was a key reason why an organisation might find itself
out of the competition. It was this, rather than their inability to compete in terms of their
performance that could put them out of the running. This was why enterprise
competitiveness needed to be ascertained in terms of technological readiness, which
might include the adoption of cloud computing services. The findings from the primary
method underlined that Saudi SMEs' enterprise competitiveness does not hinder their
intention to adopt cloud computing, resulting in the rejection of the hypothesis (H14).




                                            220
7.1.15 Culture
The survey findings do not support the hypothesis (H15): Increasing the technology
culture for IT managers, top managers, and employees increases the Saudi SMEs'
intention to adopt cloud computing services. Culture here specifically refers to the attitude
and behaviours of employees toward adopting cloud computing services. This research
measured this factor using three items mentioned in Chapter 5, Section 5.11.1.15. This
study showed no relationship or significant correlation between Culture (CL) and the
Saudi SMEs' intention to adopt cloud services (ITAC) as follows (β = .145, p < 0.446).
This differed from the results of Alkhater et al. (2018), who found a negative relationship
and indirect impact of cultural factors on the decision of Saudi SMEs to adopt the Cloud;
and Sabi et al (2018), who found that culture significantly impacts the cloud computing
adoption decision in developing countries. Since both these studies were outdated, this
factor needed a more recent, large-sample study to investigate if anything had changed,
which is precisely what this one did.
